ANNUAL
2020

The National Data Protection Commission (CNPD) is a

independent public institution with legal personality. She

enjoys financial and administrative autonomy.

It is responsible for verifying the legality of files and all collections,
uses and disclosures of information about individuals
identifiable and must ensure in this context respect for the freedoms and rights
fundamental rights of natural persons, in particular their private life.

2

ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPDINTRODUCTION It must in particular check and verify whether the data subject to processing are processed in
compliance with the provisions:
• the General Data Protection Regulation;
• the law of 1 August 2018 on the organization of the National Commission for the Protection of

data and the general data protection regime;

• the law of 1 August 2018 on the protection of natural persons with regard to the processing
personal data in criminal matters as well as in matters of national security;
• the amended law of 30 May 2005 on the protection of privacy in the sector of

electronic communications;

• legal texts providing for specific provisions on the protection of personal data

personal character.

It is not competent to control the processing operations of personal data.
personnel carried out by the courts of the judiciary, including the public prosecutor, and
administrative order in the exercise of their judicial functions. This mission falls to
the judicial data protection supervisory authority.

3

2020 ANNUAL REPORT CNPD MISSIONS
The CNPD aims to protect the privacy of citizens and to ensure respect for the
data protection legislation which entrusts it with the following tasks:

Inform and guide with:
• raising public awareness and understanding of the risks, rules, guarantees and

rights relating to processing;

• raising the awareness of data controllers and processors with regard to the

obligations incumbent on them.

Advise through:
• notices relating to bills and regulatory or administrative measures concerning the

processing of personal data;

• suggestions and recommendations addressed to the government, in particular concerning the
relevant developments, insofar as they have an impact on data protection
of a personal nature, in particular in the field of information technology and
communication and business practices;

• the promotion of good practices and the publication of thematic guidelines;
• approval of codes of conduct, certification schemes and accreditation of organizations

certification;

• recommendations to the controller in accordance with the consultation procedure

prior.

Supervise and ensure transparency by:
• on-site visits relating to data protection following complaints or its

own initiative;

• audits relating to data protection following complaints or on its own initiative;
• response to data breaches;
• maintaining internal records of GDPR violations;
• the establishment and updating of a list in connection with the obligation to carry out an analysis

impact relating to data protection;

• approval of Binding Corporate Rules;
• reviewing certifications and monitoring certifiers;
• the adoption of corrective measures (e.g. warning, prohibition of treatment or fine

administration).

Cooperate through:
• exchanges with other national or foreign supervisory authorities;
• contributing to the activities of the European Data Protection Board.

4

2020 ANNUAL REPORT CNPDINTRODUCTION TO VALUES
The CNPD independently exercises the missions assigned to it. She determines her
own priorities within the limits of its legal framework. It chooses its priorities, in particular on the basis
criteria such as the seriousness and extent of potential violations of the law and the extent of
affected individuals.

Expertise is very important for the CNPD, which is dedicated to quality work. To this end, the
CNPD strives to work with interdisciplinary teams and invests in the development
of its agents to improve their knowledge and skills.

The CNPD ensures transparency with regard to its results and its choices, which generates support
for his work and invites dialogue. The CNPD is open, honest and visible. She promotes a
positive and respectful atmosphere.

The CNPD is proud to work for the protection of a fundamental right. She bears witness to her
commitment to its work and its staff and is a full player in the environment
socio-economic Luxembourg.

CNPD 2020 ANNUAL REPORT The college: Thierry Lallemang, Marc Lemmer, Tine A. Larsen and Christophe Buschmann.

Since March 2020, the daily life of Luxembourgers has been disrupted by the coronavirus pandemic.

coronavirus (COVID-19).

Whether in the private or professional sphere, the pandemic has had an impact on all aspects of our
life. Practices hitherto uncommon such as teleworking, online meetings and conferences or
distance education have been put in place due to confinement.

8

1FOREWORDANNUALREPORT2020 CNPDANNUALREPORT To allow the continuity of their activities while respecting the social distancing measures decided
by the government, the actors of economic and social life, just like the citizens have resorted massively
to digital tools. In addition to social networks and online media already firmly rooted in
habits of the vast majority of citizens before the pandemic, videoconferencing platforms have
generalized in the world of work, at least for the activities which allowed it.

Like most organizations that could do so, the CNPD switched to teleworking as soon as
March 17, 2020. This transition was quick and efficient and took place without major incidents, which allowed
the CNPD to ensure the continuity of its missions without interruption.

The digital transformation got a boost in a few months and the rapid deployment towards solutions
has obviously led to an increase in the processing of personal data.

Across Europe, governments and public and private organizations have taken action to stem
and mitigate the proliferation of COVID-19. Unprecedented data processing has been implemented to respond
punctually to health emergencies such as "contact tracing", temperature measurement
body at the entrance to premises or the "large scale testing" of the population.

The CNPD, together with its European counterparts, played an important role in ensuring that the deployment
of these measures taken is carried out in compliance with data protection rules.

Indeed, even in this extraordinary period, data controllers and subcontractors
must guarantee the protection of the personal data of the persons concerned. This is valid in
particularly for health data which is at the heart of the health crisis. Their use imposes respect
essential principles protecting the privacy and confidentiality of medical information concerning patients
citizens.

In Luxembourg and in Europe, the debate on the issue of data protection and the protection of privacy
in the context of the COVID-19 pandemic was animated, which reflects a great sensitivity within the company
civil society, economics and politics on these subjects.

The Government, aware of the issues, has sought the opinion of the CNPD for successive COVID-19 bills.
The National Commission has, on each occasion, quickly notified these bills by pointing out the need for
put in place safeguards for the protection of citizens' data.

Despite the situation, the project to fit out the new premises has experienced little delay. Thus, the CNPD has
been able to move to the Belval site in June.

9

2020 CNPD2020 CNPD ANNUAL REPORT GDPR - 2 YEARS LATER

While international data protection news in 2020 was dominated by the invalidation
of the “Privacy Shield” (which regulated the transfer of data between the EU and the United States) by the Court of Justice of
the European Union (“Schrems II” affair) as well as by Brexit, another point to be noted is the evaluation report
of the General Data Protection Regulation (GDPR)1 published by the European Commission two years after
entry into force of the regulation.

This report showed that the GDPR has achieved most of its objectives, in particular by giving citizens
a solid set of rights and by creating a new European system of governance and control of its
application. The GDPR has proven to be a flexible tool to support the development of digital solutions in
unforeseen circumstances such as the COVID-19 crisis. The report also found that companies
develop a culture of regulatory compliance and increasingly promote as a benefit
competition the high level of data protection they provide.

Citizens are better equipped and more aware of their rights: the GDPR increases transparency and confers
individuals more rights, such as the rights of access, rectification and erasure, the right to object
and the right to data portability. Today, 69% of the EU population aged over 16
are aware of the existence of the GDPR while 71% have heard of their national authority responsible for
of data protection, according to the results of a survey by the Union Agency for Fundamental Rights
European2.

While the European Commission's assessment is generally positive, it is still too early to draw conclusions.
definitive. However, some challenges are already on the horizon.

In particular, the processing of cross-border files based on the "one-stop shop" mechanism requires a
even more efficient and more harmonized approach as well as an effective use of all the tools provided for in the
GDPR, so that data protection authorities can cooperate efficiently.

With respect to international data transfers and in light of the invalidation of the “Privacy Shield”,
it is necessary to adapt the data transfer mechanisms, including the standard contractual clauses which are the tool
most commonly used data transfer method.

In terms of new technologies such as artificial intelligence, blockchain, connected objects or
facial recognition, the CNPD with the other European authorities within the European Data Protection Board
(EDPB), continued its monitoring and its guidance and support actions.

1FOREWORDANNUAL REPORT2020 CNPDANNUAL REPORT CNPD headquarters in Belval

Thus, the year 2020 was an exceptional year in many respects. With the COVID-19 crisis which
will continue to accompany during the current year and probably beyond, the challenges that this situation will generate in terms of
data protection and privacy will not be less.

Whether with citizens, private companies or public bodies, the CNPD will continue its
objective of promoting a culture of data protection in Luxembourg while ensuring, in this context,
the role of guarantor of compliance with the GDPR. With the allocated resources, the CNPD will be able to use the legal means
that the legislation provides to best guarantee the right balance between the information society and the protection
of privacy.

The Luxembourg supervisory authority will thus continue to implement its 2020-2022 work program
with a year 2021 certainly marked by the first decisions that were taken following investigations
opened in previous years.

 
Luxembourg, August 31, 2021

CNPD 2020 ANNUAL REPORT 1 RAISING PUBLIC AWARENESS

Informing citizens as well as data controllers and subcontractors is a priority for the
CNPD in order to make known the respective rights and duties of each. It conducts awareness-raising actions,
informs the public through its website and organizes or participates in training and conferences.

Due to the pandemic, the CNPD organized and participated in fewer events than in previous years.
Some conferences have been canceled or have been postponed to later dates. Others have taken place but
virtual mode or sometimes in mixed "remote / face-to-face" mode. Some initiatives envisaged just
before the start of the pandemic to transform training courses (carried out so far face-to-face and on
slide base) in virtual modules fully or partially recorded to be broadcast on platforms
learning, will continue in the years to come.

 

28 JANUARY 2020: 14TH INTERNATIONAL DATA PROTECTION DAY

The “International Data Protection Day”, an initiative of the Council of Europe and the Commission
European Union, has been celebrated on January 28 every year since 2006. The aim is to raise awareness among citizens about
the importance of protecting their personal data and respecting their fundamental rights and freedoms,
especially their private lives.

On January 28, 2020, the CNPD took part in the Data Privacy Day organized by the Restena Foundation and the University of
Luxemburg.

Data Privacy Day is an annual event for people interested in data protection and
the protection of privacy. The objective is to raise awareness and promote best practices in this area
at European and international level.

Mr. Christophe Buschmann, Commissioner of the CNPD, gave the opening speech and gave a
presentation entitled “Feedback on the CNPD governance and process for investigation”.

 

“DAPROLAB” WORKSHOPS

The “DaProLab” (Data Protection Laboratory) are workshops developed by the CNPD where a single subject
defined in advance is discussed with a limited number of people.

14

3ANNUAL REPORT2020 CNPDANNUAL REPORT ACTIVITIES IN 2020 Presentation by Christophe Buschmann during Data Privacy Day (January 28, 2020).

15

2020 CNPDANNUAL REPORT2020 CNPD The objective of these workshops is to exchange knowledge, ideas, interpretations and points of view on a subject
predetermined between data protection professionals.

The events of 2018 and 2019 focused in particular on the security of exchanges in the field of health,
analyzes of the impact on data protection or on the processing of data in the sector of
finance/insurance. Due to the pandemic, the editions planned for 2020 have been postponed. Topics
planned for the year 2020 were, among others, the data protection impact assessments (DPIA) in the
context of scientific research and the identification of (co-)processors and processors in
the context of scientific research.

 

TRAINING, CONFERENCES AND SEMINARS

The National Commission has also participated in training, conferences and seminars to raise awareness among
public who are more expert in data protection issues.

As in previous years, the supervisory authority intervened during general training courses on the
data protection with the EST (Higher School of Work), the INAP (National Institute of Administration
public sector) of the CSSF (Financial Sector Supervisory Commission) and the CSL (Chamber of Employees) /
LLC (Luxembourg Life Long Learning Center).

brokerage? organized by APCAL (Professional Association of Insurance Brokers in
Luxemburg);

• “GDPR Evaluation after 2 years” organized by ISACA (International Audit and Consulting Association)

computer science) ;

“Digital Around the World” conference;

• “Updates from the CNPD” during the PwC Cybersecurity Day;

TECHNOLOGICAL AND LEGAL MONITORING

At the start of 2020, the CNPD began to set up a technology watch and legal watch unit and
regulatory. This must enable the CNPD services:

• to have a quick and summary overview of the issue of personal data in the context of

new technologies ; And

• to be able to orient their research in the context of the processing of complaints or investigations and to

the drafting of legal opinions or the preparation of training guidance or recommendations.

The CNPD experts thus opened or updated the following files during 2020:

• Fintech
• COVID-19 tracing Apps
• Telework
• Homeschooling
• Artificial intelligence

 

COLLABORATIONS WITH RESEARCH AND INNOVATION (RDI) PLAYERS

Government policy places significant emphasis on the economic diversification of the country through research
and innovation (RDI). The priorities of public and private RDI actors are largely science-oriented

17

ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPD digital, cybersecurity, artificial intelligence, digital trust and, in general, technologies
innovative digital.

In this context, the CNPD must actively observe the major political orientations, large-scale projects
national actors and to remain open to collaborations deemed useful and necessary for development
products and services that respect the protection of personal data and privacy.

The CNPD was asked to participate in the following projects by being part of the monitoring committee or the
project management:

• National Digital Twin: LIST project (Luxembourg Institute of Science and Technology) and co-financed by the FNR;
• Smart Schoul 2025: FNR project of the Interdisciplinary Center for Security, Reliability and Trust (SnT) of the University
of Luxembourg in collaboration with the SCRIPT of the Ministry of National Education, Childhood and
Youth, and the Lycée Edward Steichen Clervaux (LESC).

In the context of the activities of the "Research Luxembourg COVID-19 Taskforce", the CNPD contributed to the work
evaluation and preparation for the implementation of a national Contact Tracing App solution.

Active participation in these projects allows the CNPD to remind public research actors and to
innovation the important principles of the protection of personal data such as in particular the
"Privacy by design and by default", limitation of retention periods, limitation of the purposes of
processing, the determination of the conditions of lawfulness or the clarification of the links between controllers
and subcontractors.

2 RESPONDING TO REQUESTS FOR INFORMATION FROM INDIVIDUALS
  AND PROFESSIONALS

The CNPD informs and advises individuals and professionals who wish to obtain information
legal information, information on the exercise of their rights or assistance in bringing their
processing to data protection legislation. A dedicated service answers questions from
applicants received by e-mail, telephone or postal mail.

The National Commission received 655 written requests for information in 2020, a decrease compared to
the previous year (-7%). However, we observe a gradual increase since 2011, marked by
the exceptional peak in 2018, the year the GDPR came into force.

In 2020, slightly less than half of the requests came directly from data subjects, in
clear progress compared to 2019. Private companies are still introducing many new
requests, as well as public administrations. The National Commission was approached by a certain
number of public entities, including more and more municipal administrations.

Unsurprisingly, the year 2020 was marked by numerous requests relating to data processing
in connection with the COVID-19 pandemic, in particular with regard to the tracing of persons exposed to a person
positively tested, temperature taking and the use of digital platforms for the realization of
videoconferences. Surveillance in the workplace, above all by means of new IT tools
put in place by the employer, was a subject of concern with the massive use of teleworking throughout
the year 2020.

19

ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPD As in previous years, a large number of requests concerned the exercise of the rights of individuals
concerned, in particular the right of access to personal data and the right to erasure. These
requests demonstrate that citizens make greater use of the rights granted by the GDPR and that the protection of
data continues to be a major concern.

Other recurring questions include:

• the conditions of lawfulness (in particular consent);
• education (especially image rights);
•  geolocation ;
•  human resources ;
• supervision (in the workplace);
• the use of the identity document / registration number (RNPP);
• video surveillance.

3 INTERVENING IN THE LEGISLATIVE PROCESS

The CNPD advises the Chamber of Deputies, the Government and other public institutions and bodies
through notices relating to bills and regulatory or administrative measures concerning the
processing of personal data and presenting a particular risk with regard to the privacy of individuals
concerned.

In 2020, the National Commission issued 24 opinions on draft laws or grand-ducal regulations, i.e. 8
more than in 2019.

All opinions can be consulted on the CNPD website at https://cnpd.public.lu/fr/publications/
reports/index.html.

The following themes were addressed in the CNPD's opinions:

• the fight against COVID-19 (8 opinions);
• the fight against money laundering and the financing of terrorism (3 opinions);
• the processing of personal data in the electronic communications sector;
• the processing of data concerning health in the field of insurance and reinsurance;
• the examination-competition for admission to the Police training course;
• the quality of services for the elderly;
• the lease for residential use;
• procedures for registering establishments of food business operators;
• housing assistance files;
• the recognition of professional qualifications in the field of inland navigation.

21

2020 CNPD ANNUAL REPORT In addition, an opinion on surveillance in the workplace, hereinafter referred to as "work opinion", has been adopted.
in 2020 (procedure of Article L.261-1 paragraph (4) of the Labor Code) on the geolocation of passenger cars
service and construction machinery.

Finally, the CNPD also issued 4 opinions relating to the processing of personal data in
criminal matters and national security. These are presented in part II.9.b) dedicated
specifically to the role of the National Commission relating to the control of data processing carried out by
law enforcement or national security authorities.

Among the opinions issued in 2020, the CNPD would like to highlight the following three opinions. Other reviews may
be consulted on the CNPD website at https://cnpd.public.lu/fr/decisions-avis.html.

 

NOTICES RELATING TO THE FIGHT AGAINST COVID-19

In 2020, the National Commission issued 8 opinions concerning the introduction of a series of measures concerning
natural persons in the context of the fight against COVID-19.

The National Commission has emphasized in its opinions that the protection of personal data is not
consider as an obstacle to the implementation of personal data processing in the context of
the fight against the COVID-19 epidemic, as long as the fundamental principles provided for by the GDPR are respected. She
limited its comments to the provisions of the bill that have an impact on privacy and
the protection of personal data.

In its position papers, the CNPD notably commented:

• the creation of an information system by the Health Department, in order to monitor the evolution of the situation related to

to COVID-19 and the processing of "sensitive" data through this system;

• the establishment of a tracking system to identify as soon as possible any person at risk or with high
risk of becoming infected in order to implement the necessary precautions (such as quarantine) and
thus preventing the spread of the infection by these people who are in turn contagious;

• the retention period of the personal data contained in the information system and

the anonymization/pseudonymization of this data;

• persons authorized in the context of contact tracing to access the data of infected persons

or at high risk of being infected contained in the information system;

• the collection of personal data within the framework of the vaccination programme. She took a stand
as to questions concerning the purpose pursued by the collection of data and the retention period of the
data collected as part of the vaccination program.

22

3ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPDTHE ACTIVITIES IN 2020 The CNPD has also issued an internal request to notify bill no. 7635 which provides that “employees must
immediately report to the employer or designated employees and safety and health representatives any
work situation which they have reasonable cause to believe presents a serious and immediate danger to
safety and health in the context of the COVID-19 epidemic).

The CNPD's own referral was made in the context of numerous requests for information and complaints
introduced to it concerning on the one hand the data, in particular health, that the employees would be authorized
to communicate to the employer in the event of suspicion or contamination with the SARS-CoV-2 virus, and on the other hand the
obligations of the employer with regard to the processing of this data.

 

NOTICE OF THE REGISTER OF TRUSTS AND TRUSTS

The CNPD has issued an opinion on draft law no. 7216B, which aims to transpose article 31 of the directive (EU)
2015/849 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 20 May 2015 relating to the prevention of the use of
financial system for the purposes of money laundering or terrorist financing taking into account the
FATF recommendation 25.

The bill has two parts. First, the bill imposes an obligation on trustees and
trustees to obtain and retain data relating to beneficial owners and other persons
specified in the bill. Secondly, the text aims to establish a register of trusts and trusts kept
by the Administration of registration, domains and VAT in which the fiduciaries and trustees will have to
register certain data that they are obliged to collect under the draft law.

The National Commission has commented on several elements of the draft law. She has in particular
recalled the need to respect the principle of data minimization and therefore recommended to plan with

23

2020 ANNUAL REPORT CNPD clarifies the data to be collected by trustees and fiduciaries as part of their obligations
arising from this bill. In accordance with the principle of data minimization, only the data
needed should be addressed.

Furthermore, with regard to access to the register, the CNPD notably recommended limiting the access of professionals to the
data contained in the register to only necessary data. Regarding access by the general public
on the basis of legitimate interest, the CNPD reiterated the importance of a clear definition of legitimate interest and considered
necessary to define the criteria on the basis of which this legitimate interest would be analysed. She also formulated
comments on the personal data to be provided with the request to access the register.

The National Commission has also issued recommendations relating to the processing of the authorities
authorities and self-regulatory bodies, in particular with regard to compliance with the principle of limitation
purposes and the transfer of data to third countries.

 
 

PREVENTING THE USE OF THE FINANCIAL SYSTEM FOR MONEY LAUNDERING
OR TERRORIST FINANCING

The CNPD has issued its opinion on draft law no. 7467, which aims to transpose certain provisions of the directive
2018/843 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 30 May 2018 amending Directive (EU) 2015/849 relating
preventing the use of the financial system for the purposes of money laundering or the financing of
terrorism and to implement FATF recommendations, in particular in the event that these recommendations
are not subject to equivalent provisions in Directive (EU) 2015/849. She made comments
on several elements of the bill.

In its opinion, the CNPD first recalled the importance of respecting the legislation on the protection of
privacy and personal data when transposing Directive 2018/843 and implementing
implementation of FATF recommendations.

It also made remarks relating to the duration of data retention by professionals as well as
as well as the rights of data subjects, raising the need to provide a precise and proportional legal framework
for the desired purpose. With regard to the rights of data subjects, the CNPD recommended specifying
the cases in which the right of access of data subjects could be limited and to provide for a right of access
indirect in case of limitation of this right.

The National Commission finally made suggestions as to the processing of the FIU, the national authorities
and self-regulatory bodies, in particular with regard to compliance with the principle of purpose limitation
and the transfer of data to third countries.

3ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPDACTIVITIES IN 2020 4 GUIDE PRIVATE AND PUBLIC ACTORS

Actors in the private and public sectors must adopt preventive and responsible conduct with regard to
personal data they collect. Guiding and directing them in this process is one of the roles
of the supervisory authority. This role is assumed by the CNPD by developing recommendations or guidance
on themes for a very specific target audience.

 

COLLECTION OF PERSONAL DATA IN THE CONTEXT OF CORONAVIRUS (COVID-19)

Personal data, particularly health data, are at the heart of the issues linked to the crisis
health of COVID-19. From the start of the pandemic, the CNPD supported private and public actors
Luxembourgish.

They faced complex challenges in their day-to-day operations. New challenges have
emerged as the confinement and then the deconfinement progressed, in particular due to the return of employees
at their place of work as well as during successive stages depending on the health measures taken by the
government.

Professionals and individuals have wondered both about the measures implemented to limit the
spread of the virus and ensure the resumption of activity in complete safety, only on the conditions in which the
personal data, in particular health data, may be used.

The CNPD has published recommendations to guide professionals in the pursuit of their activities and to
respond to people's questions about their rights.

25

2020 CNPD ANNUAL REPORT In its guidance, the CNPD notably recalled a few rules concerning:

• employers' duty of safety;
• the employee/agent security obligation;
• data processing by employers:

  - temperature readings at the entrance to the premises;
  - carrying out tests by the employer and questionnaires on the state of health;

• requests and recommendations from health authorities.

The recommendations have been updated on the CNPD3 website on several occasions, taking into account
of the evolution of the pandemic.

 
 

GUIDELINES ON THE CONSEQUENCES OF BREXIT ON TRANSFERS
INTERNATIONAL DATA

 

Following the agreement between the United Kingdom and the European Commission, published on December 25, 2020, the CNPD has
updated its guidelines regarding the consequences of Brexit for international transfers
of data. These guidelines are intended to guide companies, public bodies and associations
Luxembourg companies who are required to transfer personal data to the United Kingdom, and who
intend to continue such transfers in 2021.

The National Commission has also updated its thematic dossier devoted to international transfers of
personal data in relation to the General Data Protection Regulation.

The following scenarios are dealt with in the thematic dossier:

• Transfers within the European Economic Area (European Union, Liechtenstein, Norway and Iceland);
• Transfers to a country outside the European Economic Area with an adequate level of protection;
• Transfers to a country outside the European Economic Area that does not have a level of protection

adequate ;

• International cooperation in police and judicial matters;
• The consequences of Brexit in terms of international data transfers.

As a reminder, the appointment of a DPO is mandatory in three situations:
• the processing is carried out by a public authority or a public body, with the exception of courts acting

in the exercise of their judicial function;

• the core activities of the controller or processor consist of processing operations
which, due to their nature, their scope and/or their purposes, require regular and systematic monitoring at
large scale of the persons concerned;

• the core activities of the controller or processor consist of large-scale processing
scale of special categories of data referred to in Article 9 or of personal data relating
criminal convictions and offenses referred to in Article 10.

Unless it is clear that an organization is not required to appoint a DPO, the CNPD always recommends
document the internal analysis carried out to determine whether or not a DPO should be appointed.

2020 CNPD ANNUAL REPORT 5 SUPPORTING COMPLIANCE

The General Data Protection Regulation has introduced several voluntary tools to
enable organizations to manage their compliance and demonstrate that they are complying with the legislation: analyzes
impact on data protection, certifications, codes of conduct or transfer framework
international data.

 

CERTIFICATION

The CNPD continued its work started in 2018 to set up and manage activities related to the
certification as proposed in the GDPR. These instruments enable data controllers and data processors to
processors to demonstrate that their processing operations comply with the Regulation.

The establishment of certification mechanisms can promote transparency and compliance with the GDPR and allow
data subjects to assess the level of data protection offered by the products, services, processes
or systems of private and public actors.

The framework for the practical application of these new tools is being developed in consultation with the other regulatory authorities.
control at the EDPB level to ensure consistency but also, at the national level, in collaboration
with interested private sector actors.

The many exchanges between the CNPD and companies during the GDPR preparation phase have demonstrated
that they have a particular interest for certification according to the regulation.

Convinced of the added value that certification can offer, the CNPD has taken a particularly
proactively by developing, together with auditing companies, a certification framework based on the
international conformity assessment ISAE (International Standard on Assurance Engagements).

Thus, the CNPD proposed the plan called "GDPR-CARPA" which was submitted to an initial consultation
public in June 2018. After integrating until the end of 2020 the feedback received at national and European level, in
particular via exchanges with the other European supervisory authorities within the EDPB, a consultation
final was completed in early 2021.

The CNPD has oriented its work according to two pillars:

• The first pillar concerns the certification criteria that must be met by an organization that wants

some of its data processing is certified;

28

3ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPDTHE ACTIVITIES IN 2020 • The second pillar concerns the accreditation criteria that must be met by an organization wishing to act

as a certification body.

In this context, she contributed as lead-rapporteur to the European Data Protection Board to:

• the establishment of procedures for the adoption of accreditation criteria for certification bodies;
• the adoption of certification criteria and
• the adoption of a European label for certification.

It should be noted that the CNPD is the first data protection authority in Europe to submit its criteria
to the EDPB for advice and, once these criteria are adopted, Luxembourg will be the first country to allow the
certification according to GDPR criteria.

Approval of accreditation criteria for certification bodies

On April 3, 2020, the CNPD adopted Decision No. 8/2020 approving the licensing criteria for
certification bodies.

These accreditation criteria apply to certification bodies whose activities relate to mechanisms
and certification criteria that the CNPD has designated by the “Alpha set” label as defined in the attached document
to the decision.

The purpose of the accreditation is to certify with the necessary authority the skills of the certification bodies, this
which builds trust in the certification mechanism.

29

ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPD

CODES OF CONDUCT

Codes of conduct are voluntary accountability tools that define specific rules in terms of
data protection for certain categories of controllers and processors.

They can be a useful and effective accountability tool by providing a detailed description of
all the most appropriate and ethical behaviors and incorporating best practices in a
given sector.

Professional associations or bodies representing a given sector may develop codes to
help companies in this sector to comply with the GDPR in an effective way.

In 2020, the CNPD was in contact with the federation of a professional sector which has initiated an approach to
establishment of a code of conduct. However, the CNPD observes that, unlike neighboring countries,
actors in Luxembourg have not yet really demonstrated their interest in codes of conduct. However, she
encourages them to do so and plans to promote the use of this tool in the near future.

 

DATA PROTECTION IMPACT ASSESSMENT (DPIA)

DPIA - what is it?

A AIPD (Data Protection Impact Analysis, or DPIA)
Assessment”) is a tool that helps identify and minimize the data protection risks of new
projects involving personal data processing processes. It is part of the obligation
provided by the GDPR for data controllers and strongly contributes to the accountability approach
advocated by the GDPR, in particular via the concepts of data protection by design and data protection.
default data (“Privacy by design”, “Privacy by default”).

A DPIA helps data controllers to identify and deal with problems at an early stage, to demonstrate
their compliance with data protection obligations and to meet the expectations of individuals
in terms of privacy.

If an organization has identified processing of personal data likely to generate high risks
for the rights and freedoms of the persons concerned, it must carry out a DPIA for each of these processing operations.

The CNPD has drawn up a list of types of processing operations for which it considers that a DPIA is
obligatory in all cases4.

4 The list can be consulted on the CNPD website: https://cnpd.public.lu/fr/professionnels/obligations/AIPD.html

as of 31.12.2020.

of the regulation in 2018 and it is no longer adapted to the evolution of the matter.

new tools.

If, following this risk analysis, it results that the level of residual risk remains high, the organization must
You must consult the CNPD, which will give an opinion on the planned processing and the risks associated with it.

In this case, the processing cannot be implemented before receipt of the opinion of the CNPD, and, if necessary,
the implementation of additional measures.

In 2020, two requests for prior opinions were submitted to the CNPD. This figure is very low in comparison
with those of neighboring countries indicates that the use of impact assessments does not yet seem to be very
widespread among data controllers established in Luxembourg.

ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPD

INTERNATIONAL TRANSFERS OF PERSONAL DATA

Personal data can circulate freely from Luxembourg within the Space
European Economic Area (EEA), as long as the general principles of the GDPR are respected. Transfers of
data outside the EU and the EEA are possible, provided that a level of data protection is ensured
sufficient and appropriate. These transfers must be regulated using different legal tools (clauses
contractual agreements, Binding Corporate Rules (BCR), codes of conduct, certification mechanisms or
specific safeguards for transfer between public authorities or bodies).

32

3ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPDACTIVITIES IN 2020 Invalidation of the “Privacy Shield”

On July 16, 2020, the Court of Justice of the European Union (CJEU) invalidated decision 2016/1250
on the adequacy of the protection provided by the EU-US Privacy Shield (“Privacy
Shield”). In its judgment in Data Protection Commissioner v Facebook Ireland and Maximilian Schrems
(Schrems II), the Court found that the “Privacy Shield” did not provide a level of protection essentially
equivalent to that guaranteed by the GDPR and the EU Charter of Fundamental Rights.

Therefore, companies can no longer legitimize their transfers to the United States of America based on
of the EU-US "Privacy Shield" Framework, but will have to carry out the same analysis as for any other country outside
of the European Economic Area that does not have an adequate level of protection.

Binding corporate rules

Binding Corporate Rules (BCR) help ensure
a sufficient level of protection for data transferred within a group of companies both within and outside
outside the European Economic Area. This appropriate guarantee lends itself especially to corporate groups
multinationals implementing a large number of international data transfers.

The BCRs constitute a “charter for the protection of personal data” drawn up by a group
which defines its policy on the transfer of personal data. This charter should
be binding and respected by all group entities, regardless of their country of establishment, as well as
than by all their employees. In addition, it must confer on the persons concerned (customers, suppliers
and/or employees) enforceable rights with regard to the processing of their personal data
staff.

In 2020, the CNPD handled files concerning binding corporate rules (BCRs, article 47 of the
GDPR) including 7 as principal authority (i.e. for multinational companies whose establishment
authority in the European Union is located in Luxembourg), 3 as a secondary authority (“co-reviewer”)
(i.e. in support of the main supervisory authority) and 15 as the authority concerned.

33

CNPD 2020 ANNUAL REPORT 6 PROCESSING CITIZENS' COMPLAINTS

If a direct complaint by a data subject to a controller has remained unanswered
afterwards, he can contact the CNPD. Handling complaints from data subjects
is one of its missions. The CNPD in principle informs the data controller of the facts raised in
the complaint and, in the event of breaches noted, asks it to comply and to respect
the rights of data subjects.

34

3ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPDTHE ACTIVITIES IN 2020

ADOPTION OF THE COMPLAINTS PROCEDURE

On October 16, 2020, the CNPD adopted its complaints procedure5.

The CNPD thus first examines whether a complaint is justified, i.e. it verifies whether the alleged facts
by the claimant relating to the processing of personal data may or may not constitute
a breach of applicable data protection law.
 
When the CNPD considers that the disputed data processing is indeed contrary to the law, it
will endeavor to remedy this, in a first step, without having to trigger a formal investigation procedure
in accordance with articles 37 to 41 of the law of 1 August 2018 on the organization of the National Commission
for data protection and the general data protection regime.

It thus has discretionary power to assess whether it investigates a complaint or not. Depending of
specific to each complaint, the CNPD may decide to investigate or not to investigate a complaint.
complaint, taking into account in particular the degree of seriousness of the alleged facts, the alleged violation, the degree
impact on fundamental rights and freedoms, the degree of priority in relation to the number of complaints and the
resources available.

 

NATIONAL AND EUROPEAN COMPLAINTS

In 2020, the CNPD received a total of 485 complaints:

• 324 people called directly on the services of the CNPD when they felt that there had been a

violation of the law or interference with the exercise of their rights;

• In addition to complaints handled at national level, through the European cooperation system, 161
complaints for which the CNPD was the presumed lead authority because the controller
with activities at European level, had established its headquarters in Luxembourg. These complaints are made
for the attention of the CNPD by the supervisory authority or authorities of the countries in which the person(s)
concerned lodged their complaint via the IMI system.

Compared to the previous year, this is a decrease of 140 claims. However, their number
remains very high since the entry into application of the GDPR with an average of 520 complaints per year since
2018.

Requests to erase or rectify data constitute 26% of complaints received in 2020.
These included, among other things, requests to close accounts with online services/commerce or

3ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPDACTIVITIES IN 2020 THE INTERNAL MARKET INFORMATION SYSTEM (IMI)

The IMI system is the European Union's IT platform which guarantees the proper implementation of
implementation of cooperation between supervisory authorities for a wide range of regulations.
This tool has also been put at the service of the GDPR since May 25, 2018 as provided for by the GDPR.
The supervisory authorities of the Member States cooperate closely to ensure protection
data protection rights across the EU.

Now more than ever, mutual assistance and coordinated decision-making in
cross-border data protection cases are of utmost importance.

In addition, the European Data Protection Board issues opinions and takes decisions
binding when national data protection authorities have positions
different in a cross-border case.

Using the IMI system, the control authorities can in particular:

• determine which is the lead supervisory authority in a cross-border dispute;
• cooperate to reach a settlement of cross-border disputes;
• request and provide assistance to the supervisory authorities of other Member States;
• organize joint operations involving the supervisory authorities of several States

members ;

• consult the European Data Protection Board for an opinion or a decision

binding.

requests to erase personal data (email addresses, ratings, etc.) accessible on sites
Internet (such as directories for example).

of the treatment. In some cases, the latter have refused the persons concerned access to their data,
ignored their requests or did not give them enough information in relation to the legal obligations to
respect in terms of the right to information and access.

11% of complaints related to the right to object, particularly in the area of prospecting. The CNPD
occurred when prospecting unsubscribe links in emails were not functional or
when the data controller does not respond to requests for opposition.

In 11% of cases, complainants asked the CNPD to verify the legality of certain practices
administrative or commercial. They questioned in particular:

comply with the requirements of Articles 13 and 14 of the GDPR, implementation of additional security measures,
etc.) without having had to initiate a formal investigation procedure in accordance with Articles 37 to 41 of the law of the 1st

3ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPDACTIVITIES IN 2020 7 ANALYZE DATA BREACHES

Two types of data breaches must be notified to the CNPD:

• data breaches under the General Data Protection Regulation; And
• data breaches in the electronic communications sector.

 

GDPR DATA BREACHES

The GDPR requires that a breach of security resulting, accidentally or unlawfully, in the destruction, loss,
the unauthorized alteration or disclosure of personal data must be managed in accordance with the
requirements of Articles 33 and 34 of the GDPR.

Data controllers must notify personal data breaches to the CNPD within
a period of 72 hours after becoming aware of it if the violation in question is likely to cause a
risk to the rights and freedoms of data subjects.

The CNPD would like to point out that the following statistics are only based on data breaches at
personal nature which have been notified to the CNPD. They do not reflect the complete number of security incidents
in relation to personal data that occurs in organizations. The CNPD does not know
not the total number of incidents which is difficult to estimate. However, data controllers are required
maintain documentation of all security incidents involving personal data.

In 2020, 379 data breaches were notified to the CNPD, 25 more than in 2019.

39

CNPD 2020 ANNUAL REPORT CAUSES OF VIOLATIONS

Internal non-malicious act (64%)
Malicious internal act (6%)
External non-malicious act (4%)
Malevolent external act (24%)
Others (2%)

In total, since the entry into force of the GDPR on May 25, 2018, the National Commission has received 905 violations
of data. This corresponds to an average of 29 data breach notifications per month.

The main cause of personal data breach remains human error (internal act not
malicious) in 64% of cases.

Most human errors occur:
• when an existing procedure is not followed;
• when an existing security rule is circumvented: this type of case has been the subject of incidents with consequences

important;

• when staff are not sufficiently aware of the rules of confidentiality to be applied;
• following a careless error: depending on the context, the implementation of a control mechanism before

transmission of data (e.g. 4-eye principle) would probably have avoided this type of incident.

40

3ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPDTHE ACTIVITIES IN 2020 Malicious external acts are the cause of almost a quarter of the breaches notified. This guy
often has a greater impact on the people involved. In many cases, these
acts target the access or obtaining of data that allows financial transactions to be carried out without the knowledge
of the persons concerned (e.g. interception of bank payment card data, phishing to obtain
connection information to a payment service, identity theft to carry out a transaction
financial, etc).

Malicious internal acts occurred mainly during the departure, voluntary or not, of employees
of an organization and in particular the members of the management in medium or small structures: this
situation causes people to copy data for potential use in their new situation.
Similarly, situations of cessation of activity / merger / takeover of companies are periods of risk for companies.
unauthorized exfiltration of data. These acts are often the consequence of a lack of knowledge of the framework
legal or technical and frequently give rise to complaints to the public prosecutor's office.

The other scenarios are related to technical bugs which often result in the disclosure of data to
personal nature to unauthorized third parties (e.g. setting up or updating a new online service, case
unplanned use of a service, etc.).

Type of impact

Almost all data breaches have an impact related to loss of data privacy
concerned. We note the appearance of mixed cases concerning "ransomeware" type attacks that combine
a loss of availability but also more and more regularly a loss of confidentiality following the exfiltration
partial or total data. Data controllers are generally in difficulty when it comes to the analysis of
this type of incident, mainly because of the insufficient or unsuitable level of detail of the available logs.

Structural analysis

The health sector, due to the sensitivity and volume of information it processes, encounters a significant number
security incidents involving personal data. COVID-19 and computer processing
relative have further accentuated this state. However, we note the efforts of this sector and more particularly of the
data protection officers, in order to deal with and prevent these incidents by raising awareness among staff or
further harmonization work on risk analysis methodologies.

In the field of banking and insurance, data controllers seem to have analytical grids
risk very different from one manager to another, thus leading some of them, despite being of significant size,
little or no notification.

Small and medium-sized businesses sometimes have difficulty understanding the notification management process

More than half of security incidents are detected no later than 5 days after their occurrence. Nevertheless,

minimum one month after having occurred: these are more particularly incidents related to continuous violations
the organisation's security policy (e.g. management personnel send professional data
on their personal email to work from home and the personal email is hacked), data used by
employees when leaving an organization and also data theft linked to undetected hacks.

The CNPD also draws the attention of data controllers to the fact that a certain number of acts of
hacking and phishing are targeted at the management staff of an organization and their entourage (ex:
management assistant/secretary). These malicious acts are often aimed at obtaining information
enabling fraudulent financial transactions.

2020 CNPD ANNUAL REPORT DATA BREACHES IN THE ELECTRONIC COMMUNICATIONS SECTOR

In accordance with Regulation (EU) n°611/2013 of the European Commission of June 24, 2013, suppliers
electronic communications services available to the public, such as fixed telephone companies/
mobile phone or Internet access providers, must notify the CNPD within 24 hours of the observation
of a breach of security and confidentiality of personal data and, in addition, inform their
subscribers in the event that the incident observed is likely to adversely affect the level of protection of their
privacy and data concerning them.

In order to facilitate the task of providers of electronic communications services, the National Commission
provides a security breach notification form available on its website. This form
lists all the relevant questions that suppliers will have to answer in such a situation.

In 2020, no data breaches in the electronic communications sector were reported to
the CNPD.

3ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPDACTIVITIES IN 2020 RETENTION OF TRAFFIC AND LOCATION DATA

As a reminder, European Directive 2006/24/EC on data retention had been transposed
at the national level by the law of July 24, 2010 amending the law of May 30, 2005 on the protection of
privacy in the electronic communications sector. The purpose of this guideline was to
keep for a certain period of time the data processed by the telecommunications operators and
internet service providers for research, detection and prosecution purposes
offences. One of the major challenges of this directive was to maintain the balance between, on the one hand,
part, access to data processed by electronic communications providers in the
framework of the fight against terrorism and serious crime, and, on the other hand, the protection of life
deprived of citizens.

However, the directive was annulled by the Court of Justice of the European Union on April 8, 2014
by the “Digital Rights Ireland” judgment. However, the national transposition laws have not been
modified accordingly and the National Commission has not received any instructions in this context from
his supervising ministry. She continues to transmit to him annually with a view to their continuation at the
European Commission for Statistics on Data Retention under Articles 5 and
9. To this end, the service providers or operators retain and continue to the Commission
at the request of the latter, information including in particular:

• the cases in which information has been transmitted to the competent authorities in accordance with

to the applicable national legislation,

• the lapse of time between the date from which the data was stored and the date

to which the competent authorities have requested their transmission,

• cases in which data requests could not be fulfilled.

In 2020, the competent authorities made 2,990 requests to operators. This digit
increased slightly (+13%) compared to 2019 when 2,601 requests were
been made.

45

2020 CNPD ANNUAL REPORT 8 MONITORING AND ADOPTING CORRECTIVE MEASURES

To ensure compliance with applicable data protection legislation, the Commission
national authority has investigative powers to verify the application of the rules on the protection of
data. In the event of processing contrary to the regulations, the CNPD may adopt corrective measures
(e.g. warning, call to order, temporary or definitive limitation or prohibition of processing, imposition
an administrative fine, etc.).

46

3ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPDACTIVITIES IN 2020 The CNPD can launch investigations following complaints (reactive approach) or on its own initiative
(proactive approach). These investigations can be carried out in the form of on-the-spot checks or in the form
of audits. When the CNPD wishes to verify compliance with a specific GDPR theme, the
investigations can take the form of on-the-spot checks or sample audits
organizations selected on the basis of risk criteria, such as sector of activity or size
of the organization, the number of data subjects or the type of data processed.

The power of investigation allows the CNPD to obtain from the controller or processor access to:

• all personal data that is processed and, in general, all information

necessary for the exercise of its mission;

• all premises, in particular any installation and any processing means.

The regulation relating to the investigation procedure adopted by the CNPD on January 22, 2020, at the same time as its
rules of procedure, specifies the conduct of such an investigation (see box).

While taking into account the adversarial principle, this regulation defines the different stages of the process
an investigation of the opening decision by the College of Commissioners sitting in plenary formation, in
going through its instruction until the communication of any grievances raised by the head of investigation.
 
The decision following an investigation is finally taken by the College of Commissioners sitting in formation
(the "Restricted Training") on the basis of the proposals of the head of investigation formulated in the communication
grievances.

In the first half of 2020, the CNPD transposed the provisions of the regulation relating to the procedure
of investigation to all the investigations in progress since 2018 and a significant number of proposed decisions
was transferred to the Restricted Formation for decision-making.

It should be noted that in comparison with previous years, the number of investigations (checks and audits) was low.
high due to pandemic restrictions.

 

CONTROLS

The CNPD carries out proactive on-site inspections as part of thematic campaigns (video surveillance
and COVID-19 – see below) or reactive controls following incidents notified, complaints received,
information relayed in the media or following a previous check.

47

CNPD 2020 ANNUAL REPORT These controls are carried out through on-site visits not announced in advance.

The CNPD carried out 8 on-site visits in 2020. These investigations related to the field of video surveillance and
the regularity of treatments in connection with the fight against COVID-19.

At the end of 2020, the investigation files of the control campaign concerning video surveillance and geolocation
started in 2018 were passed on to the Restricted Panel, which adopted its first decisions in early 2021.

In addition, the CNPD launched, at the beginning of December 2020, a control campaign to verify compliance
with respect to the GDPR of the collection and use of personal data put in place by the
organizations as part of the measures they have taken to combat the spread of the pandemic
COVID-19. This campaign is part of the CNPD's ongoing monitoring of specific features and risks
in terms of data protection induced by the COVID-19 pandemic. Site visits for this campaign
started in December 2020 and continued throughout 2021 on a sample of 20 organizations
public and private of all sizes and from various sectors of activity.

AUDITS

 
 
With the entry into force of the GDPR, the CNPD has also set up surveys according to the methodology
audit, particularly well suited when it comes to verifying compliance with data protection legislation.
personal data in a more complex organizational or technical context.

As with on-the-spot checks, audits can be organized either reactively in the context of a
investigation decided following a complaint received, either proactively in the form of a thematic campaign
relating to selected obligations of the regulation. Such audit campaigns carried out with organizations
selected on the basis of sampling criteria specific to the chosen theme, allow the CNPD
to assess the level of compliance of organizations with data protection.

Given the impact of the new role of the data protection officer and the importance of its integration into
the company, the CNPD had decided to launch a first thematic audit in September 2018 on the function
data protection officer (DPO). The objective of this campaign was to verify the compliance of
organizations with the obligations of the GDPR in terms of appointing the DPO, his missions and his functions.

In 2020, 21 files (out of 25 in total) were transferred to the Restricted Panel which will make its decisions at the end
of each of these surveys. Many of these decisions will be made during 2021. In addition
individual decisions published anonymously, it is planned to publish the results of this campaign
in the form of guidelines or recommendations in order to enable data controllers or data processors to
contractors to benefit from the good and avoid the bad practices identified during the audits.

48

3ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPDTHE ACTIVITIES IN 2020 An audit on the theme of transparency was launched in 2020 and 6 surveys of companies in the
e-commerce have been opened in this context.

Transparency is not a new concept but a well-established principle and obligation in terms of
Protection of personal data. Its primary objective is to build trust between managers
processing and data subjects. However, the notion of transparency takes on an even more
strong with the GDPR and the new principle of accountability, to which it is intrinsically linked.
The principle of transparency allows data subjects to control their personal data and
to hold data controllers to account in this regard.

The e-commerce sector was targeted in the choice of companies to be audited. Indeed, by the very specificities
industry, transparency is more important. Another motivation that guided the CNPD to choose
this theme was the fact that the e-commerce sector experienced exceptionally strong growth during the
pandemic and during confinement.

 

CORRECTIVE MEASURES

As part of its missions, the CNPD has, among other things, the power to adopt corrective measures
provided for in Article 58.2 of the GDPR (e.g. warning, call to order, compliance, temporary limitation,
definitive, or prohibition of processing, etc.), including the power to impose administrative fines in
application of Article 83 of the GDPR in addition to or instead of other corrective measures depending on the
characteristics specific to each case.

Violations by the controller of the GDPR may be subject to administrative fines which may
amount to up to twenty million euros or up to 4% of the total worldwide annual turnover of the previous financial year,
the highest amount being retained. The GDPR nevertheless requires that sanctions be “effective, proportionate
and dissuasive" - this implying that in each specific case, the elements listed in Article 83.2 of the GDPR
taken into account, such as the nature, gravity and duration of the violation, the number of people
data subjects affected and the level of damage they have suffered, the categories of personal data
affected by the breach or whether the breach was willful or negligent.

A corrective measure can only be pronounced by the National Commission sitting in restricted formation
when taking a decision on the outcome of an investigation in accordance with article 41 of the law of 1 August 2018
on the organization of the National Commission for Data Protection and the general regime on the
Data protection.

The first decisions of the National Commission sitting in restricted formation on the outcome of investigations carried out
since 2018 will be taken and published in 2021.

49

CNPD 2020 ANNUAL REPORT THE CNPD INVESTIGATION PROCEDURE

The opening of an investigation may be proposed at any time by a member of the College in accordance
in article 38 of the aforementioned law of 1 August 2018. He submits this proposal to the College which approves it
within a period of one month by a majority of votes and which appoints a member of the College, with the exception
of the President, as chief of investigation.

The investigation file is then instructed by the head of investigation on the basis of the information, statements and/or
or documents collected during the investigation, including through any interviews and/or requests
additional information. At the end of the investigation, the head of investigation retains the violations
alleged to the GDPR that it intends to materialize in the form of grievances. Grievances are then forwarded to the
controlled which will have the possibility of responding to it in a contradictory way.

These possible exchanges are included in the investigation file which will be sent to the Restricted Panel.
In the event that the head of investigation does not identify or retain any grievance, he transmits to the
Training Restricts a closing proposal.
     
Pursuant to Article 10 2° b) of the CNPD's internal rules adopted by decision
No. 3AD/2020 of January 22, 2020, the National Commission sitting in restricted formation informs
the control of the date of the meeting during which the case concerning him is registered and of the
faculty offered to him to be heard there. During the said session, the head of the investigation is heard to
present oral observations on the case and, when he attends the session, the controlee is invited
to present oral observations.

Following these sessions, the National Commission sitting in restricted formation takes up the case.
deliberate. The decisions it takes are published anonymously, unless anonymization
is not possible or if the CNPD has decided to apply article 52 of the aforementioned law of 1 August 2018,
that is to say, to order the publication of a decision in full and by name.

  LAW ENFORCEMENT OR NATIONAL SECURITY

In application of the law of 1 August 2018 relating to the protection of natural persons with regard to the processing

also competent to control the processing of data carried out by the law enforcement authorities or the authorities
national security, such as the Grand Ducal Police, the State Intelligence Service, the National Authority
Security or Customs Administration.

Article 10 of the law of 1 August 2018 on the organization of the National Commission for the Protection of
data and the general data protection regime also provides that the annual report of the CNPD
must include a list of the types of violations notified and the types of penalties imposed under the law
of 1 August 2018 on the protection of individuals with regard to the processing of personal data
personnel in criminal matters as well as in matters of national security.

the address https://cnpd.public.lu/fr/publications/rapports/index.html.

Video surveillance for police purposes (VISUPOL)

The CNPD notified draft law no. 7498 amending the amended law of July 18, 2018 on the Police
grand ducal.

The purpose of the bill is to give the police video surveillance system a legal framework following
the repeal of the legal basis governing the latter with the entry into force of the law of August 1, 2018 on
organization of the National Data Protection Commission and the general data protection regime
Datas.
 
In general remarks on the scope of the bill, the CNPD welcomed the choice of the
government to legally regulate the installation, management and operation of cameras in public spaces by
the Grand Ducal Police thus taking the example of its neighbours, Belgium, Germany and France. Nevertheless, the
National Commission found that the government had adopted a restrictive approach to the installation

52

3ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPDTHE ACTIVITIES IN 2020 video surveillance devices for public security purposes, limiting their use to the police. THE
municipal officials, many of whom also want to have such devices in order to ensure the safety
public spaces in their municipalities cannot therefore install such surveillance measures. The CNPD has
recalled that the police video surveillance system called "VISUPOL" was limited to the territory of the City of
Luxemburg. Therefore, for a municipality, as data controller, to be able to install a
similar device in public spaces and for public security purposes, a legal text would be needed offering it
such a possibility. In this regard, the National Commission recommended taking the example of legislators
Belgians and French.

In its comments on article 1 of the bill adding a new article 43 bis to the amended law of 18
July 2018 on the Grand Ducal Police, the CNPD notably underlined the imperative to respect the law as well as
European Union case law.

Indeed, the National Commission welcomed the efforts made to respect the principles of accessibility and
transparency of the draft law as well as the quality of the latter, in particular with regard to the delimitation of the
public places presenting risks and ministerial authorization for the installation of the VISUPOL system in
these latter.

Finally, in terms of the proportionality of data processing and appropriate safeguards for safeguarding
fundamental rights and freedoms of citizens, the CNPD considered that the masking procedures of the system
VISUPOL must include the interior of a private place. Regarding the retention period of images
collected, the National Commission considered that the two-month period was proportionate with regard to the purposes
continued.

a period of 72 hours after becoming aware of it, if the violation in question is likely to cause a
risk to the rights and freedoms of data subjects.

EUROPEAN COOPERATION

Coordinated supervision of data processing in EU information systems and by bodies,
European offices and agencies will be gradually transferred from coordination groups (such as the Group of
SIS II Supervision Coordination Group or the European Information System Supervision Coordination Group
Customs) to the Coordinated Supervisory Committee (CSC), created in December 2019 and since then operating under the aegis
of the EDPB.

The CSC brings together in its biannual meetings the supervisory authorities of the EU, the European Protection Supervisor
(EDPS), as well as the supervisory authorities of non-EU Schengen Member States
where EU law so provides.

The main objective of the CSC is to ensure the coordinated oversight of EU information systems and
EU bodies, offices and agencies in the areas of borders, asylum and migration (SIS, EES,
ETIAS and VIS), police and judicial cooperation (SIS, European Public Prosecutor's Office, Eurojust, ECRIS-TCN) and the market
interior (IMI).

54

3ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPDTHE ACTIVITIES IN 2020 The tasks of the Committee consist, in particular, in the support to be provided to the supervisory authorities in carrying out
audits and inspections, the preparation of work on the interpretation or application of legal acts of
the EU concerned and proposals for harmonized solutions, the study of problems related to the exercise of control
independent or the exercise and promotion of the rights of data subjects.

In 2020, the Committee published its work program for the period from 2020 to 2022 with the activities
planned:

• promoting the rights of data subjects;
• examination of difficulties in the interpretation or application of Community and national law;
• the exchange of information;
• carrying out joint audits or coordinated inspections;
• the preparation of supervision at the start of the activities of the European Public Prosecutor's Office and other EU bodies

and information systems that will fall within the scope of CCS in the future.

EUROPEAN DATA PROTECTION BOARD (EDPB)

The EDPB is an independent European body which contributes to the consistent application of the rules on
data protection within the European Union and encourages cooperation between EU authorities responsible for
of data protection.

It is composed of representatives of the national data protection authorities and the Supervisor
European data protection authority, in English “European Data Protection Supervisor” (EDPS).

The EDPB is established by the General Data Protection Regulation and is based in Brussels. There
European Commission has the right to take part in the activities and meetings of the Committee, but does not have the right
of voting.

The EDPB has a secretariat, which is provided by the EDPS. A Memorandum of Understanding defines the terms of the
cooperation between the EDPB and the EDPS.

The purpose of the EDPB is to ensure the consistent application of the General Data Protection Regulation as well as
as the European Directive on Data Protection in the field of law enforcement in the Union
European.

It can adopt general orientation documents in order to clarify the provisions of European legislative acts
in terms of data protection and, in this way, provide the actors concerned with a consistent interpretation
of their rights and obligations.

57

2020 CNPD ANNUAL REPORT The GDPR also entrusts it with the mission of adopting decisions binding on the supervisory authorities
to ensure consistent application of its provisions.

The EDPB has adapted its way of working in the context of the pandemic and organized 27 plenary meetings, including 25
remotely bringing together its members (including the CNPD), compared to 11 plenary meetings per year in
average since its inception. This number of meetings is explained in particular by the intensification of the work of a
hand, due to the emerging issues in the context of the COVID-19 epidemic with regard to the protection of
data, and, on the other hand, by the legal tasks and the judgment Schrems II delivered on July 16, 2020 by the Court of
Justice of the European Union which questioned the mechanism/practices of transfers of personal data
personnel to third States from many actors.

Participation of the CNPD in sub-groups (“Expert SubGroups”)

Alongside the plenary meetings, the data protection authorities of the European Union meet within
thematic sub-groups (legal and IT).

The CNPD has therefore ensured its participation in the work and continues to be represented within all the sub-groups.
thematic experts and networks of the Committee, of which there are 14, as well as the biannual meetings of the
Coordinated Supervisory Committee (CSC).

following the Schrems II judgment of the CJEU and the other, the development of recommendations to help

ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPD Documents adopted in 2020

The EDPB has adopted numerous working documents, guidelines and letters. Some of these documents are summarized
