Contribution to the evaluation of the GDPR by the European Commission

As part of the assessment of the GDPR by the European Commission, all the authorities that are members of the Committee,
including the CNPD, have contributed individual responses to a questionnaire on the evaluation of the GDPR two
years after its implementation sent by the European Commission to the Committee. Based on these answers
EDPB members developed a common response to the European Commission.

The Committee concludes in the communication that it is premature to revise the GDPR. He is convinced that cooperation
between supervisory authorities will lead to a common culture and consistent practice and presents solutions
possible but recognizes at the same time the difficulties in the application of the GDPR resulting from the mosaic of
national procedures. In June 2020, the European Commission confirmed the Committee's analyses.

Guidelines

The EDPB has adopted 9 new guidelines aimed at clarifying the range of GDPR provisions. The lines
guidelines adopted concerned:

- connected vehicles and mobility-related applications;

  The guidelines focus on the processing of personal data in relation to

the non-professional use of connected vehicles by the persons concerned.

- transfers of personal data between public authorities and bodies in the EEA and non-members

of the EEA;

  This guidance aims to provide guidance on the application of Article 46(2)(a)
and paragraph 3, point b), of the GDPR relating to transfers of personal data from authorities or
public bodies in the EEA to public bodies in third countries or to international organisations, in
to the extent that these are not covered by an adequacy decision taken by the European Commission.
Public bodies may choose to use these mechanisms, which the GDPR considers to be better
adapted to their situation, but they are also free to rely on other relevant tools offering
appropriate guarantees in accordance with Article 46 of the GDPR.

6 https://edpb.europa.eu/our-work-tools/our-documents_en

60

3ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPDTHE ACTIVITIES IN 2020 - consent;

  These guidelines provide an in-depth analysis of the concept of consent in the GDPR. The concept
of consent as used hitherto in the Data Protection Directive (“Directive
95/46/EC) and in the “privacy and electronic communications” Directive has evolved. The GDPR brings
clarifications and additional details on the conditions for obtaining and demonstrating a
valid consent.

- the interaction between the second Payment Services Directive (PSD2) and the GDPR;

  The Committee clarifies the roles, the conditions under which the new service providers of initiation of
(PSIP) and account information service providers (PSIC) for which PSD2 introduces
legal framework, can access payment account information. The guidelines for
which the CNPD was rapporteur in 2020, recall that the processing of sensitive data is in principle
prohibited, if the controller has not obtained the explicit consent of the data subject or if
the processing is necessary for reasons of important public interest.

- the concepts of controller and processor;

  These guidelines provide guidance on the question of the criteria by which to qualify the different
actors as controller, processor or joint controller. The document
provides important clarifications on the evolution of these concepts due to the entry into force of the GDPR
and several judgments of the Court of Justice of the European Union. The guidelines are complemented by
graphic illustrations making it easier to understand the different concepts.

- the targeting of social media users;

  These guidelines aim to provide practical guidance to stakeholders and contain various
examples of situations allowing these stakeholders to quickly identify the closest “scenario”
of the targeting practice they intend to deploy. The document aims to clarify in particular the roles and
responsibilities of the social media provider and to identify potential risks to the rights and freedoms
of the persons concerned.

- relevant and reasoned objections;

  As part of the cooperation mechanism established by the GDPR, supervisory authorities have a duty to exchange
any relevant information and to cooperate in an effort to reach a consensus. In accordance with Article 60,

61

ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPD paragraphs 3 and 4, of the GDPR, the lead supervisory authority is required to submit a draft decision
("draft decision") to the supervisory authorities concerned, who may then raise an objection
relevant and reasoned within a specific deadline.

  Two options are available to the lead supervisor when it receives a relevant and reasoned objection
(“reasoned and relevant objection”). If it does not follow this objection or considers that it is not motivated or
relevant, it refers the matter to the Committee within the framework of the consistency control mechanism (Article
65 GDPR). When, on the contrary, the lead supervisory authority intends to follow up the objection and submits a draft
revised decision, the supervisory authorities concerned may express a relevant and reasoned objection to the
the revised draft decision within two weeks.

  This guideline has made it possible to define more precisely what is meant by “relevant and reasoned objection”.

Work targeting the processing of personal data in the context of the fight against the epidemic of
COVID-19

In the context of the pandemic, the EDPB has undertaken to advise governments, authorities, responsible for the
treatment and developers of digital solutions to enable them to overcome the challenges posed by the crisis
of COVID-19 while respecting the fundamental rights to data protection and privacy.

Here is a selection of the work finalized by the Committee made public during the year:

• In guidelines on the use of location data and contact tracing tools
to combat COVID-19, the Committee responds to certain initiatives taken by governments to
fight the pandemic, including the use of location data or contact tracing, in
indicating which may be acceptable purposes while insisting on the principles of efficiency,
necessity and proportionality;

• The statement on the impact of data protection on the interoperability of tracing applications
contacts contains a more in-depth analysis of key aspects, including transparency, the basis
legal status, the status of data controller, the rights of data subjects, the storage and
data minimization, information security and data accuracy in the creation
an interoperable network of applications;

• Guidelines on the processing of health data for research purposes aim to shed light on questions
urgent legal issues that were emerging in the context of the COVID-19 epidemic regarding the use of
health data;

• The EDPB responded to a request for consultation from the European Commission concerning its project
of guidelines on applications to support the fight against the COVID-19 pandemic. In his response,
the EDPB specifically addressed the use of apps for the contact tracing function and

62

3ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPDTHE ACTIVITIES IN 2020 alert. In this area, increased attention must be given to minimizing the impact on the protection
privacy while allowing the processing of data for the purpose of safeguarding public health;

• The declaration on the rights of data subjects in relation to the state of emergency in the Member States
essentially recalls the main principles applicable to the limitations of the rights of data subjects and
recalls that, even in a state of emergency, the protection of personal data must be preserved
in all emergency measures of a rule of law. According to the statement, the Committee had also given
a mandate to the “Key Provisions” sub-committee to work on guidelines on the limitations of rights
people concerned in general. These guidelines have been submitted for public consultation;

Schengen following the COVID-19 pandemic is particularly aimed at measures such as screening for
COVID-19, the requirement for certificates issued by health professionals and the use of a
voluntary contact tracing.

European cooperation in the context of Brexit

Preparation for Brexit, including the impact on borders, free movement and the exchange of data between forces
of the order of the Member States and the United Kingdom has been the subject of several declarations and information notes which
have been published on the Committee's website.

The EDPS has published a statement on the end of the Brexit transition period, in which it describes the main
implications of the end of this period for data controllers and processors.
The European Data Protection Supervisor insisted in particular on the question of the mechanisms for
data transfers to a third country as well as the consequences in the field of surveillance
regulation and the one-stop-shop mechanism.

Recommendations

The EDPB has issued recommendations on European essential safeguards for surveillance measures
and on the measures that complement the transfer tools to ensure compliance with the level of data protection
of a personal nature from the EU.

These recommendations, to which the CNPD provided its support within the dedicated taskforce, have as
objective of helping data exporters to comply with the Schrems II judgment, which requires
identify and implement effective additional measures where needed. It is therefore
to allow lawful transfers of personal data to third countries while guaranteeing
that the data transferred benefit from a level of protection essentially equivalent to that guaranteed in
the EEA.

63

CNPD 2020 ANNUAL REPORT Recommendations contain a roadmap of steps that data exporters should follow and
a non-exhaustive list of examples of additional measures and the conditions under which they may
be effective.

This document should be read in conjunction with the recommendations on European Core Safeguards
providing data exporters with elements to enable them to determine whether the legal framework governing
access by public authorities to data for surveillance purposes in third countries can be considered
as a justifiable interference with the rights to privacy and data protection
staff.

Both documents were submitted for public consultation after their adoption. A final version will be adopted
in 2021.

Consistency Notice

In order to ensure the consistent application of the GDPR in cases with cross-border implications, the EDPB has
issued 31 consistency opinions.

These opinions concerned in particular the draft lists submitted by supervisory authorities concerning
processing operations for which a DPIA is required, as well as those for which no DPIA is
required. The other opinions related to standard contractual clauses, Binding Corporate Rules (BCR),
the powers of the supervisory authorities and the criteria for approving the organizations responsible for monitoring the codes
of conduct or certification.

The first opinions on the accreditation criteria for certification bodies have been adopted by the Committee.
The CNPD submitted its accreditation criteria (“GDPR CARPA”) to the EDPB at the same time as the authority of the
United Kingdom. She was co-rapporteur with the EDPB secretariat for the opinion on the accreditation criteria
of the UK authority, while the latter was co-rapporteur with the EDPB Secretariat for the opinion
rendered on the CNPD accreditation criteria. These opinions aim to establish a coherent and harmonized approach
regarding the requirements that supervisory authorities and national accreditation bodies will apply when
accreditation of certification bodies. This work constitutes an important step towards the delivery
by the EDPB of notices of approval of the certification criteria and therefore towards the European seal of protection of
data.

Guidelines on the analysis of certification criteria for which the CNPD was rapporteur in 2020
will support the efforts of the control authorities in the promotion and implementation of the European seal of
Data protection.

64

3ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPDACTIVITIES IN 2020 Binding decisions

The EDPB also acts as a dispute resolution body and issues binding decisions. In 2020,
it issued its first binding decision regarding a dispute that arose following the draft decision of
the Irish authority regarding the company Twitter.

New register of decisions taken by national authorities

The EDPB has published on its website a new register containing the decisions taken by the national authorities of
data protection following the cooperation procedure with the “one-stop shop” mechanism (Article.
60 GDPR).

Under the GDPR, supervisory authorities have a duty to cooperate in matters having a
cross-border to ensure consistent application of the GDPR. Within the framework of this "one-stop shop" mechanism
unique” (in English “OSS” or “One-Stop-Shop mechanism”), the lead supervisory authority is in charge
to prepare draft decisions and works with the other supervisory authorities concerned to reach a
consensus.

Until the beginning of June, the supervisory authorities adopted 110 decisions under the window mechanism
unique. The register includes access to decisions as well as summaries of decisions in English prepared
by the EDPB secretariat. This register will be an important source of information for practitioners of the
data protection who will have access to information showing how the supervisory authorities
work together to enforce the GDPR in practice. The information contained in the register has been
validated by the lead authorities in question and in accordance with the conditions provided for by the legislation
national.

Strategy 2021-2023

At the end of 2020, the EDPB adopted its 2021-2023 strategy, which defines the strategic objectives of the Committee,
gathered around four pillars, as well as three key actions per pillar to help achieve these objectives.

The four pillars of the EDPB strategy are:

• promote harmonization and facilitate compliance;
• supporting the effective application of the rules and effective cooperation between national supervisory authorities;
• an approach to new technologies based on fundamental rights;
• the global dimension.

65

ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPD The strategy will also be implemented through a work programme, which will further detail the
EDPB actions. This work program will be adopted at the beginning of 2021.

As part of its 2021-2023 strategy, the EDPB has decided to create a group of support experts based on
a “Support Pool of Experts” (SPE) pilot project. The objective is to provide material support to its members
in the form of expertise useful for investigations and enforcement activities and to improve
cooperation and solidarity between EDPB members by sharing, strengthening and complementing strengths,
as well as meeting operational needs.

 

GLOBAL PRIVACY ASSEMBLY

The “Global Privacy Assembly” (GPA) is a forum for discussion on a global scale, where 130 members share
best practices and adopt common positions. Founded in 1979, it was known as
“International Conference of Data Protection and Privacy Commissioners” (ICDPPC) until 2019. This
name change reflects the will of this assembly to strengthen its visibility and its position as an actor
effective and influential at the international level.

The CNPD participated in the 2020 "At your desk" edition of the international conference on Global Privacy
Assembly. Initially scheduled in Mexico, the 41st conference took place online in 3 sessions of 3 hours
from October 13 to 15, 2020.

The data protection authorities as well as some NGOs reflected together on the challenges posed by the
COVID-19 in terms of data protection and privacy.

The discussions focused on important issues such as:

• the global interoperability of laws and regulations relating to data protection and the protection of

private life ;

• developing innovative regulatory cooperation approaches to address AI challenges;
• the relationship between data protection and other rights and freedoms;
• ways to improve cooperation between authorities in investigations;
• the promotion of cooperation between the authorities responsible for data protection, data protection and

consumers and competition;

• ways to address privacy and data protection in digital education and data protection

children's privacy.

The CNPD contributed to the following working groups:

66

3ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPDACTIVITIES IN 2020 The 2020 “At your desk” edition of the international conference of the “Global Privacy Assembly”. From left to right: Jérôme Commod,
Tine A. Larsen, Marc Lemmer and Romy Schaus.

• Digital Education Working Group: technical support for the reorganization and revitalization of the

sharing of CIRCABC educational tools;

• Policy Strategy Working Group: co-drafting of the Digital Economy Background Paper;
• Legal framework and practices of data protection authorities relating to the exercise of the rights of minors;
• COVID-19 related Privacy and Data Protection Issues Working Group: Resolution on privacy challenges

and data protection in the context of the COVID-19 pandemic.

Several resolutions were passed at the international conference. The CNPD was co-sponsor of two
between them :

• the resolution on international humanitarian aid and
• the resolution on joint statements on emerging global issues.

One of the most striking resolutions was that on the challenges related to privacy and data protection.
in the context of the COVID-19 pandemic.

In this resolution, GPA members recognized the important work done by the COVID-19 taskforce to
identify via a survey among the members of the assembly, the problems related to the protection of privacy that
emerged as a result of the pandemic:

67

CNPD 2020 ANNUAL REPORT • “contact tracing”, including digital contact tracing, which was the most pressing concern

courts;

• the processing of employee data in work-from-home and return-to-work situations;
• the processing of children's/students' data associated with the use of training and

online schooling;

• exchange of health data between hospitals, ministries of health and other agencies

competent governments.

The taskforce has developed a compendium of good practices in the context of the pandemic. It is designed as
a document likely to be updated by the members of the GPA.

According to the resolution, data protection authorities should continue to play a central role in providing
advice and assistance to governments, relevant organizations and other stakeholders
on how to process and share personal data in the context of the pandemic.

One of the decisions of the resolution is to build on the work carried out by the taskforce and to create a
new temporary working group (Working Group on Data Protection and Privacy Issues)
related to COVID-19), with an initial mandate of one year. This working group will have as its main mission
formulate recommendations and coordinate the responses of the GPA on issues arising in the
context of the COVID-19 pandemic and on the road to recovery.

The “Digital Education Working Group”

The priority of this working group is to make children, adolescents and adults more aware of the
data protection issues, as this strengthens their ability to claim their data protection rights.
data in the digital world.

An electronic library tool allows sharing between data protection authorities (DPAs) at the
world-class privacy education materials available and other online resources
line from both authorities and other stakeholders. The Commission's CIRCABC platform
European Union hosts this online tool created in 2015.

The CNPD, together with its French counterpart, the CNIL, defined and implemented the project to revitalize the
online bookstore to ensure that it remains effective and attractive. The goal of the project is to increase the use
made by the authorities, the promotion of the tool, the distribution of the didactic elements stored there and the encouragement
authorities to draw inspiration from this material for use in their country.

42020 CNPD ANNUAL REPORTRESOURCES,STRUCTURES AND OPERATION Adoption of the internal rules, the procedure relating to complaints and the procedure relating to
to investigations

In 2020, the CNPD adopted several important documents concerning its internal organization and procedures
of work. These include the internal rules of procedure (ROI), the procedure relating to complaints
(described in more detail in part II.6. of this report) and the procedure relating to investigations (described more
in detail in Part II.8. of this report).

The internal rules of the CNPD were adopted pursuant to Articles 32 paragraph (1) and 33 of the
organic law of 1 August 2018 and defines the operating conditions, the organization of services and the rules
procedure applicable before the CNPD.

Chapter 1 describes the operating conditions of the CNPD and in particular the composition, business
day-to-day, external representation, financial management and the conduct of the deliberation sessions of the
College.

The organization of the departments and the general structure of the National Commission are presented in Chapter 2.
These are more particularly the services "Awareness", "Guidance", "Compliance", "Complaints",
“Investigations” and “Administration”. In addition, the following functions directly attached to the
National Commission: the Secretary(ies) of the College, the European and International Relations Officer and the
data protection officer.

Chapter 3 defines the procedures applicable before the CNPD in the following cases:

• Notification of a personal data breach;
• Prior consultation;
• Notification of the appointment of the Data Protection Officer;
• Approval of codes of conduct;
• Accreditation of organizations monitoring codes of conduct;
• Approval of certification criteria;
• Approval of certification bodies;
• Authorizations of contractual clauses and administrative arrangements;
• Approval of Binding Corporate Rules;
• Submission of a complaint;
• Requests for prior opinion under Article L. 261-1 of the Labor Code;
• Surveys;
•  Remedies.

75

2020 CNPD ANNUAL REPORT 3 MANAGEMENT REPORT ON THE 2020 FINANCIAL STATEMENTS

In May 2020, after carrying out a strategic and forward-looking reflection on its operation and its
missions, the National Commission for Data Protection (CNPD) has adopted the document “Strategy and
work program 2020-2022”. This document will enable it to better plan, coordinate and monitor its
activities during the relevant period and thus improve the management of its resources.

It is precisely in this order of ideas that a new organizational chart had already been adopted by the College
of the CNPD in January 2020, enabling it to face not only the challenges of a digital world, but
also to the paradigm shift brought about by the General Data Protection Regulation (GDPR)
and which is expressed by the switch from a priori control to a posteriori control.

The structure thus adopted provides since then and apart from the secretariat of the College, the person in charge of relations
European and international organizations as well as the data protection officer, the following six departments:

•  Sensitization ;
• Guidance;
•  Compliance ;
• Complaints;
• Surveys and
• Administration.

After the reinforcement of the workforce carried out between 2015 and 2018 in preparation for the changes
generated by the new European regulations on data protection, the strategic document
now aims to consolidate the operational teams. It is now a question of expanding the resources of the
different departments according to their respective needs, needs estimated at a total workforce of 62 FTE (equivalent
full-time) compared to a total authorized workforce of 54 FTE in December 2020. It should be noted that in the concern
to optimize the operation of the CNPD, the total workforce should have reached 62 FTEs in 2023. However, given the
reduction of the allocation allocated to the CNPD for the 2021 financial year and following, the recruitments planned for the
years 2021 to 2023 had to be carried over to the years 2025 to 2027.

With regard to expenditure for the 2020 financial year, it should be noted that the figures in question cannot be considered
as representative due to two factors that have largely influenced the operation and therefore
CNPD expenses.

It is first and obviously about the health crisis and the measures resulting from it, but also about
fitting out and moving to the new CNPD headquarters.

76

4ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPDRESOURCES,STRUCTURES AND OPERATION While the health crisis has resulted in a reduction in certain categories of expenditure, the development
new premises and the move generated additional costs.

The budget

The CNPD budget for the 2020 financial year amounted to €7,667,224, an increase of
40.88% compared to the budget of the previous year. The increase of €2,224,808 compared to 2019
results mainly from the reinforcement of the workforce, which in fact represents 84.73% of the total budget. Add to it
in addition, the rent and rental charges for the new headquarters, while the old premises were made available
by the Belval Fund at the cost of the charges.

Expenses

During the 2020 financial year, the total expenditure of the public establishment amounted to €6,336,137 compared to
to €4,799,182 in 2019, an increase of 32.03%, while remaining below the budget appropriation
granted and amounting to €7,667,224.

The budget surplus is mainly explained by a capital loss for personnel expenses in
due to the fact that following the health crisis and in view of the move to the new headquarters, all recruitments
planned from January 2020 had to be postponed until the end of the year, or even at the beginning of the following year.

It should be noted that the expenses relating to the development of the new headquarters amounting to €509,946 are not included or
in the total expenditure, nor in the amount of the budget appropriation granted for the 2020 financial year listed above.
Indeed, and in accordance with the decision of the College taken during the plenary session of May 27, 2020, the financing
related was realized through the allocation of the capital gain for the 2019 financial year.

 

1. Fixed assets

In compliance with article 46 of the law of August 1, 2018 on the organization of the National Commission for
data protection and the general data protection regime providing that "the accounts of the
CNPD are held according to the rules of commercial accounting”, the expenses related to the acquisition of
furniture, software and computer equipment have been recorded in the balance sheet of the public institution; their
depreciation being recorded under operating costs.

In 2020, the additional furniture required as part of the fitting out of the new offices was
resulted in an expense of €70,644.

77

CNPD 2020 ANNUAL REPORT Expenditure for the acquisition of new IT equipment amounted to €21,681, while
that in 2019 no corresponding expenditure was recorded.

 

2. Operating costs

Operating costs amounted to €806,279 compared to €405,529 in 2019, an increase
of €400,750 respectively 98.82%, without however exceeding the initial budget forecasts of a
amount of €1,033,260.

 

2.1. Overheads

The aforementioned increase of 98.82% results in particular from the fact that, following the increase in its workforce and given
the impossibility of the Belval Fund to offer a short-term solution to the needs of the CNPD, the latter
forced to move towards a rental on the private market. If the old seat had been gracefully put
made available by the Belval Fund, the CNPD must now bear the cost of the rent relating to its new
premises located in the NAOS building, as well as the related rental charges. In 2020, the fees in question
amounted to €547,657, an increase of €392,870 compared to 2019 where expenditure at
charge of this item in the amount of €154,787 mainly included the rent for the new headquarters (in progress
planning) due for the months of October to December 2019.

Expenditure relating to office equipment and supplies amounted to €19,924. The progress of
13.74% compared to 2019 follows the reinforcement of the workforce at the end of 2019 and during 2020.

With the move into the new premises, the related development and maintenance costs have gone from
€21,097 in 2019 to €42,335 for the year 2020. Since the surface of the new headquarters substantially exceeds
that of the old one, the costs related to daily cleaning have increased accordingly.

Documentation costs decreased by 37.28% from €10,716 in 2019 to €6,721 for the financial year
2020. The same applies to shipping and telecommunications costs which amounted in 2020 to
€10,426 compared to €11,418 in 2019, i.e. a decrease of 8.69%.

Professional fees amounting to €34,253 include expenses relating to the maintenance
accounting books, payroll management and auditing of accounts, an amount exceeding 76.99%
that of the expenses for the 2019 financial year amounting to €19,353. This increase is primarily due to a
increase in costs resulting from the management of salaries and then by the fact that in 2020 this position
included both the fees due to the auditor for the current year and the balance due for
the 2019 financial year.

78

4ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPDRESOURCES,STRUCTURES ANDOPERATION Other general operating expenses in the amount of €19,107 remained stable except that an expense
exceptional at the rate of €30,448 is added to this for moving costs to the new premises.

 

2.2. Communication and public relations

Due to the health crisis, all meetings and conferences abroad have been replaced by video-
conferences so that travel, representation and public relations expenses in the amount of €9,002
remained well below budget forecasts amounting to €52,000 and the expenses incurred
in 2019 and amounting to €61,238, i.e. a reduction of 85.30%.

Communication and publication costs decreased from €33,571 in 2019 to €23,145 in 2020 and
include, among other things, the publication costs of the 2019 Annual Report amounting to €9,312 as well as the
advertisements relating to vacancies amounting to €8,566.

Due to the health crisis, no data protection awareness campaign has been possible.
be organised, so that the expenses charged to this item have been reduced to zero, whereas in 2019 they
amounted to €29,679.

 

2.3. IT costs

IT costs amounted to €63,261 in 2020 compared to €61,271 in 2019 and include
As in previous years, the hosting by a specialized company of the “CNPD Compliance Support” tool
Tool”, developed by the CNPD in cooperation with LIST (Luxembourg Institute of Science and Technology) and the
Media and Communications Department for an amount of €54,405. Plus maintenance costs.
IT tools operated by the “Administration” department.

It should be noted that the expenditure for this item remained below the budget forecasts. Indeed, since
the migration in 2017 of IT systems to the services of the Information Technology Center
of the State (CTIE), the costs in question remained below forecasts due to the fact that the platform
"Sharepoint" working tool used since then by the CNPD, was able to be developed using exclusively
CTIE standard components. It follows that the system thus exploited can be operated on a standard platform
and pooling of the CTIE, allowing the related costs to be considerably reduced.

 

3. Personnel costs

The remuneration of the staff liquidated in 2020 amounted to €5,387,440 compared to €4,362,465
in 2019. This increase is due to significant recalculations resulting from the abolition of the reduction made on
internship allowances as well as the reinforcement of staff carried out in mid and end of 2019 and during 2020.

79

CNPD 2020 ANNUAL REPORT In addition to the four commissioners, the permanent staff included as of December 31, 2020:

• 39 staff in salary or allowance group A1, including 16 officials,
• 1 employee from indemnity group A2 as well as
• 5 staff in salary or allowance group B1, including 4 officials.

Added to this is a temporary A1 employee on a fixed-term contract.

Despite a considerable increase in the number of staff noted at the end of the year, actual expenditure remained in
below the budget forecast. Indeed, with the health crisis, some of the planned recruitments could not
be carried out only at the end of the year, respectively had to be postponed to the beginning of the following year.

Apart from salaries, staff costs also include an amount of €4,555 representing the
expenses relating to staff training. The increase compared to 2019 of the expenditure in question
follows the increase in the number of employees in service activity.
 
 

4. Other costs

In this case, these are the costs generated by the service car in the amount of €4,242, i.e. a reduction
of 42.21% compared to 2019 and resulting from a renegotiation with retroactive effect of the leasing contract based on
the distances actually traveled.

The depreciation of furniture and IT equipment recognized for an amount of €37,993 follows that of
investments made and represents an increase of 62.61% compared to €23,365 for the financial year
2019.

Added to this are bank charges and similar charges in the amount of €3,303.

•  The air conditioning.

The recipes

If the CNPD does not have revenue in the proper sense of the term, it may nevertheless and under Article 47 of the
Law of 1 August 2018 organizing the National Commission for Data Protection and the regime
general on data protection, impose fees within the framework of its powers of authorization and
consultation. Although the CNPD adopted in 2020 a regulation setting the amount and terms of payment
related, no fees were collected in 2020.

Thus the receipts were limited to the rent relating to the subletting of the parking spaces included
in the lease relating to the headquarters located in the basement of the NAOS building and amounting to €20,045. Add another
amount of €762 representing, among other things, participation in the mPass costs of CNPD agents.

The operating result

data has benefited from the State in application of article 47 of the aforementioned law of August 1, 2018, the result
operating costs of the public establishment amounted to €934,270 as of December 31, 2020.

2020 ANNUAL REPORT CNPD Opinion on bill no. 7511 on the processing of health data
on insurance and reinsurance and amending the amended law of
December 7, 2015 on the insurance sector.

(Deliberation n°2/2020 of 01/27/2020)

In accordance with Article 57, paragraph 1, letter (c) of Regulation No. 2016/679 of April 27, 2016 relating to
the protection of individuals with regard to the processing of personal data and the freedom
movement of such data, and repealing Directive 95/46/EC (general data protection regulation)
(hereinafter “the GDPR”), to which article 7 of the law of 1 August 2018 on the organization of the Commission refers
national data protection system and the general data protection regime, the Commission
Commission for Data Protection (hereinafter referred to as “the National Commission” or “the CNPD”)
“advises, in accordance with the law of the Member State, the national parliament, the government and other institutions
and organizations regarding legislative and administrative measures relating to the protection of the rights and freedoms of
natural persons with regard to the processing”.

By letter dated December 23, 2019, the Minister of Finance invited the National Commission
to decide on draft law no. 7511 relating to the processing of data concerning health in the field of
insurance and reinsurance services and amending the amended law of 7 December 2015 on the insurance sector
insurance (hereinafter “Bill No. 7511”).

It appears from the explanatory memorandum that the purpose of this bill, by its sole article, is to introduce into
the amended law of 7 December 2015 on the insurance sector a national provision to legitimize the
processing of health data in insurance matters based, in accordance with Article 9 paragraph (2)
letter g) of the GDPR, reasons of important public interest. Health data is indeed strictly regulated
by said Article 9 of the GDPR and can only be processed if one of the ten conditions listed therein is met.

In the commentary to the sole article of bill no. 7511, the authors of the bill argue that
sufficiency of law why the consent of persons wishing to take out insurance cannot be
a “reliable and solid enabling base for the processing of health data”. For this reason, this
legislative intervention is an essential requirement to remove the legal uncertainty in which companies
insurance companies are at the current stage when they process special categories of data within the meaning of
Article 9 paragraph (1) of the GDPR and more particularly health data.

Indeed, already in its second additional opinion of 8 June 20181 concerning bill no. 7184 which has become
the law of 1 August 2018 on the organization of the National Commission for Data Protection and the system

1 Deliberation n°423/2018 of June 8, 2018.

88

5ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPDANNEXES general data protection, the National Commission had considered that, even if it is a fact that the
insurance companies must be able to process health data for certain types of insurance contracts,
“the explicit consent provided for in Article 9 paragraph (2) letter a) of the GDPR of the data subjects does not
does not make it possible to legitimize this data processing, whereas it could not be considered as free within the meaning
of the GDPR for certain types of insurance (e.g. life insurance in the context of a mortgage loan, insurance
remaining due balance, …) ". Furthermore, since an insurance contract is a membership contract, the Commission
national authority had considered that consent was not to be considered appropriate to legitimize the processing
of health data in this context, but that an appropriate condition of legitimacy could have been the one provided
in Article 6 paragraph (1) letter b) of the GDPR (“the processing is necessary for the performance of a contract to which the
data subject is a party or to the execution of pre-contractual measures taken at the latter's request"). Gold,
it turns out that the European legislator did not provide for this purpose in Article 9 of the GDPR.

The National Commission had also suggested to the legislator to introduce a provision in Bill No. 7184
aforementioned allowing insurance companies to process health data, with the exception of genetic data,
when it is necessary for the performance of an insurance contract to which the data subject is a party or for the performance
pre-contractual measures taken at the latter's request. However, this proposed text did not meet
the consent of the Council of State which considered that it is a “national derogation, added to the list of paragraph
2 [of Article 9 of the GDPR], which Member States are not allowed to introduce. The device cannot
no longer be deemed to introduce additional conditions within the meaning of Article 9(4).
new paragraph 2 of article 66 of the bill, in its amended version, is therefore not in conformity with article
9 of the regulations. The Council of State must formally oppose it and request its deletion”.2

The single article of bill n°7511 as proposed by the legislator is based on article 9 paragraph
(2) letter g) of the GDPR which provides that “the processing is necessary for reasons of important public interest, on
the basis of Union law or the law of a Member State which must be proportionate to the objective pursued, respect
the essence of the right to data protection and provide for appropriate and specific measures for safeguarding
fundamental rights and interests of the data subject”.

According to the National Commission, this provision does not add derogations to those provided for in Article 9
paragraph (2) of the GDPR and does not rely on Article 9 paragraph (4) of the GDPR allowing Member States
to “maintain or introduce additional terms, including limitations, with respect to the
processing of genetic data, biometric data or data concerning health”. The treatment
of health data by insurance and reinsurance companies can thus be based on
Article 9 paragraph 2 letter g) of the GDPR, provided that the national law specifically provides "for
appropriate and specific measures to safeguard the fundamental rights and interests of the person
concerned”, which is currently not the case. In other words, the purpose of the bill under notice is to
fill this gap in national law.

2 Second additional opinion of the Council of State of 17 July 2018 concerning bill no. 7184.

89

ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPD The CNPD welcomes the text of the bill under examination, while point 2 of article 181bis of the amended law
of 7 December 2015 on the insurance sector, introduced by the single article of bill no. 7511 under
review, sufficiently lists what are these appropriate and specific measures for the safeguarding of
fundamental rights and interests of the data subject which are to be observed in the event of data processing
of health necessary for the performance of pre-contractual measures in matters of insurance or reinsurance or
the performance of an insurance or reinsurance contract, following the example of other European legislation such as
example that of Ireland, the United Kingdom or the Netherlands.

However, the National Commission notes that the commentary to the single article, as well as the explanatory memorandum
mention next to Article 9 paragraph (2) letter g) of the GDPR as the legal basis for data processing
of health by the insurance companies Article 9 paragraph (4) of the GDPR. However, because of the above explanations, it
is of the opinion that the only legal basis applicable in cash is Article 9 paragraph (2) letter g) of the GDPR and it
thus proposes to delete any reference to Article 9 paragraph (4) of the GDPR.

Finally, the CNPD would like to point out that the bill under consideration has no impact on the application
of the rules of the GDPR, i.e. all the provisions provided for in the GDPR remain applicable to companies
insurance and reinsurance companies that process health data in accordance with the single article of the bill
No. 7511. In particular, the principles relating to the processing of personal data listed in Article
5 of the GDPR and all the general obligations incumbent on the controller and provided for in Chapter IV
of the GDPR must be observed. Furthermore, the persons concerned, i.e. the customers and prospects of the said
companies, have all the rights provided for in Articles 13 to 22 of the GDPR under the conditions listed therein and in
no case, the single article of bill n°7511 could be used as a legal basis to circumvent the
compliance with the aforementioned rights.

Thus decided in Esch-sur-Alzette on January 27, 2020.

52020 ANNUAL REPORT CNPDANNEXES Opinion on bill no. 7498 amending the amended law of 18 July
2018 on the Grand Ducal Police.

(Deliberation n°04/2020 of February 28, 2020)

In accordance with article 46, paragraph 1, letter (c) of directive (EU) n°2016/680 of 27 April 2016 relating to
the protection of individuals with regard to the processing of personal data by
competent authorities for the purposes of prevention, investigation and prosecution of criminal offenses
or execution of criminal penalties, and on the free movement of such data, and repealing the Framework Decision
2008/977/JHA of the Council (hereinafter referred to as "the directive"), to which article 8 of the law of 1 August refers
2018 on the organization of the National Commission for Data Protection and the general regime on the
data protection (hereinafter referred to as the “Law of 1 August 2018 on the organization of the National Commission
for data protection and the general data protection regime”), the National Commission
for Data Protection (hereinafter referred to as “the National Commission” or “the CNPD”), “advises the
Chamber of Deputies, the Government and other institutions and bodies regarding legislative measures and
administrative procedures relating to the protection of the rights and freedoms of natural persons with regard to the processing of
personal data ".

By letter dated November 5, 2019, the Minister of Internal Security invited the National Commission to
to rule on bill n°7498 amending the amended law of July 18, 2018 on the Police
Grand-Ducale (hereinafter referred to as "the bill").

The purpose of the bill is to give the police video surveillance system a legal framework following the repeal
of the legal basis governing the latter with the entry into force of the law of 1 August 2018 on the organization of
the National Commission for Data Protection and the general data protection regime. In effect,
this repeals article 17 of the law of 2 August 2002 on the protection of individuals with regard to the processing of
personal data, as amended in 2007 (hereinafter referred to as "the law of 2 August 2002"). The article in
question was the legal basis for the creation and operation of the video surveillance device for police purposes
called VISUPOL. Pursuant to Article 17, the Grand-Ducal regulation of 1 August 2007 authorized the creation and
the operation of VISUPOL by the Grand Ducal Police within security zones (hereinafter referred to as “the regulations
of application”). The implementing regulation delegated the determination of the safety zones concerned to the minister having
in its attributions the Grand-Ducal Police, namely the Minister of Internal Security. Moreover, it is relevant
to mention that the law of 1 August 2018 on the protection of natural persons with regard to the processing
personal data in criminal matters as well as in matters of national security applies to
processing of personal data by the Grand Ducal Police in the performance of its missions3. It's about
including missions to detect, investigate and prosecute criminal offences, or

3 Law of 1 August 2018 on the protection of individuals with regard to the processing of personal data in criminal matters and in criminal matters.

national security, Article 1 paragraph 2. a).

91

2020 CNPD ANNUAL REPORT on the execution of criminal sanctions, including protection against threats to public security and prevention
of these 4.

The bill refers to the opinion of the CNPD of March 15, 2019 relating to the video surveillance of spaces and places
public for public safety purposes. The CNPD then self-seized the problem of the legal basis framing
CCTV devices. In that opinion, the National Commission then adopted a global approach
with regard to the use of video surveillance devices for police purposes, namely, prevention, research and
the finding of infringements6.

The CNPD wishes to recall that video surveillance systems interfere with the rights
fundamentals and freedoms granted to individuals. Therefore, providing a legal basis for such systems
is crucial in order to establish safeguards for people who are subject to surveillance and control as a result.
emanate. However, respect for fundamental rights is not absolute since interference with them
is recognized in Article 52 paragraph 1 of the Charter of Fundamental Rights of the European Union (hereinafter
referred to as “the Charter”). The European Convention on Human Rights (hereinafter referred to as “the ECHR”) makes
this limitation is also possible while linking it to respect for private and family life in Article 87.

I. General comments on the scope of the draft law

- A restrictive approach adopted by the government

The CNPD notes that the bill aims to amend Chapter V of the Law of 18 July 2018 on the Police, Chapter
dedicated to the processing of personal data by including an article 43 bis.

In its opinion of March 15, 2019 relating to the video surveillance of public spaces and places for the purposes of
public security, the CNPD has already emphasized that as a whole, the amended law of 18 July 2018 on
the Grand-Ducal Police did not provide for any provision relating to the video surveillance system. The CNPD welcomes
the government's choice to legally regulate the installation, management and operation of cameras in space
public by the Grand Ducal Police. Such an approach follows the examples of neighboring countries, namely France,
Belgium and Germany.

On the other hand, the CNPD regrets that the bill did not adopt a global approach relating to the installation of
video surveillance device with a public safety purpose8. Indeed, the Grand Ducal Police is not
the only one willing to manage, operate and use video surveillance for public safety purposes. The responsibles
of municipalities are also numerous to want to equip themselves with such devices in order to ensure the safety of the premises
public in their municipalities. In this respect, it is useful to recall that in its opinion of May 10, 2019 relating to the appeal

4 Ibid., paragraph 1.
5 Deliberation n°36/2019 of March 15, 2019.
6 In accordance with article 17 of the law of 2 August 2002 relating to the protection of individuals with regard to the processing of personal data (repealed) and

Article 2 of the amended law of 18 July 2018 on the Grand Ducal Police.

7 European Convention on Human Rights, signed in Rome, 4.XI.1950, article 8.
8 Bill no. 7498 amending the amended law of 18 July 2018 on the Grand-Ducal Police, comments on the articles p.6.

92

5ANNUAL REPORT2020 CNPDANNEXES video surveillance by the municipalities, requested by the Minister of the Interior, the CNPD reported the
numerous requests for information from municipal officials regarding the installation of devices
video surveillance in public spaces and the desire of some elected officials to link their municipalities to VISUPOL. Of
moreover, the Minister of Internal Security recently underlined that the interest of the mayors in the installation
of surveillance devices in the municipalities persists9.

The CNPD has already noted in its aforementioned opinions that the municipal law of 13 December 198810 and the law
of 18 July 2018 on the Grand Ducal Police11 both highlight the interactions between the Police
and the mayors without however distinguishing their respective competences within the framework of police power
administration.

In addition, it is necessary to remember that for the moment the VISUPOL police video surveillance system is limited
to the territory of the City of Luxembourg and that, according to information from the CNPD, an extension of the latter
to other municipalities is not envisaged. However, if a municipality wishes, as data controller,
install a similar device in public spaces (covering areas of more or less
large as e.g. public squares, municipal parks, municipal playgrounds or leisure areas…) for purposes
of public security, including in general the security of persons and property, a legal text should be
gives him that opportunity. To do this, the National Commission is of the opinion that the addition of such a provision
in the bill under consideration is essential. Indeed, the bill under consideration provides that only the
Director General of the Grand Ducal Police may take the initiative, with the authorization of the Minister, to extend
the VISUPOL video surveillance system to other municipal territories than that of the City of Luxembourg.
As more fully developed in its opinions of March 15, 2019 relating to the video surveillance of spaces and
public places for public security purposes (deliberation no. 36/2019) and of 10 May 2019 relating to the appeal of the
video surveillance by the municipalities (deliberation n°39/2019), the installation of video cameras by a municipality
in public spaces and places for the purposes of public security would hardly be reconcilable with the legislation
current data protection policy.

The CNPD therefore considers that it would be appropriate to adopt a global approach to video surveillance at
purposes of public security, a choice which appears to have been adopted by the Belgian and French legislators.

- A global approach adopted by the Belgian and French legislators

The question as to the only modification of the amended law of 18 July 2018 on the Grand Ducal Police lies
also in the fact that the authors of the bill were inspired by the Belgian model12.

If it turns out that the Belgian legislator has "retained to regulate video surveillance for police purposes in the dedicated law
to the Police”13, the CNPD observes that the latter nevertheless opted for a global approach to the management,

9 During the program “Face à face” on RTL on November 15, 2019, available at: https://www.rtl.lu/radio/face-a-face/a/1431367.html, consulted for the

last seen on 11/25/2019.

10 Articles 67 and 68.
11 Articles 35 et seq.
12 Bill no. 7498 amending the amended law of 18 July 2018 on the Grand-Ducal Police, comments on articles p.4.
13 Ibid.

93

CNPD 2020 ANNUAL REPORT operation and use of video surveillance in the broad sense. Indeed, the law of March 21, 201814 modifies
both the law on the function of the police in order to regulate the use of cameras by the latter15 and the law of 21
March 2007 regulating the installation of surveillance cameras16. For all intents and purposes, the material scope
of the said law on the management and operation of video surveillance devices can be recalled. In chapter
2, article 6 paragraph 1 of the law of March 21, 2018 provides that: “This section governs the installation and
the use of cameras in a visible manner by the police services”. In paragraph 2 of the same article, it is
added that “the provisions of this section are applicable to the police services when they have access in
real time to images from surveillance cameras installed by other data controllers, in application
of the law of March 21, 2007 regulating the installation and use of surveillance cameras or other laws, if this
access involves recording the images within the police services themselves. ". In chapter 3, the article
64 of the law of March 21, 2018 provides that “this law does not apply to cameras of
monitoring, the installation and use of which are regulated by or under specific legislation,
surveillance cameras in the workplace […], surveillance cameras installed and used by
public inspection and control services, expressly authorized by law, decree or ordinance, which regulates
their skills, to use cameras or to take film or video shots, as part of their
assignments”.

There is therefore no doubt that the Belgian legislator has chosen a global approach to video surveillance.
The scope of the law of March 21, 2018 concerns both the management and operation of
video surveillance by the police and by other data controllers such as municipalities.

The French legislator has also adopted a global approach since article L251-2 of the Security Code
Interior provides that “the transmission and recording of images taken on public roads by means of
video protection can be implemented by the competent public authorities”. By public authorities
competent, it is in particular the prefect as depositary of the authority of the State and considered as authority
administrative police17. They are also the police and gendarmerie forces as well as the mayors18.

Consequently, the National Commission is of the opinion that it would be appropriate to follow the example of
approaches adopted by Belgian and French legislators with regard to the legal framework for
CCTV systems installed in public spaces and places in Luxembourg for the purpose of
public security.

II. Comments on article 1 of the bill adding a new article 43bis to the amended law of July 18

2018 on the Grand Ducal Police

The CNPD recalls the case law of the European Court of Human Rights according to which the law “must define
the scope and methods of exercising the power with sufficient clarity – taking into account the legitimate aim pursued

regulating private and particular security.

5ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPDANNEXES – to provide the individual with adequate protection against arbitrariness”19. The Court of Justice of the European Union
for its part, emphasizes the importance “of providing clear and precise rules governing the scope and application of a
measure and imposing a minimum of requirements, so that the persons whose personal data
concerned have sufficient safeguards to effectively protect their data against
risks of misuse as well as against any illicit access and use of this data”.

In respect of the principles relating to the processing of personal data as provided for
by the law of 1 August 2018 on the protection of natural persons with regard to the processing of personal data
personal nature in criminal matters as well as in matters of national security, the CNPD recalls that personal data
collected by monitoring devices must have specific, explicit and legitimate purposes and must not
must not be processed in a manner incompatible with those purposes.

It notes that Article 43 bis does not expressly mention the purposes of video surveillance devices
for police purposes. Indeed, the installation of the said devices is not explicitly attached to the missions of
prevention and detection of crimes and criminal offences. This is certainly due to the fact that the article
43 bis is incorporated into the law of 18 July 2018 on the Grand Ducal Police, thus making implicit the link between the
Police missions with the installation of surveillance cameras.

It may also be worth mentioning that the laws of neighboring countries explicitly link the use of
video surveillance device for law enforcement missions22. Consequently, the CNPD considers that it would be
appropriate for the Luxembourg legislator to do the same.

- Ad paragraphs (1), (2), (3) and (4) of the new article 43bis

The CNPD wishes to recall that the installation of these video surveillance devices for police or
public security in public places must meet objective criteria23. For example, the regulation
application of August 1, 2007 previously cited provided that the establishment of security zones was intended to
purpose of the prevention, investigation and establishment of criminal offences24. The division of the zones of Luxembourg-
City placed under surveillance was therefore inextricably linked to the missions of the Grand Ducal Police. The risk
and the feeling of insecurity cannot be considered alone as objective criteria. There is reason to
define, delimit and document them in order to respect the principles of predictability and quality of the law such as
than provided for by European Union law.

On reading article 43 bis paragraph 1, the CNPD notes that the criterion of setting up the installation
of VISUPOL in the determined places is that of the “particular risk of committing crimes or misdemeanors or
threats to the safety of persons or property”. The CNPD welcomes the effort to define the said places and
risks they pose made in the bill. Indeed, these places are delimited on the basis of criteria such as

19 ECtHR, Amann c. Switzerland [GC], n°277998/95, February 16, 2000, para, 56.

and C-594/12, EU:C:201:2014:238, point 54.

The CNPD also welcomes the fact that the determination of a supervised area is conditioned by an authorization
ministerial comprising three elements. First of all, the combined reading of Article 43 bis paragraphs 3 and 4
makes it clear that the Director General of the Grand Ducal Police, as responsible for the processing
of VISUPOL, must carry out an impact analysis in accordance with article 26 of the law of August 1, 2018
on the protection of individuals with regard to the processing of personal data in
criminal matters and national security. This first step represents considerable progress
compared to the previous regulatory framework which did not provide for such a measure. Opinions are then collected
the mayor and the territorially competent State prosecutor on the basis of the impact analysis carried out. Finally, the
Director General of the Grand Ducal Police is required to communicate to the Minister "the justification of the need to
video surveillance", "the delimitation of the places to be monitored", "the number, type, location and scope of
vision of the cameras”, “an assessment of the number of people affected by the video surveillance” as well as
“the permanent nature or not of video surveillance”. These three elements are appreciated by the minister who
decides whether or not to issue the ministerial authorization to install the VISUPOL system in a given place.

The fact that the bill under consideration details the steps leading to ministerial authorization and that this
be published in the official journal complies with the principles of accessibility and transparency of the law
as well as its quality.

- Ad paragraph (5) of new article 43bis

Article 43 bis paragraph 5 provides that: “the video surveillance system takes images of persons
circulating in the field of vision of the cameras and records these images, as well as the day and time at which the
images were taken on a computer tool”29. In the same paragraph, it is added that the Grand Ducal Police
can have “use of focusing techniques and automatic detection of situations likely to
correspond to the purpose pursued”.

The CNPD considers that mentioning the techniques used allows individuals subject to this type of surveillance
to have a more precise idea of the knowledge that is extracted regarding their displacement as well as the techniques used
for these purposes.

She also welcomes the commentary to the articles relating to paragraph (5) which states that "The recording of sounds
is excluded…” as well as “The use of any other automatic detection technique such as the recognition

25 Article 43bis paragraph (12 paragraph 1° of the bill amending the amended law of 18 July 2018 on the Grand-Ducal Police.
26 Ibidem, paragraph 2°.
27 Ibidem, paragraph 3°.
28 Ibidem, paragraph 4°.
29 Ibid., paragraph (5).

52020 ANNUAL REPORT CNPDANNEXES is excluded”. Indeed, the use of facial recognition techniques in the law enforcement field,
currently under discussion at European level, is still very questionable from a protection point of view
data and privacy. The CNPD is therefore wondering whether it should not be expressly excluded in
the text of the law both the capture of sounds and the use of automatic recognition detection techniques
facial.

- Ad paragraph (6) of new article 43bis

In terms of proportionality of data processing and appropriate safeguards to safeguard the rights
and fundamental freedoms of citizens, the National Commission also congratulates the authors of the bill
for having specified that the cameras cannot film the interior of private access places, nor their entrances. THE
text also provides for the obligation to use irreversible masking techniques when the configuration
of places does not make it possible to exclude from the field of vision of the cameras entrances to private access places. If the
Grand-Ducal regulation of 1 August 2007 does not contain a similar provision, it is also true that the Police
Grand Duchy already uses irreversible masking techniques in practice within the framework of the system
Current VISUPOL.

However, reading paragraph (6) of the new article 43bis, only “images from inside places of private access,
neither specifically, those of their entries » cannot be viewed. Furthermore, the use of methods
masking is limited to “entrances to places of private access”. Conversely, the interior of a private place can be
filmed and the use of masking processes is not mandatory when the private place does not aim to access or
an entrance. The CNPD therefore considers it necessary to specify the provision under examination, in order to guarantee that neither
the interior, nor the access or the entrance of a private place can be filmed and that according to the configuration of the places,
any interior, access or entrance to a private place is technically masked in an irreversible manner.

- Ad paragraph (7) of new article 43bis

Paragraph (7) of the new draft article 43bis specifies that "the public is informed in a clear and
of the existence of the surveillance system”.

Insofar as paragraph (8) of the new article 43bis refers to the law of 1 August 2018 on the
protection of natural persons with regard to the processing of personal data in criminal matters
as well as in matters of national security, it should be noted that Article 11 paragraph (1) of this law obliges
already any data controller to take “reasonable measures to provide any information,
referred to in Article 12, ….., to the data subject in a concise, comprehensible and easily accessible manner,
in clear and simple terms. The information is provided by any appropriate means, including by
electronic”.

97

ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPD The National Commission therefore does not see any added value in the text of paragraph (7) in question
as she does not understand the use and meaning of the term “permanent” in the context of this
arrangement.

With regard to the practical implementation by the Grand Ducal Police to inform citizens in the
framework of the VISUPOL system, the CNPD strongly recommends following the guidelines drawn up by the Committee
European Data Protection, namely: “Guidelines 3/2019 on processing of personal data through
video devices, version 2.0, adopted on 29 January 2020”.

- Ad paragraph (8) of new article 43bis

Paragraph (8) provides, among other things, that “a Grand-Ducal regulation determines the technical and
organizational measures to be implemented by the data controller to ensure the security of the processing in
application of article 28 of the law of 1 August 2018 on the protection of individuals with regard to the
processing of personal data in criminal matters as well as in matters of national security and rule
the procedures for exercising the right of access provided for in Article 13 of the same law”.

The CNPD regrets that a draft Grand-Ducal regulation was not attached to the bill under consideration.
However, it already emphasizes that when drawing up the Grand-Ducal regulation, attention
Particular attention should be paid to the logging and tracing of access by members of the police. AT
As such, the CNPD recalls that it regularly recommends keeping the logs for a period of 5 years
while this period also corresponds to the limitation period in tort (e.g. breach of secrecy
professional). Note that the Grand-Ducal regulation of 1 August 2007 only provided for a retention period
3 years old.

- Ad paragraph (9) of new article 43bis

This provision regulates the retention period of images from surveillance cameras. The text under
examination does not change from the Grand-Ducal regulation of 1 August 2007, while it retains
a period of two months. The CNPD considers this period to be proportionate with regard to the purposes pursued by the
data processing.

- Ad paragraph (10) of new article 43bis

The National Commission welcomes this provision, insofar as the viewing of images
in real time is limited to only members of the Police designated for this purpose by the Director General of the Police.

European.

100

52020 ANNUAL REPORT CNPDANNEXES According to the explanatory memorandum, the purpose of the bill is to bring to the amended law of November 12, 2004
relating to the fight against money laundering and the financing of terrorism (hereinafter “the 2004 law”) the
adaptations necessary to ensure the transposition of certain provisions of Directive (EU) 2018/843 of
European Parliament and of the Council of 30 May 2018 amending Directive (EU) 2015/849 on the prevention
the use of the financial system for the purposes of money laundering or terrorist financing as well as
Directives 2009/138/EC and 2013/36/EU (hereinafter “Directive 2018/843”) and to ensure the implementation of
recommendations of the FATF, in particular in the case where these recommendations are not the subject of provisions
equivalents in Directive (EU) 2015/849 on the prevention of the use of the financial system for the purposes
money laundering or terrorist financing as well as Directives 2009/138/EC and 2013/36/
EU (hereinafter “Directive 2015/849”). The bill further seeks to supplement the powers of the authorities of
controls and self-regulatory bodies and to strengthen the cooperation of control authorities with their
foreign counterparts.

Article 5 of Directive 2015/849 allows Member States to “stop or maintain in force, in the field
governed by this Directive, stricter provisions to prevent money laundering and the financing
terrorism, within the limits of Union law”. The Court of Justice in a case concerning Article
corresponding to Directive 2005/60/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council of 26 October 2005 relating
preventing the use of the financial system for the purposes of money laundering and the financing of
terrorism, as amended, recalled that the national legislation adopted must, among other things, be proportional,
which requires an assessment “…of the degree of interference of the customer due diligence measures that it
provides with other rights and interests protected by Union law, such as the protection of personal data
provided for in Article 8 of the Charter …”30.

The CNPD recalls that the protection of personal data is a distinct and independent fundamental right
in the EU legal order, guaranteed by Article 8 of the Charter of Fundamental Rights of the European Union.
The Court of Justice has thus emphasized that an act “constitutes an interference with the fundamental right to
protection of personal data guaranteed by article 8 of the charter since it provides for the processing
personal data”. This right is not absolute and may be limited in accordance with Article 52,
paragraph 1 of the Charter, which requires that the limitation be provided for by law, respects the essential content of the right
to data protection and, in compliance with the principle of proportionality, that it is necessary and that it
effectively meets objectives of general interest recognized by the Union or the need to protect the rights and
freedoms of others.

The Court of Justice recalled in cases C-293/12 and C-594/12 Digital Rights that the fight against terrorism
international law and serious crimes constitutes a purpose of general interest32. In this regard, the CNPD subscribes to the
remarks of the European Data Protection Supervisor on the proposal for Directive 2015/849,

30 Judgment of 10 March 2016, Safe Interenvios, C-235/14, ECLI:EU:C:2016:154, point 109 and also points 94 to 110, concerning Article 5 of the 2005 Directive.
31 Judgment of 8 April 2014, Digital Rights Ireland, joined cases C-293/12 and C-594/12, ECLI:EU:C:2014:238, paragraph 36; see also Judgment of November 9, 2010,
Volker und Markus Schecke, Cases C-92/09 and C-93/09, ECLI:EU:C:2010:662, paragraph 58. See also European Data Protection Supervisor, Guide for
the assessment of the need for measures limiting the fundamental right to the protection of personal data (11 April 2017).

32 Judgment of 8 April 2014, Digital Rights Ireland, joined cases C-293/12 and C-594/12, ECLI:EU:C:2014:238, paragraphs 41-42.

Ensure transparency of sources of payments, deposits and transfers of funds in order to combat terrorism

they must ensure compliance of the provisions with the data protection regulations at
personal character. The CNPD does not know if such an assessment has been made. She finds, however, that many

natural persons, insofar as the number of processing operations carried out and the data processed both

In general, the authors of the bill should analyze the necessity and the proportionality of the proposed measures,
all the more so if the measures to be implemented in national law have not been the subject of such an assessment during the
their development.

to reiterate the remarks made in these notices, in particular with regard to the particular categories of
data, data relating to criminal convictions, offenses or security measures and
data transfers to third countries by professionals.

In this opinion, the National Commission refers to the articles of the coordinated version of the directive
2015/849.
