52020 CNPD ANNUAL REPORTANNEXES I. Regarding the retention of personal data

The draft law under opinion aims in its article 5, point 7, letter c) to modify article 3, paragraph 6, letter a) of
the 2004 law relating to the retention period of data processed by professionals by replacing the words
"may retain" with "retain".

This storage period applies according to the new paragraph 2 of paragraph 6 of article 3 of the law of 2004
also to data accessible through the centralized mechanisms referred to in Article 32bis of the
Directive (EU) 2015/849.

According to the commentary to the articles, this amendment is intended “to support the mandatory nature of the
conservation for an additional period of five years when this conservation is necessary for the
effective implementation of internal measures for the prevention or detection of acts of money laundering or financing
terrorism as provided for in Article 40, paragraph 1, subparagraph 2, of Directive (EU) 2015/849.”34.

As already raised in CNPD opinion no. 51/2018 of 21 January 2018, article 40, paragraph 1, subparagraph
2 of Directive 2015/849 sets the retention period for data collected at 5 years after the end of the relationship
business or occasional transaction. Member States may allow or require that the data
be retained for a period not exceeding five additional years, "after careful
assessed the necessity and proportionality of this prolonged storage and whether it was deemed necessary for the purposes
to prevent or detect acts of money laundering or terrorist financing or to investigate
in the matter”.

The authors of the bill did not carry out a careful analysis of the necessity and proportionality
the extension of the retention period, the CNPD is not in a position to assess the need and the
proportionality of the provision in question. It also refers to its remarks made in its opinion
n°51/2018 of January 21, 2018.

II. Regarding the rights of data subjects

The CNPD takes note of the alignment of paragraphs 3 and 4 of article 3, paragraph 6bis of the 2004 law, as
as modified by the bill, on the GDPR. She notes that the authors of the bill have clarified the scope
application of the limitation of the data subject's right of access.

Thus, in accordance with the amended article, the right of access provided for in Article 15 of the GDPR may be limited "in application
of Article 5, paragraph (5), subparagraph 1”, namely when “information is, will be or has been communicated
or provided to the authorities pursuant to paragraphs (1), (1bis), (2) and (3) [of Article 5] or that an investigation

34 Bill n°7467, Commentary on the articles, page 51.

103

ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPD of the financial intelligence unit on money laundering or terrorist financing is in progress or could
be opened” (article 5, paragraph 5, first paragraph).

The provision under review constitutes a limitation of the rights of data subjects within the meaning of Article 23
of the GDPR, according to which the scope of the right of access may be limited by the law of the Union or of a Member State,
when this limitation respects the essence of the fundamental rights and freedoms and constitutes a necessary measure
and proportionate in a democratic society to guarantee one of the objectives provided for exhaustively in Article 23,
paragraph 1 of the GDPR. Legislative measures should include specific provisions relating, at least,
the elements referred to in paragraph 2 of the article in question, including provisions relating to the wording
of the purposes or categories of purposes, the categories of personal data concerned and the scope
limitations.

For the sake of legal certainty and in order to delimit with sufficient precision the cases in which the
data subjects’ right of access could be limited, the draft law should be clarified in order to satisfy the
requirements of Article 23 of the GDPR, indicating, among other things, the precise conditions applying to the limitation
of the rights of the data subject. The CNPD refers in this respect to its opinions and to the opinions of the Council of State relating to the
bills n°7250 and n°7373. In the present case, the National Commission wonders, in particular, about the
possibility of limiting the right of access, in the event that an investigation is not opened but “could be”
by the FRC. Indeed, the provision does not specify when and how a controller-professional
may consider that an investigation could be opened by the FIU.

Following the example of its opinion n°51/2018 of January 21, 2018, the CNPD still insists on the need to provide for a right
indirect access if the data subject's right of access has been limited.

III. Regarding the cooperation between the FIU, supervisory authorities and self-regulatory bodies

To. The principle of purpose limitation

i. General considerations

In accordance with Article 8(2) of the Charter of Fundamental Rights, personal data
must be processed fairly and for specific purposes. Thus, in accordance with the purpose limitation principle,
the personal data must be collected for specified, explicit and legitimate purposes, and
must not be further processed in a manner incompatible with those purposes. This principle applies
data processing carried out by the authorities, including in the context of national cooperation and
international.

The CNPD fully subscribes to the opinion of the European Data Protection Supervisor on the proposal for
the European Commission amending Directive (EU) 2015/849 and Directive 2009/101/EC that the principle

104

5ANNUAL REPORT2020 CNPDANNEXES limitation of purposes is particularly important “with regard to the interference of public order in
the protection of personal data, because the proportionality of their processing will have to be assessed in
according to the political objective defined by the legislator.

In this regard, we consider that legislative instruments allowing multiple and/or simultaneous processing of
personal data by different data controllers and for incompatible purposes,
without specifying the purpose for which each data processing is carried out, risks causing great confusion
with regard to the application of the principle of proportionality.

In cases where the purposes of data processing are defined by general terms or
vague, or for which the various data controllers have a completely
different with the purpose pursued both in terms of the structure or resources and the capacity of each
responsible to comply with the rules in certain given circumstances, the principle of purpose limitation is
formally and substantially compromised and, therefore, the principle of proportionality will not be
no longer duly applied”35.

It is therefore necessary to indicate precisely the purposes of the processing undertaken by the authorities.

To satisfy the requirement of specificity, the purpose must be clearly and fully identified in order to determine
what processing is or is not included in the specified purpose and to allow the assessment of compliance with the law and
the application of data protection safeguards. The purposes still need to be made explicit, i.e.
i.e. clear, explained or expressed in some form to ensure that all persons
concerned have the same unequivocal understanding of the purposes of the processing, regardless of any diversity
cultural or linguistic.

Furthermore, the basis of the processing should be clear and precise and its application should be foreseeable for the
litigants, in accordance with the case law of the Court of Justice of the European Union and the European Court
human rights.37

ii. Obligation to cooperate with the FIU, supervisory authorities and self-regulatory bodies

(articles 5 and 9-1 of the 2004 law, as amended by the bill)

According to the new article 5, paragraph 1 of the 2004 law, professionals, their managers and employees
must cooperate with "the Luxembourg authorities responsible for the fight against money laundering and
financing of terrorism and self-regulatory bodies, in particular in the exercise of their
respective supervisory powers conferred by Articles 8-2 and 8-2bis”.

37 Recital 41 GDPR (“such legal basis or legislative measure should be clear and precise and its application should be foreseeable for litigants,
in accordance with the case law of the Court of Justice of the European Union (hereinafter referred to as the "Court of Justice") and the European Court of Human Rights
Humans”) and recital 33 of Directive (EU) 2016/680 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 27 April 2016 on the protection of natural persons

ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPD The CNPD wonders about the scope of this provision. Indeed, given the reference to the powers of the authorities
officials and self-regulatory bodies conferred by Articles 8-2 and 8-2bis, should it be understood that
Article 5, paragraph 1 of the 2004 law concerns only the supervisory authorities and bodies
of self-regulation?

For the sake of legal certainty, the National Commission considers it necessary to further specify
this provision, in particular by specifying the entities covered by the concept of “Luxembourg authorities
responsible for the fight against money laundering and the financing of terrorism”. Furthermore and in
taking into account the above concerning the limitation of purposes, the CNPD considers it necessary to delete
the words "in particular" and to specify the framework in which professionals must cooperate with the authorities
in question.

In addition, it is worth recalling in this respect Article 5, paragraph 2 of the 2004 law, which specifies that “the
information and documents provided to the authorities, other than the judicial authorities, pursuant to paragraphs
(1) and (1bis) may only be used for the purposes of combating money laundering or the financing of
of terrorism. » In accordance with the modification of article 5, paragraph 1, paragraph 2 should be modified
to include self-regulatory bodies.

With regard to article 9-1 of the law of 2004, as it results from the bill, it provides that the FIU, the
supervisory authorities and self-regulatory bodies cooperate closely with each other. As for the form and the
purposes of the cooperation, paragraph 2 specifies that “the supervisory authorities and the financial intelligence unit
are authorized to exchange the information necessary for the accomplishment of their respective missions in
in the context of the fight against money laundering and the financing of terrorism. The supervisory authorities and the
financial intelligence unit use the information exchanged only for the accomplishment of these
assignments. »

No information is provided either on the form or on the purposes of the cooperation between the FIU, the authorities.
regulators and self-regulatory bodies. Specifically, the bill does not indicate whether organizations
self-regulatory bodies may exchange personal data with the FIU and supervisory authorities. In
taking into account paragraph 2 of article 9-1 cited above, it therefore seems that there will be no exchange of data
between the self-regulatory bodies, on the one hand, and the FIU and the supervisory authorities, on the other hand.

If, despite this, the self-regulatory organizations could be required to exchange data with the
CRF and the supervisory authorities, the Council of State has repeatedly emphasized that the communication of data
to third parties may constitute an interference with privacy38 and thus constitutes under Article 11, paragraph
3, of the Constitution, a matter reserved for formal law. It is therefore necessary that the essential points be fixed
by the law.

38 Opinion of the Council of State of 21 November 2017 relating to bill no. 7182, doc. speak. 7182/02, page 7. Opinion of the Council of State of June 7, 2016 relating to the draft

law n°6975, page 4.

106

5ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPDANNEXES Thus, taking into account the above and if the FIU, the supervisory authorities and the self-regulatory bodies
would have to exchange personal data, the draft law should regulate the cooperation of
all controllers in question, specifying the form and purpose of this cooperation for all
controllers, including self-regulatory bodies.

iii. National cooperation between the CSSF and the CAA acting for the purposes of the fight against money laundering and
against the financing of terrorism and for the purposes of the prudential supervision of
credit and financial institutions or the supervision of financial markets (article 9-1bis of
2004 Act, as amended by the Bill)

Article 1, paragraph 37 of Directive 2018/843 introduces specific provisions concerning the exchange
information between competent authorities by inserting a new article 57a in Directive 2015/849.
In accordance with this article, the competent authorities responsible for the supervision of credit institutions
and financial institutions within a Member State and in different Member States in accordance with the
Directive or other legislative acts relating to the supervision of credit institutions and institutions
financial institutions can exchange data.

Paragraph 3 of Article 57a of Directive 2015/849 lists the purposes for which personal data
exchanged can be used. These include, among other things, “…the fulfillment of the duties incumbent on them
pursuant to this Directive or other legislative acts in the field of combating money laundering
capital and the financing of terrorism, prudential regulation and supervision of institutions
credit institutions and financial institutions, including the imposition of sanctions” and “in the context of procedures
judicial proceedings initiated under special provisions provided for by Union law in the field of
this Directive or in that of the prudential regulation and supervision of credit institutions
and financial institutions. ".

According to the commentary to the articles of the bill, article 57bis of directive 2015/849 would be transposed by
Article 9-1bis of the 2004 law, as amended by the draft law, according to which the CSSF and the CAA may
exchange data “(…) for the purposes of the fight against money laundering and the financing of terrorism as well as
for the purposes of other legislative acts relating to the regulation and prudential supervision of institutions
of credit and financial institutions or relating to the supervision of the financial markets”. Like the
Directive, paragraph 3 sets out the purposes for which data may be exchanged, with reference
also to “…other legislative acts in the field of…regulation and prudential supervision
credit institutions and financial institutions as well as financial market supervision. ".

The commentary to the articles specifies that "[t]his new article takes into account the fact that information of a
prudential information relating to credit institutions and financial institutions, such as data relating

39 Article 9-1bis, paragraph 1 of the 2004 law, as amended by the bill.

107

CNPD 2020 ANNUAL REPORT to the good repute of directors and shareholders, internal control mechanisms, governance or
compliance and risk management, are often essential for the proper supervision of these institutions
in terms of the fight against money laundering and the financing of terrorism. Likewise, the information
on the fight against money laundering and the financing of terrorism are also important for the supervision
prudential of these institutions. It is with this in mind that Directive 2018/843 aims to provide a legal framework
by expanding the exchange of confidential information and cooperation between competent authorities in matters of
the fight against money laundering and the financing of terrorism as well as in terms of the prudential supervision of
credit institutions and financial institutions. In all cases, the information exchanged must
present a relevant element in the fight against money laundering and the financing of terrorism. »40

In view of the foregoing and taking into account the principle of purpose limitation, the National Commission
considers that the purpose as currently drafted, namely “(…) for the purposes of other legislative acts relating to
the regulation and prudential supervision of credit institutions and financial institutions
or relating to the supervision of the financial markets" is formulated too vaguely. She therefore considers
necessary to specify the legislative acts on the basis of which the CSSF and the CAA could exchange data
personal character. Furthermore, in order to ensure the principle of purpose limitation and to avoid any ambiguity,
the clarification in the commentary of the articles that the information exchanged must be necessary in terms of
fight against money laundering and the financing of terrorism should appear in the body of the text itself.

iv. International cooperation of supervisory authorities with their foreign counterpart authorities
(article 9-2bis of the 2004 law, as amended by the bill) and international cooperation
of the CSSF and the CAA with their foreign counterpart authorities (article 9-2quater of the law of 2004,
as amended by the bill)

Articles 9-2bis and 9-2ter of the 2004 law, as amended by the bill, aim to establish a framework
legal framework for the cooperation of the supervisory authorities with their foreign counterpart authorities (article 9-2bis),
respectively for the cooperation of the CSSF and the CAA with their foreign counterpart authorities acting in the
purposes of the fight against money laundering and terrorist financing and for the purposes of prudential supervision
credit institutions and financial institutions or financial market supervision (Article
9-2quater).

The CNPD notes that the bill under opinion does not distinguish between European cooperation and cooperation
international cooperation with the authorities of third countries, as is the case in Directive 2015/849. In fact, the guideline
provides a framework for cooperation between the authorities of the Member States, for example by determining the cases in which the
national authorities may reject a request for assistance. The conditions surrounding European cooperation
are not imposed by Directive 2015/849 for cooperation with authorities with third countries, but
are introduced by the bill under opinion in order to regulate cooperation with the authorities of third countries. So,

40 AProjet de loi n°7467, Commentaries on the articles, pages 65-66.

108

5ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPDANNEXES national authorities could in future only reject requests from the authorities of third countries in accordance
the criteria provided for in Article 9-2ter. The National Commission notes that a request for cooperation could
be refused, if "the requesting counterpart authority is not able to effectively protect this information"
(Articles 9-2ter, letter b) and 9-2quater, paragraph 4 of the 2004 law, as amended by the bill). In order to
ensure data protection during cooperation, the information protection assessment should
also include an assessment of the level of protection of personal data.

With regard to the purposes of the exchanges, the CNPD refers to its observations made in point III.a.i. and iii. this-
above concerning the precision of the purposes. In addition, paragraph 6 of article 9-2bis and paragraph 6 of
article 9-2quater of the law of 2004, as amended by the bill, restricts the use of data received
by national authorities by foreign authorities. Insofar as the list of purposes constitutes a list
limiting, the words “in particular” should be deleted from the provision.

In addition, the National Commission wonders about the scope of this cooperation, among other things with regard to
the data that can be transmitted. For example, the authorities must transmit "any information required"
(articles 9-2bis, paragraph 2, first paragraph and 9-2quater, paragraph 2, first paragraph of the law of 2004, as amended
by the bill). Paragraph 9 of the new article 9-2quater of the 2004 law provides a list of "types
of information” that may be exchanged by the CSSF and the CAA in the context of international cooperation with their
foreign counterpart authorities, in particular counterpart authorities sharing a common responsibility vis-à-vis
vis-à-vis credit institutions or financial institutions operating within the same group.

In addition, the authorities must take “without delay the necessary measures to collect the information
solicited by making use of the powers available to it by virtue of the powers conferred on it by law.
(articles 9-2bis, paragraph 2, subparagraph 3 and 9-2quater, paragraph 2, subparagraph 2 of the law of 2004, as amended
by the bill). It is not clear under which law the authorities could collect the requested information.

Also taking into account that the purposes for which the data could be exchanged
are not indicated with sufficient precision (see above), the National Commission considers that the
wording used in article 9-2bis and 9-2quater is too vague and considers it necessary to provide more
the cooperation of the authorities, in particular by setting more precisely the data that could be exchanged,
the sources of the data as well as the guarantees surrounding these exchanges. We should also replace the word
"required" by the word "necessary".

b. Confidentiality of data exchanged by supervisory authorities

As for confidentiality, the CNPD notes that articles 9-2bis, paragraph 7 and 9-2quater, paragraph 8 of the law
of 2004, as amended by the bill, provide that the authorities “ensure a degree of confidentiality

109

ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPD appropriate to any request for cooperation and to the information exchanged, so as to protect the integrity of the
investigations or searches for information, in compliance with the obligations of both parties in terms of compliance
privacy and data protection. ". The CNPD recalls that the authorities must, in any event,
act in accordance with data protection and privacy legislation and that the assurance of a
"appropriate degree of confidentiality" can in no way lead to a reduction in the level of protection offered
by data protection legislation.

vs. Data transfers to third countries

The CNPD recalls that any transfer of personal data by the FIU, the supervisory authorities and the
self-regulatory organizations to third countries, i.e. outside the European Economic Area, must be
in accordance with chapter 3 of the law of 1 August 2018 on the protection of natural persons with regard to
the processing of personal data in criminal matters as well as in matters of national security,
respectively in Chapter V of the GDPR.

As for the GDPR, it follows from Article 44 of the GDPR that all the provisions of Chapter V must be applied.
in such a way that the level of protection of natural persons guaranteed by the GDPR is not compromised.
Thus, in the absence of an adequacy decision within the meaning of Article 45 of the GDPR or of guarantees appropriate to the
meaning of Article 46 of the GDPR, a transfer or a set of transfers of personal data to a
third country or an international organization can only take place in the cases exhaustively listed by
GDPR Article 49. Regarding the use of Article 49 of the GDPR, the CNPD refers to the guidelines
2/2018 of the European Data Protection Board 2/2018 relating to the derogations provided for in Article 49
of Regulation (EU) 2016/679. Like these guidelines, the CNPD would like to point out that these exemptions
“must be interpreted in such a way as not to contradict the very nature of derogations, which are exceptions
to the rule that personal data cannot be transferred to a third country unless
that country offers an adequate level of data protection or that appropriate safeguards are put in place
spot »41.

IV. As to the powers of supervisory authorities and self-regulatory bodies

The National Commission has already had the opportunity to advise the powers of the supervisory authorities in its opinion
n°51/2018 of January 21, 2018.

It notes that section 8-2bis of the 2004 law, as amended by the bill, aims to grant new
powers to the competent bodies of self-regulatory organizations, for example the right to have access to any
document in any form whatsoever and to receive or make a copy thereof and the right to demand communication
telephone records, electronic communications or data records relating to

41 Guidelines 2/2018 on derogations provided for in Article 49 of Regulation (EU) 2016/679 of the European Data Protection Board, p. 5.

110

5ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPDANNEXES held by persons subject to their respective supervisory powers42. According to the comment
of the articles, the articles seek to align the supervisory powers of the self-regulatory organizations with those of the
supervisory authorities43.

According to Article 48, paragraphs 2 and 9 of Directive 2015/849, when self-regulatory organizations are
vested with the supervisory mission provided for in paragraph 1 of that Article, the Member States must ensure that
that they have “appropriate powers, including the power to require the production of any relevant information
to monitor compliance with obligations and the power to carry out verifications…”. Article 61 of the Directive
provides that competent authorities and self-regulatory bodies must put in place mechanisms
of “whistleblowing”. However, according to Article 60 of Directive 2015/849, only the competent authorities must
publish decisions that have not been the subject of any appeal and instituting a sanction or an administrative measure
due to an infringement.

The CNPD therefore questions the proportionality and the need for the introduction of such powers and missions to
self-regulatory bodies. Since Article 48(3) of Directive 2015/849 provides
that “Competent authorities have enhanced supervisory powers with regard to
credit institutions, financial institutions and gambling service providers and
by chance. », it seems that it is not envisaged that all the competent authorities and organizations
of self-regulation have identical powers. The CNPD therefore recommends that the authors of the draft
law to analyze whether it is necessary and proportional to grant self-regulatory bodies powers
identical to those conferred on the supervisory authorities.

Thus decided in Esch-sur-Alzette on February 28, 2020.

the protection of individuals with regard to the processing of personal data and the freedom

(hereinafter “the GDPR”), to which article 7 of the law of 1 August 2018 on the organization of the Commission refers
national data protection system and the general data protection regime, the Commission
Commission for Data Protection (hereinafter referred to as “the National Commission” or “the CNPD”)
“advises, in accordance with the law of the Member State, the national parliament, the government and other institutions
and organizations regarding legislative and administrative measures relating to the protection of the rights and freedoms of
natural persons with regard to the processing”.

By letter dated October 11, 2019, the Minister of Finance invited the National Commission to
to decide on the government amendments to bill n°7216B establishing a register of trusts
and transposing Article 31 of Directive (EU) 2015/849 of the European Parliament and of the Council of

112

52020 ANNUAL REPORT CNPDANNEXES or financing of terrorism, amending Regulation (EU) No 648/2012 of the European Parliament and of the Council
and repealing Directive 2005/60/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council and Directive 2006/70/EC of the
Commission (hereinafter "the bill").

Part of Article 31 of Directive (EU) 2015/849 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 20 May 2015
on the prevention of the use of the financial system for the purposes of money laundering or financing
terrorism (hereinafter "Directive 2015/849"), as amended by Directive (EU) 2018/843 of the Parliament
European Parliament and of the Council of 30 May 2018 amending Directive (EU) 2015/849 on the prevention of the use
of the financial system for the purposes of money laundering or terrorist financing as well as the directives
2009/138/EC and 2013/36/EU (hereinafter “Directive 2018/843”) was transposed by the law of August 10, 2018
relating to the information to be obtained and kept by trustees (hereinafter “the law of 10 August 2018”). Of the
therefore, in order not to have two separate laws at national level transposing the same article of the directive
2015/849, the authors propose to repeal the law of August 10, 2018 and to insert the provisions previously contained
in the law of August 10, 2018 in this bill while ensuring that the changes made by the directive
2018/843 to these provisions are reflected in this draft law n°7216B. The authors also aim
to take into account the principles established by recommendation 25 of the Financial Action Task Force (hereafter “FATF”).

The bill therefore has two parts. First, the bill provides for the obligation for trustees and
trustees to obtain and retain data relating to beneficial owners and other persons
specified in the bill. Second, the bill seeks to establish a register of trusts and trusts
(hereinafter referred to as “the register”) kept by the Registration, Domains and VAT Authority (hereinafter
referred to as “the AED”) in which the fiduciaries and trustees will have to register certain data that they are
obliged to collect under the draft law.

The CNPD has already had the opportunity to rule on the transposition of directives in the fight against
money laundering and against the financing of terrorism in its opinions n°51/2018 of January 21, 2018, n°485/2018 of
November 22, 2018 and n°9/2019 of January 17, 2019.

This opinion of the National Commission will take into account the government amendments based on
the numbering of the coordinated text. It also refers to the articles of the coordinated version of the directive
2015/849.

As a preliminary point, the CNPD notes that the draft law uses the concept of "person concerned" in the context of
information relating to natural persons and legal persons to be entered in the register. In order to
to avoid any confusion with the notion of “data subject” relative to a “natural person”
subject to data protection regulations, the CNPD recommends that the authors of the project
of law to use different terminology with respect to “legal persons”.

2020 CNPD ANNUAL REPORT I. Regarding data processed by trustees and fiduciaries (articles 2 and 3)

The collection of data by trustees and fiduciaries is mainly governed by Articles 2 and 3 of the draft
law. However, these articles do not clearly define the data to be collected by fiduciaries and trustees.

Article 31, paragraph 1, subparagraph 2 of Directive 2015/849 concerning the data to be collected by
fiduciaries and trustees provides that each Member State requires that the “fiduciaries/trustees of any trust
trust/of any express trust administered in that Member State obtain and retain information
adequate, accurate and current on the beneficial owners of the fiduciary/trust. These informations
include the identity of (a) the settlor(s), (b) the trustee(s), (c) the
protectors (if any), (d) beneficiaries or class of beneficiaries, and (e) any other
natural person exercising effective control over the fiduciary/trust. ". This information includes, at least, the
name, month and year of birth, country of residence and nationality of the beneficial owner, as well as the
nature and extent of the effective interests held (article 31, paragraph 4, subparagraph 2 of directive 2015/849).
 
By transposing this article, article 2, paragraph 1 of the bill requires trustees and fiduciaries to obtain
and to maintain "information" on the beneficial owners of any express trust administered in Luxembourg or
of any trust for which they act as trustee or fiduciary. This information “includes”
the identity of the settlor(s), trustee(s), protector(s) (if any),
beneficiaries or category of beneficiaries as well as any other natural person exercising effective control
on the trust or fiduciary. While the Bill and the Commentary to the Articles does not refer to it, the Commentary
of the articles of the bill n°7216 specified that "the information to be obtained and kept includes the information
to be registered in the Register of Trusts in accordance with Article 14, paragraph 2, of the draft law”44.

In addition, trustees and fiduciaries must transmit to professionals "data relating to the assets of the
trust and the assets of trusts held or managed within the framework of the business relationship” (Article 6, paragraph 2 of the
law Project). The commentary to the articles does not explain which types of data are covered by this obligation.

Furthermore, according to Article 8, paragraphs 5 and 6 of the draft law, fiduciaries and trustees, respectively
professionals, provide, upon request, "any information they hold about any fiduciary or any trust"
to the persons referred to in Article 1, paragraph 1, point 1, letters a) to c) and point 2 of the bill, namely the
state attorney general, state prosecutors and members of their public prosecutor's offices, investigating judges, the
Financial Intelligence Unit (hereinafter “the FIU”) and supervisory authorities.

In addition to the data indicated above, it seems that trustees and fiduciaries must also collect and,
if necessary, send to the AED supporting documents (whatever they are) to which the bill makes
reference in its article 18.

44 Bill no. 7216, doc. parl no. 7216/00, p. 11.

114

52020 ANNUAL REPORT CNPDANNEXES Article 3, paragraph 1, provides that “the trustees of express trusts administered in Luxembourg and the
trustees obtain and maintain basic information about other regulated agents and service providers
trust or fiduciary services, including investment advisers or investment managers,
accountants and tax advisers. ". The persons concerned are obliged to provide the trustees and fiduciaries
all the information necessary for them to meet their obligations under this
paragraph 1 (paragraph 2).

The CNPD first notes that article 3 of the bill, unlike article 2, does not appear in article
31 of Directive 2015/849, but is taken exclusively from FATF Recommendation 25. The authors of
amendments that do not provide explanations as to a possible analysis of the need and
the proportionality that would have been carried out regarding such an obligation, so that the CNPD is not in
able to analyze the compliance of such a provision with the regulations on the protection of
data. However, it wishes to point out that the wording that trustees and fiduciaries must
collect information relating to “other regulated agents and service providers of the trust or
the trust, including investment advisers or investment managers, accountants
and tax advisers” is not worded precisely enough to allow trustees,
the trustees and the persons referred to in the article to identify with precision the persons concerned concerned.
In addition, the article provides that trustees and fiduciaries must collect "basic information",
without specifying this “elementary information”. Moreover, contrary to Article 2, paragraph
2, Article 3 does not require that the data be “adequate”, which contributes to the vagueness of this
arrangement.

Article 5, paragraph 1, letter (c) of the GDPR states that only adequate, relevant and necessary data
with regard to the purposes pursued by the controller must be collected (principle of minimization
Datas). In order to prevent indiscriminate and arbitrary data collection, it would be appropriate to analyze the
necessity and proportionality of the data to be processed by trustees and fiduciaries in the context
of this Bill, including the necessity and proportionality of Article 3 of the Bill. Furthermore, to
following the example of the recommendation of the European Data Protection Supervisor (hereinafter "the EDPS") issued
in its opinion on draft directive no. 2015/84945, the CNPD considers it necessary to amend the bill in order to
to indicate therein the categories of data, which must be obtained and kept by trustees and fiduciaries.
If the authors of the bill consider it necessary to impose a collection of supporting documents, they should
also specify the supporting documents in question.

The National Commission also notes that the notions of “category of beneficial owners” and “scope
effective interests held" are not defined in the draft law. In order to clearly define the information
that must be kept and thus respect the principle of data minimization, it therefore suggests
clarify these concepts.

45 Opinion of the European Data Protection Supervisor of 4 July 2013 on a proposal for a directive of the European Parliament and of the Council on the

ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPD II. As for the transmission of data held by fiduciaries and trustees to national authorities,

self-regulatory bodies and professionals (Articles 5 and 6)

Article 5, paragraph 1 and 2 of the draft law stipulates that fiduciaries and trustees must provide the authorities
authorities for the purposes of their missions and to self-regulatory organizations for the purposes of their missions under
of this law and of the amended law of 12 November 2004 relating to the fight against money laundering and
financing of terrorism, on request, "the information referred to in Articles 2 and 3, as well as, where appropriate,
the unique registration number referred to in Article 13(3) or a certificate providing proof of
registration in an equivalent register set up by another Member State or an extract from the
beneficial ownership information maintained in such a register”. The "national authorities" are all
the authorities, administrations and entities indicated in Article 1, paragraph 1, point 1 of the draft law.

This article aims to transpose Article 31(3) of Directive 2015/84946, which provides that “States
Member States require competent authorities and FIUs to have timely access to the information referred to
in paragraph 1”.

As for the purposes of access to data, the commentary to the articles of the bill explains that this paragraph
aims not only to transpose Directive 2018/843, but also Article 22, paragraph 1a of Directive
2011/16/EU of the Council of 15 February 2011 relating to administrative cooperation in the field of taxation47.

In order to ensure compliance with data protection legislation, the text of the draft law should
specify the missions of the authorities that can access the data and clearly identify the purposes of the access.

Indeed, Article 8(2) of the Charter of Fundamental Rights requires that personal data
be processed fairly and for specific purposes. Thus, in accordance with the principle of purpose limitation,
the personal data must be collected for specified, explicit and legitimate purposes, and
must not be processed in a manner incompatible with those purposes.

To satisfy the requirement of specificity, the purpose must be clearly and fully identified in order to determine
what processing is or is not included in the specified purpose and allow the assessment of compliance with the law and
the application of data protection safeguards48. The purposes still need to be made explicit, i.e.
i.e. clear, explained or expressed in some form to ensure that all persons
concerned have the same unequivocal understanding of the purposes of the processing, regardless of any diversity
cultural or linguistic.

In this regard, the CNPD agrees with the opinion of the EDPS on the European Commission's proposal amending the
Directive (EU) 2015/849 and Directive 2009/101/EC, according to which “In this respect, we consider that

46 Bill n°7216B, doc. parl n°7216B/03, Commentary on the articles, p. 11.
47 Bill n°7216B, doc. parl n°7216B/03, Commentary on the articles, p. 11.
48 Article 29 Working Party, Opinion 03/2013 on purpose limitation (WP203), page 39.
49 Article 29 Working Party, Opinion 03/2013 on purpose limitation (WP203), page 39.

52020 ANNUAL REPORT CNPDANNEXES Legislative instruments allowing multiple and/or simultaneous processing of personal data by
different data controllers and for incompatible purposes, without specifying the purpose for which each
data processing is carried out, is likely to cause considerable confusion as regards the application of the
principle of proportionality.

In cases where the purposes of data processing are defined by general terms or
vague, or for which the various data controllers have a completely
different with the purpose pursued both in terms of the structure or resources and the capacity of each
responsible to comply with the rules in certain given circumstances, the principle of purpose limitation is
formally and substantially compromised and, therefore, the principle of proportionality will not be
no longer duly applied”.

Thus, in order to respect the principle of purpose limitation, the bill should specify the purposes for
which national authorities can receive data.

In addition, the CNPD can only agree with the opinion of the Council of State relating to Article 1 of Bill No. 7216A
concerning “national authorities”, according to which the authors of the bill should restrict access
to “judicial police officers approved by the Director General of the Grand Ducal Police or the Director of
the Customs and Excise Administration” and should delete point h), namely the Customs Administration
and excise.
 
As for the form of the request, namely "on request", it is also useful to recall that the public authorities,
such as tax and customs authorities, financial investigation units, administrative authorities
independent financial market authorities or authorities responsible for the regulation and supervision of
securities markets, which are likely to receive communication of personal data
in the context of a specific fact-finding mission in accordance with Union law or the law of a Member State
are not considered recipients. Requests for communication sent by the authorities
public should always be presented in writing, be reasoned and be of an occasional nature, and they should not
should not relate to the entirety of a file nor lead to the interconnection of files and the processing of data
by the public authorities in question must comply with the applicable rules on the protection of
data according to the purposes of the processing.

As for professionals, the bill requires trustees and fiduciaries to provide
professionals, upon request, for the sole purpose of implementing their duty of care arising
of the amended law of 12 November 2004 "information on the assets of the trust and the assets of
trusts held or managed within the framework of the business relationship” (article 6, paragraph 2 of the bill).
According to the commentary to the articles, this provision ensures the implementation of the requirements arising

III. With regard to Article 8, paragraphs 5 and 6

In addition to the obligation provided for in Article 5 of the draft law, Article 8, paragraphs 5 and 6 of the draft law
law obliges fiduciaries and trustees, respectively, professionals to provide, upon request, any information
that they hold on any trust or any trust to the persons referred to in Article 1, paragraph 1, point 1,
letters a) to c) and point 2 of the bill, namely the State Attorney General, the State Prosecutors as well as
the members of their public prosecutor's offices, the investigating judges, the FIU and the supervisory authorities. Being
given that article 5 of the draft law obliges trustees and fiduciaries to transmit information relating to
to any trust and any express trust, the provision under review seems to relate to any trust and not only
express trusts.

Apart from the mention that the provisions ensure the implementation of the requirements resulting from the interpretative note
25, point 4 of the FATF, the commentary to the articles does not provide any explanation of the necessity or proportionality
of such an additional obligation. It follows from Article 31(3) of Directive 2015/849 that the
competent authorities and FIUs must have timely access to the information referred to in paragraphs
1 of Section 31, namely to information collected by "the trustees of any express trust/of any
express trust”. Furthermore, the provisions do not mention the purposes for which the persons indicated
in article 1, paragraph 1, point 1, letters a) to c) and point 2 of the bill could receive the data. there
adds that any information about any fiduciary or trust could be requested. Is this the data referred to in
Article 14 of the bill or other data? Furthermore, data controllers who must
transmit the information to the persons indicated in Article 1, paragraph 1, point 1, letters a) to c) and point
2 of the bill are not precisely defined, so that the scope of this provision is
uncertain and vague.

IV. As for the register (chapters 3 to 5)

To. Regarding roles and responsibilities (Articles 16 to 18)

The CNPD notes that the AED should be considered as the data controller within the meaning of the GDPR (article 16,
paragraph 1 of the bill) for the register of trusts and trusts, the purposes of which are the conservation and
the provision of the information referred to in Article 14 (Article 12 of the draft law). The Technology Center
and information of the State would be considered as the processor within the meaning of the GDPR (article 16, paragraph 5
Bill).

51 Bill n°7216B, doc. parl n°7216B/03, Commentary on the articles, p. 12.
52 Bill no. 7216B, doc. parl n°7216B/03, Commentary on the articles, p. 12.

118

5ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPDANNEXES The draft law specifies that the AED is not responsible for the content of the information entered (article 16, paragraph
3 of the bill), but that it can refuse an incomplete registration or one that does not comply with the legal provisions
or regulations (section 18 of the bill).

The CNPD wonders on the basis of which documents or data the AED would verify the conformity of the registration.
No details are provided either in the bill or in the commentary to the articles.

In the event that it is envisaged that the AED consults third-party databases to verify the accuracy of the
data, such consultations should be provided for by law, which specifies the databases to be consulted, the
terms of consultation and security measures.

As for Article 18, paragraph 2 of the draft law, it provides that in the event of refusal of an application for registration,
the applicant must submit the required supporting documents, without however indicating the supporting documents
could be requested.

The National Commission questions the necessity and proportionality of the transmission of supporting documents
to the AED and the preservation of these documents by the latter. Directive 2015/849 makes no mention of parts
supporting documents as part of the information contained in the register. Furthermore, as mentioned above,
section 2 of the bill requires fiduciaries and trustees to obtain and maintain information relating to
to the beneficial owners and Article 5 obliges them to transmit this information to the national authorities and to the
self-regulatory bodies, upon request.

The CNPD is therefore wondering whether it is necessary for supporting documents to be sent to the AED and
kept by the latter. Notwithstanding this, to the extent that the trust or fiduciary should convey
supporting documents to the AED, the nature or categories of these documents should be indicated in a legal text.
In the absence of such an indication, the controllers would risk transmitting information that is not
relevant.

b. As for access to the register by national authorities, self-regulatory bodies
    and professionals (articles 25 and 26)

As for the purposes of access to the register, it appears from article 25 of the bill under opinion that the authorities
national authorities have access to the information referred to in article 14 of the bill “in the exercise of their missions”
without these missions being specified. Article 26 specifies that self-regulatory organizations will be able to access
to data “in the exercise of their supervisory mission in the fight against money laundering and
the financing of terrorism”.

ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPD Following the example of its remarks made in point III, the CNPD considers it necessary to specify the missions of the authorities
national purposes for which they can access the data held in the register, in order to circumscribe
clearly the access to this data and the use that can be made of it.

As for accessible data, the bill uses the same wording to regulate access to the data.
register by national authorities, self-regulatory bodies and professionals. Thus, these
“have access to the information referred to in Article 14 registered in the Register of Trusts and Trusts”53. These
same data will, in future, also be available through the system of interconnection of
national registers, in accordance with the national legislation of the Member State implementing the provisions
of Directive 2015/849 (Article 31(9) of Directive 2015/849). Article 33, paragraph 2 of the draft
of law, which transposes the said provision of the directive, thus indicates that the data referred to in its article 14 will be
available “in accordance with the terms of access provided for by this law and the measures taken for its
execution ".

The CNPD wonders whether the wording of Articles 25, paragraph 1 and 26, paragraph 1 and 2 implies
that the modalities of access to the register, including the accessible data and the search criteria,
will be the same for national authorities, self-regulatory bodies and professionals. By
elsewhere, is it intended to allow national authorities, self-regulatory bodies and
professionals to access the data stored in the register in accordance with Article 20 of the draft
law ? However, under the law of 13 January 2019, self-regulatory organizations and professionals cannot
can access only a limited selection of the data recorded in the register of beneficial owners at the
time of the consultation, namely surname, first name(s), nationality(ies), day of birth,
month of birth, year of birth, place of birth, country of residence, nature of interests
number of employees held and the extent of the effective interests held (article 12 of the law of January 13, 2019). Moreover,
the search criteria are not identical for professionals and self-regulatory bodies,
on the one hand, and national authorities on the other. Since any processing of data must be limited
to what is strictly necessary and also taking into account Article 31, paragraph 9, subparagraph 3 of the
Directive 2015/849, which explicitly requires that access via the system of interconnection of
registers to the information entered in the register must be done "in compliance with the rules on
of data protection", it raises, among other things, the question of the need for professionals
to have access to all the data entered in the register within the framework of the bill under opinion.
In view of the foregoing, the CNPD considers that the current wording of the bill allows too much access
broad in the register and considers it necessary to specify in the text of the bill the terms of access to the register, including
including accessible data and search criteria, in compliance with data protection rules
data, in particular by limiting access to only necessary data. Concerning the
professionals, it is at most data considered to be necessary under the law of the
January 13, 2019.

53 Article 25, paragraph 1 of the draft law with regard to national authorities, Article 26, paragraph 1 of the draft law with regard to bodies

of self-regulation and Article 26, paragraph 2 of the draft law with regard to professionals.

120

52020 CNPD ANNUAL REPORTANNEXES Finally, the National Commission notes that the terms of access for national authorities will be set by
grand-ducal regulation. It regrets that the draft Grand-Ducal regulation does not accompany the bill, which
which would have enabled him to analyze the entire future legal framework.

vs. Regarding access by the general public on the basis of legitimate interest (Article 27)

Article 27 of the draft law grants access to certain data entered in the register “on the basis of a
decision on a case-by-case basis by the director of the AED or his delegate, to any natural or legal person who demonstrates
a legitimate interest in the context of preventing the use of the financial system for the purposes of money laundering or
financing of terrorism”. This provision aims to transpose Article 31(4) of Directive 2015/849.
In this regard, recital 28 of Directive 2018/843 should be recalled, which specifies that "When they set
the level of transparency of information on the beneficial owners of these trusts/trusts or constructions
similar legal provisions, Member States should take due account of the protection of fundamental rights
individuals, in particular the right to privacy and the right to the protection of personal data. ".

The collection and provision of data of the persons concerned, namely the beneficial owners of the
trusts and trusts, constitutes an interference with the fundamental rights to respect for private and family life
and the protection of personal data enshrined in Articles 7 and 8 of the Charter of Rights
fundamental principles (hereinafter "the Charter"). In accordance with Article 52, paragraph 1 of the Charter, the exercise of these
rights may be limited, provided that:

• the interference is prescribed by law;
• the interference respects the essential content of the rights and freedoms;
• the interference is necessary, subject to the principle of proportionality; And
• the interference effectively meets objectives of general interest recognized by the Union or the need for protection

rights and freedoms of others.

In order to be proportional, the legislator must ensure that the acts are “suitable for achieving the legitimate objectives
pursued by the regulations in question and do not exceed the limits of what is appropriate and necessary to
the achievement of these objectives.

As regards the balance between transparency and the protection of personal data, the Court
of Justice ruled that "institutions are required to weigh up, before disclosing information
concerning a natural person, the interest of the Union in guaranteeing the transparency of its actions and the infringement of
rights recognized by sections 7 and 8 of the Charter. No automatic pre-eminence can be recognized in
the objective of transparency on the right to the protection of personal data […], even if interests
important economic issues are at stake.

54 Judgment of 8 April 2014, Digital Rights Ireland, C-293/12 and C-594/12, EU:C:2014:238, point 46 and the case law cited therein.
55 Judgment of 9 November 2010, Volker und Markus Schecke, C-92/09 and C-93/09, EU:C:2010:662, paragraph 85.

ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPD Consequently, the legislator should be able to demonstrate that the derogations and limitations are limited
strictly necessary.

i. Accessible data

These people could access the surname, first names, nationalities, month and year of birth,
country of residence as well as the nature of the involvement of the person concerned in the fiduciary or in the trust
and the extent of the effective interests held (Article 27, paragraph 1 of the draft law). The CNPD welcomes the choice of
authors of the bill to limit access only to the data required by article 31, paragraph 4, subparagraph 2 of
Directive 2015/849.

ii. The requirement of legitimate interest

As for the condition of legitimate interest, the draft law specifies in its article 27, paragraph 2, subparagraph 4 that
the applicant must attach “any document likely to justify the existence of a legitimate interest”. Furthermore, the
paragraph 3 states that “for the purpose of assessing the existence of a legitimate interest, the director of the EDA or his
delegate takes into account any relevant circumstances that may indicate whether access to information is requested
with a view to contributing to the detection or prosecution of violations of the legislation relating to the fight against
money laundering and against the financing of terrorism”.

In addition, the director of the AED or his delegate takes into account the protection of the fundamental rights of persons,
in particular the right to privacy and the right to the protection of personal data when it is taken
of decision”.

It appears from the commentary to the articles that the director of the AED or his delegate can base his decision on all
the facts relating to the request submitted to him, including those which would not have been raised by the person
natural or legal plaintiff56.

According to recital 28 of Directive 2018/843, “the criteria and conditions for granting access to requests
information on the beneficial owners of trusts/trusts and similar legal arrangements should be
be sufficiently precise and consistent with the objectives of this Directive”. Thus, "Member States should
define legitimate interest in their national law, both as a general concept and as a determining criterion
access to information on beneficial owners” (recital 42 of Directive 2018/843).
  
Thus, in order to satisfy the requirement of foreseeability, the law should include objective criteria making it possible to
define the notion of “legitimate interest” in a precise and unequivocal manner57 and the criteria on which
the decision by the director of the AED or his delegate. The mere mention in the commentary of the articles that

56 Bill n°7216B, doc. parl n°7216B/03, Commentary on the articles, p. 23.
57 Opinion of 16 December 2016 of the Legal Service of the Council of the European Union, doc 15655/16, point 47.

122

5ANNUAL REPORT2020 CNPDAPPENDICES the decision could be based on all the elements relating to the request is not sufficiently clear to
meet the requirement of foreseeability and precision of a law.

A clear definition of legitimate interest is important because, as the EDPS has rightly pointed out, a definition that is too
broad scope of the concept of legitimate interest would risk encouraging those wishing to access this information to
purely opportunistic reasons. It is therefore appropriate to require a direct link between the justification and the interest and to require
that the use of the information obtained manifestly contributes to the achievement of the public objective in question59.

Thus, as specified by the legal service of the Council of the European Union when drawing up the directive
2018/843, “it would be necessary to integrate into the operative part of the proposal a definition containing criteria
objective and verifiable. In other words, the self-proclaimed mission statements of organizations focused on
transparency (or, incidentally, on the fight against money laundering) cannot be considered
as sufficient in themselves, but they must be combined with proof that the public utility of the
actions taken by the organization in this area has been independently recognized. »60.

The definition of “legitimate interest” should at least provide that a person could only access the data if
“it is able to demonstrate to the competent national authorities that it has the capacity and the intention to use
this information for the sole public interest in question (which must be precisely defined), and this in a way that does not
does not disproportionately infringe the right to respect for private life, and if it agrees to be submitted
to the authorization and control of the competent national authorities in the exercise of any activity in which it is carried out
use of all or part of the information thus obtained.

iii. The data necessary to obtain access to the register

It follows from Article 27, paragraph 2 of the draft law that a natural person wishing to obtain information
of the registry on the basis of its legitimate interest must provide the following personal data in support of
his request: his surnames, first names, nationalities, date of birth, place of birth and domicile or residence. There
applicant must still provide an extract from the criminal record.

No information is provided as to the necessity of these data and the criminal record, the comment of the
articles limiting themselves to explaining that “the criminal record must be given for each person who will have access to the
disclosed data”62.

Moreover, the bill does not specify either what type of criminal record bulletin should be provided, or the duration
retention of the criminal record following the filing of the request for access to the register. The CNPD is still wondering
on the vagueness of the wording used in the body of the bill compared to the precision in the
commentary on articles about people who must provide a criminal record. In fact, if several people

60 Opinion of 16 December 2016 of the Legal Service of the Council of the European Union, doc 15655/16, point 49.
61 Opinion of 16 December 2016 of the Legal Service of the Council of the European Union, doc 15655/16, point 49.
62 Bill n°7216B, doc. speak. No. 7216B/03, page 22.

ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPD would have access to the data, it follows that the criminal records of all these people should be provided.
What about the request made by a legal person, should we also provide a criminal record of all
the persons engaged with the legal person who could have access to the data? If so,
should the employer collect them and forward them to the AED or would the employees be expected to
transmit them individually?

In the absence of an explanation of the necessity and proportionality of these data and of more precision in
the body of the text, the CNPD is not in a position to analyze the compliance of the provision with the legislation on
data protection.

iv. The use of the data obtained

Furthermore, according to paragraph 4, the extract containing the information on the beneficial owners provided in
case of favorable decision “indicates the use for the purpose of which access is granted. The natural person or
plaintiff may not use the information for purposes other than those specified in the extract”. The CNPD
believes that the use for which access is granted should be exclusively within the context of the
bill and can in no case go beyond this framework. She thus wonders about the power of the director of
the AED or its delegate to determine the purposes for which the data from the
register and considers that the provision should be clarified in order to circumscribe this power.

d. As for access to information relating to a fiduciary or a trust that holds or owns a
controlling interest in a company or other legal entity other than those referred to in
Article 30 of Directive 2015/849 (Article 29)

Article 29 of the draft law transposes Article 31, paragraph 4, letter d) of Directive 2015/849, granting a
access to information relating to a trust or trust that owns or has a controlling interest in
a company or in another legal entity other than those referred to in Article 30(1) of the Directive
(EU) 2015/849 by direct or indirect ownership, in particular by means of bearer shares or through a
control by other means, to any natural or legal person who submits a written request relating to
such trust or trust. According to recital 28 of Directive 2018/843, “Member States should
be able to refuse a written request if there are reasonable grounds to suspect that the written request is not
not consistent with the objectives of this Directive. ". The CNPD notes that the authors of the bill have not resumed
this possibility in the draft law.

Moreover, the bill does not regulate this access. For example, it does not determine what information to
accompany the request for access, nor how to identify the trust or the fiduciary, nor the period within which the AED must
respond to the request. The bill should therefore be amended to clearly regulate this access.

124

52020 ANNUAL REPORT CNPDANNEXES e. Regarding the interconnection of the register (Article 33)

Article 33 of the bill governs the future interconnection of the register with the registers set up in the other
Member States. The information referred to in Article 14 entered in the Register of Trusts and Trusts is
available through the system of interconnection of registers established by Article 22(2),
of Directive (EU) 2017/1132 in accordance with the access procedures provided for by the draft law and the measures
taken for its execution (paragraph 2).

Directive 2015/849 provides that only information which is up to date and which corresponds to the real
beneficial owners must be made available through the national register and the system
interconnection of registers. Access to this information must take place in compliance with the rules on
data protection (article 31, paragraph 9, subparagraph 3 of directive 2015/849). Therefore, in case
incorrect data would have been made available, it must be ensured that the AED transmits the data
subsequently corrected to recipients in accordance with Article 19 of the GDPR.

Like the EDPS Opinion on the Proposal for a Directive of the European Parliament and of the Council amending the
directives 89/666/EEC, 2005/56/EC and 2009/101/EC regarding the interconnection of central registers,
trade and companies, the CNPD notes that better accessibility of personal data
also entails increased risks for them63.

As to the data accessible and the purposes of access, given that the data recorded will be
available through the system of interconnection of registers, the CNPD wonders whether all the
data provided for in article 14, including the national identification number (“matricule”) within the meaning of the law
of June 19, 2013 relating to the identification of natural persons, should be accessible. She
refers in this respect to the remarks made above in point II. In addition, it is necessary to ensure compliance with the principle
purposes, even when exchanging data.

As for the duration of data retention, Article 33, paragraph 3 of the draft law provides that the data
referred to in Article 14 are available through the system of interconnection of registers for five
years after the grounds for registration referred to in Article 13, paragraphs 1 or 2 have ceased. The directive leaving
Member States the choice between a minimum duration of five years and a maximum duration of ten years, the CNPD
welcomes the choice of the authors of the bill to limit access to data to five years.

V. Regarding the rights of data subjects

As regards the controller of the register of beneficial owners, namely the AED, it will collect
the data indirectly and must, therefore, in principle, provide all the information provided for in Article 14

ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPD of the GDPR within the deadlines provided for in Article 14, paragraph 3 of the GDPR. According to Article 14, paragraph 5,
letter (c) of the GDPR, the data controller is exempt from this obligation, if the obtaining or communication
is provided for by law, which must provide "appropriate measures to protect the legitimate interests of the
concerned person ". In this regard, recital 38 of Directive 2018/843 specifies that "persons
natural persons whose personal data are kept in national registers as
beneficial owners should be informed accordingly”.

Taking into account the publication of the data contained in the register and in order to protect the legitimate interests
data subjects, the CNPD considers it necessary to provide, like Article 21, paragraph 2 of the Royal Decree
of July 30, 2018 relating to the operating procedures of the UBO register, that the controller
should inform each natural person of their entry in the register64. The persons concerned should
also receive the other information indicated in Article 14, including in particular information relating to
their rights, as well as the procedures applicable to the exercise of these rights, in accordance with recital 38 of
Directive 2018/843. The National Commission considers that this information should be given within the deadlines
provided for in Article 14, paragraph 3 of the GDPR.

It should also be emphasized that trustees and fiduciaries, who collect the data directly
with the beneficial owners, have the obligation to provide them with the information set out in Article
13 GDPR. In order to protect the data subjects, the controller should inform the
data subjects of the specifics of the recording of their data. The authors of the bill could
be inspired by article 21 of the Belgian royal decree of 30 July 2018 relating to the operating procedures of the register
UBO.

With regard to the right to rectification, the CNPD notes that the draft law provides for a specific regime when
people notice a discrepancy between the beneficial ownership information available in the register
and the information on the beneficial owners it has and informs the AED (article 19 of the draft law).
To inform persons consulting the register that the data has been declared to be incorrect,
a note is inserted in the register and persons requesting access are informed that an update has
been requested (article 19, paragraph 3 of the bill). For the sake of clarity, it might be appropriate to add to the
bill a provision specifying the data rectification procedure to be followed by the data subject,
following the example of the procedure described in article 23 of the Belgian royal decree of July 30, 2018 relating to the methods of
operation of the UBO register:

“§ 1. Any natural person may, directly or through the Treasury Administration,
request, free of charge, from the person responsible for providing information of which he is the beneficial owner, the rectification or deletion
inaccurate data recorded in his name.

the address of the department responsible for managing the register within the Treasury Administration; 4° possible access to the register of listed entities and persons

VI. Regarding the cooperation between the FIU, supervisory authorities and self-regulatory bodies (Article 32)

It follows from Article 32 of the draft law that the FIU, supervisory authorities and self-regulatory bodies
cooperate closely with each other. As for the form and purposes of the cooperation, paragraph 2 specifies that
“the supervisory authorities and the financial intelligence unit are authorized to exchange information
necessary for the accomplishment of their respective missions in the context of the fight against money laundering and
against the financing of terrorism. The supervisory authorities and the financial intelligence unit use the
information exchanged only for the accomplishment of these missions. »

No information is provided either on the form or on the purposes of the cooperation between the FIU, the authorities.
regulators and self-regulatory bodies. Specifically, the bill does not indicate whether organizations
self-regulatory bodies could exchange data with the FIU and supervisory authorities.

If, despite this, the self-regulatory bodies could be required to exchange personal data
with the FIU and the supervisory authorities, the Council of State has repeatedly stressed that the communication
information to third parties may constitute an interference with privacy65 and thus constitutes under
of Article 11, paragraph 3, of the Constitution, a matter reserved for formal law. It is therefore necessary that the points
essential are fixed by law.

Thus, to the extent that the FIU, supervisory authorities and self-regulatory bodies would exchange information
personal data, the draft law should regulate the cooperation of these data controllers,
specifying the form and purpose of this cooperation for all controllers, including
self-regulatory bodies.

As for international cooperation, article 32, paragraph 2 of the draft law establishes a legal framework for
international cooperation whereby supervisory authorities cooperate with their foreign counterpart authorities
within the limits of the provisions of article 9-2 of the amended law of November 12, 2004.
of Article 31(7) of Directive 2015/849 that “Member States shall ensure that the authorities
authorities and the FIUs are able to provide, in good time and free of charge, the information referred to in
paragraphs 1 and 3 to competent authorities and FIUs of other Member States”. The directive therefore envisages
a European exchange and not an exchange with third countries. In the absence of an analysis of the necessity and the
proportionality of the extension of this cooperation regime to third countries, the CNPD considers that these provisions
should be limited to transposing the provisions of Directive 2015/849.

129

2020 CNPD ANNUAL REPORT Regarding Article 3 of the Bill

4. Amendment 2 concerns Article 3 of the draft law and more specifically the obligation for trustees and
trustees to obtain and maintain basic information about other regulated agents and
trust or fiduciary service providers. The CNPD, in its opinion of February 28, 2020, and the Conseil
of State, in its opinion of March 24, 2020, considered that this provision was not formulated in a way
sufficiently precise.

5. Following amendment 2, the article now specifies that this obligation concerns all other professionals
referred to in article 2 of the amended law of 12 November 2004 on the fight against money laundering and
financing of terrorism who provide services to the trust or fiduciary or who enter into a business relationship
with the trust or fiduciary. The new paragraph 2 specifies what information must be obtained and kept
by the trustees and the fiduciaries, namely the information which allows “the trustees and the fiduciaries
to identify the persons concerned and include, in the case of a natural person, the information
referred to in Article 14, paragraph 2 point 1, letters a) to c) and h) to i)”. More specifically, these are the names
and first name, nationality, country of residence and private or professional address of the person
physical.

6. The National Commission still questions the necessity and proportionality of collecting this data
of “all other professionals who provide services to the trust or fiduciary or who enter into a relationship
business with the trust or fiduciary”. Taking into account his remarks made in point 7 below and as
such an obligation could lead to the collection of a multitude of data from many people
concerned, it reiterates its comments made in its opinion of February 28, 2020 relating to the analysis of the
necessity and proportionality of such an obligation.

7. While the CNPD welcomes the clarification in paragraph 2 of article 3, paragraph 1 of the bill,
she regrets that the provision does not clearly delimit the data to be collected, but rather provides that the
data to be collected “includes” the information referred to in article 14, paragraph 2 point 1, letters a) to c)
and h) to i). In an effort to minimize data, she suggests deleting the words “and include”
of the layout.

 

With regard to article 27 of the bill

8. The CNPD takes note of the additional explanations relating to Article 27, paragraph 2, subparagraph 3 of the bill,
namely the condition of having to provide an extract from the criminal record in order to be granted, on the basis of a
legitimate interest, access to certain data contained in the Register of Trusts and Trusts. According to these
explanations, the criminal record would be necessary to verify whether an applicant provides sufficient guarantees

130

5ANNUAL REPORT2020 CNPDANNEXES of good repute. If the Council of State does not consider that the explanations would be sufficient to justify compliance
of the principles of necessity and appropriateness, the Finance and Budget Committee proposes to delete
the obligation to provide an extract from the criminal record.

9. The CNPD welcomes the clarifications relating to the fundamental rights of data subjects registered in the
Register of trusts and trusts as well as explanations as to the need to provide an extract from the register
judicial. She understands that this requirement is intended to protect data subjects on the register. She
notes with regret, however, that these explanations do not answer the questions raised by the CNPD
in its opinion of February 28, 2020 and to those raised by the Council of State in its opinion of March 24, 2020.
Moreover, like the Council of State, the CNPD wonders about the link between the extract from the criminal record and
access to the register. Would access be systematically refused to people who do not have a
clean criminal record extract?

10. Furthermore, the National Commission wonders about the two options presented by the authors: (a) maintain
the requirement to provide an extract from the criminal record, without providing guarantees aimed at supervising the processing
of the criminal record, or (b) remove this requirement from the bill without replacing it with others
measures to protect data subjects entered in the register against possible abuse. In effect,
these options do not really answer the questions raised by the CNPD and the Council of State. In case
the authors would like to remove the obligation to provide an extract from the criminal record, the CNPD further notes
that the bill does not require an applicant to provide identification in support of their application
and questions the absence of such an obligation. In any case, as the authors wish, at
rightly, to protect the fundamental rights of registered data subjects, authors could provide
other measures, such as, for example, proof of the security measures that would be implemented by the
applicant to protect the data or the requirement for more documents demonstrating the interest
legitimate (for example, information that demonstrates suspicions that a trust or fiduciary is being used or
has been used for money laundering or terrorist financing purposes).

11. Finally, the CNPD regrets that the authors of the bill did not follow its other recommendations

formulated in its opinion of February 28, 2020.
