ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPD Additional opinion relating to draft law no. 7498 amending the amended law
of 18 July 2018 on the Grand Ducal Police.

(Deliberation n°10/2020 of April 17, 2020)

In accordance with article 46, paragraph 1, letter (c) of directive (EU) n°2016/680 of 27 April 2016 relating to
the protection of individuals with regard to the processing of personal data by
competent authorities for the purposes of prevention, investigation and prosecution of criminal offenses
or execution of criminal penalties, and on the free movement of such data, and repealing the Framework Decision
2008/977/JHA of the Council (hereinafter referred to as "the directive"), to which article 8 of the law of 1 August refers
2018 on the organization of the National Commission for Data Protection and the general regime on the
data protection (hereinafter the "Law of 1 August 2018 on the organization of the National Commission for
data protection and the general data protection regime”), the National Commission for the
data protection (hereinafter the “National Commission” or the “CNPD”), “advises the Chamber of Deputies,
the Government and other institutions and bodies regarding legislative and administrative measures relating to
the protection of the rights and freedoms of natural persons with regard to the processing of personal data”.

On February 28, 2020, the CNPD notified draft law no. 7498 amending the amended law of 18
July 2018 on the Grand Ducal Police (hereinafter the “draft law”).

On April 6, 2020, the Minister of Internal Security invited the National Commission to
to vote on the government amendment to the bill which was approved by the Council of
government in its session of March 20, 2020 (hereinafter the “government amendment”).

This additional opinion will be limited to commenting on points 1° and 2° of the government amendment, the
point 3° being favorably received by the National Commission.
 
1. The introduction of a new point 4° in paragraph 2 of article 43bis of the bill
 
Paragraph 2 of article 43bis defines and enumerates the criteria of places presenting a particular risk of
commission of crimes or misdemeanors or attacks on the security of persons or property, without however aiming
namely specific places.

However, the government amendment deviates from this logic because it introduces a new point 4° in the
paragraph 2 of article 43bis which specifically targets a place: the national football and rugby stadium located in
Cockelscheuer.

132

5ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPDANNEXES The National Commission therefore wonders why this specific place is added when
points 1°, 2°, 3° and 5° of paragraph 2 of article 43bis, which do not refer to any particular place, remain
unchanged.
 
Indeed, it emerges from the general scheme of Article 43bis paragraph (2) that the initial intention of the authors of the
bill was to provide for a more general provision, likely to cover all places that fall within the
criteria of points 1° to 4°. In this logic, he will come back on the proposal of the Director of the Grand-Ducal Police and
then by the ministerial authorization to precisely designate the places (security zones) which fall within the scope
application of article 43bis.

An alternative solution would have been to provide in the bill an exhaustive list of specific places
placed under video surveillance, which, in the eyes of the CNPD, would not have been advisable from a point of view
legalistic.
 
However, as previously mentioned, the amendment under consideration deviates from the initial logic of the text.

The CNPD therefore considers that the addition of this new point 4° is superfluous and suggests deleting it, in
the extent to which the national football and rugby stadium located in Kockelscheuer is likely to be covered by the
criteria provided for the places referred to in point 3°, or even point 4° of the initial text of article 43bis of the bill.
  
This hypothesis is also expressly referred to by the authors of the bill in their commentary on
paragraph 2 of article 43bis of the bill. Indeed, the latter specify that: “Point 3 aims at
infrastructures such as stadiums or conference centers where events are regularly organized
national or international in scope. As in the past, video surveillance in these places will not be activated.
permanently, but only during the event in the context of which attacks on persons or
goods are likely to occur. We can cite, by way of example, the surroundings of the Josy BARTHEL stadium in
the occasion of an international football match (…)”.
 
2. The use of the notion of “safety zone” instead of the term “place” in paragraph 3 of article 43bis

Bill

 
The purpose of the government amendment is also to replace the term “place” with the notion of “area of
security” in paragraph 3 of article 43bis.

It appears from the commentary to the government amendment that the authors of the bill justify this
replacement, in order “to ensure consistency with previous legislation” and further specify that: “The term

133

ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPD “security zone” is also consistent with article 2 of this bill which provides that the maintenance of
video surveillance in places designated as security zones before the entry into force of this law
must be authorized in accordance with the provisions of the latter within a maximum period of twelve months following
entry into force of this law”.

First of all, it should be noted that the concept of “safety zone” introduced in paragraph 3 of article 43bis of the
bill differs from the term “safe area” as used in clause 2 of the bill, insofar as the latter
ci expressly refers to places designated as safe zones under the former legislation.
The notion of “safe area” introduced in paragraph 3 of article 43bis must be understood in the context
of this bill.
 
Thus, and although the National Commission understands the desire of the authors of the bill to take up the notion
of a “safety zone” in order to “ensure consistency with previous legislation”, it considers necessary, for
a better understanding of paragraph 3 of article 43bis, that this concept be defined in the bill.

Indeed, the introduction of the notion of “safety zone”, in isolation; to paragraph 3 of
Article 43bis, without having defined it beforehand, compromises the understanding of the said paragraph, all the more so
more while the term "safety zone" is different from the terminology used so far by the authors
of the bill in paragraphs 2 and 4 of the aforementioned article.

If the “safety zone” within the meaning of paragraph 3 of article 43bis designates a place at risk66 which has been delimited
beforehand by the Grand-Ducal Police then communicated to the Minister in charge of the Police in order to
that the latter issues a ministerial authorization for such a delimited place, then this must have emerged clearly from the
law Project.

Therefore, if the authors of the government amendment wish to make a parallelism with the former
legislation, it would be necessary to define the concept of "safe area" or to replace the term "places",
used in different places in article 43bis, by the terms “security zone”, or else delete the terms
“safety zone”, in order to standardize all the terminology used in the bill accordingly.
 
Thus decided in Esch-sur-Alzette on April 17, 2020.

52020 ANNUAL REPORT CNPDANNEXES Opinion on bill no. 7606 introducing a series of measures concerning

and amending 1. the amended law of 25 November 1975 concerning the delivery to the public
medication ; 2. the amended law of 11 April 1983 regulating the placing
in the market and advertising of medicines.

(Deliberation n°13/2020 of June 8, 2020)

In accordance with Article 57, paragraph 1, letter (c) of Regulation No. 2016/679 of April 27, 2016 relating to
the protection of individuals with regard to the processing of personal data and the freedom
movement of such data, and repealing Directive 95/46/EC (general data protection regulation)
(hereinafter “the GDPR”), to which article 7 of the law of 1 August 2018 on the organization of the Commission refers
national data protection system and the general data protection regime, the Commission
Commission for Data Protection (hereinafter referred to as “the National Commission” or “the CNPD”)
“advises, in accordance with the law of the Member State, the national parliament, the government and other institutions
and organizations regarding legislative and administrative measures relating to the protection of the rights and freedoms of
natural persons with regard to the processing”.

On June 4, 2020, the Minister of Health asked the National Commission to rule on
project no. 7606 introducing a series of measures concerning natural persons in the
framework of the fight against the SARS-CoV-2 (COVID-19) virus and amending 1. the amended law of 25 November
1975 concerning the supply of medicines to the public; 2. the amended law of 11 April 1983 on
regulation of the marketing and advertising of medicinal products (hereinafter the "draft law").
In the said letter, the Minister specified that the project in question must enter into force no later than
June 24, 2020, date of the lifting of the state of crisis, and that therefore, she asks us to send her our opinion
within the shortest possible time. The CNPD would like to point out that its opinion has thus been drawn up and adopted only
on the basis of the information available to it and subject to any unknown future considerations
nowadays.

The purpose of this bill is to create a legal framework relating to measures taken with regard to
individuals to continue the fight against Covid-19 by limiting the spread of SARS-CoV-2 on the
territory of the Grand Duchy of Luxembourg through a limited catalog of well-defined measures. It emerges
of the explanatory memorandum that alongside the measures centered on natural persons, the bill is structured around
of the following three axes:

• limitation of freedom of assembly;

137

CNPD 2020 ANNUAL REPORT • the application of protective measures as well as the identification, monitoring and rapid removal of people

infected and susceptible to infection;

• the establishment of “certain guarantees around the processing of data necessary for monitoring individuals and

the fight against the pandemic. »

The National Commission would like to stress on a preliminary basis that the protection of personal data is not
to be considered as an obstacle to the implementation of personal data processing in the context of
the fight against the Covid-19 epidemic, as long as the fundamental principles provided for by the GDPR are respected. She
thus intends to limit its observations to the provisions of the bill having an impact on respect for the
privacy and the protection of personal data, and more specifically in Article 9 thereof.

Ad article 9 of bill n°7606

Article 9 of bill n°7606 aims at the creation of an information system by the Department of Health, in order to
to monitor the evolution of the situation related to Covid-19 and to formulate recommendations in the interest of
public health for the attention of the Government (hereinafter: the “information system”). The comment of
the article specifies that for this purpose, a monitoring system with different indicators and types of data is implemented.
place, including both personal data and non-personal data which must
must be sent to the public health authority.

Pursuant to paragraph (2) of Article 9 of the Bill, various personal data concerning
people infected or presumed to be infected with Covid-19 must be sent to the Health Department by
hospitals, accommodation structures and healthcare networks with a view to detecting, evaluating,
monitoring and combating Covid-19. These data are listed in articles 3 and 4 of the law of 1 August 2018 on
mandatory notification of certain diseases in the context of public health protection. Given
that the draft law does not define other categories of individual data to be provided than those listed in
Articles 3 and 4 mentioned above, the National Commission considers that this article should be read restrictively and
that notwithstanding the fact that the list of data to be collected includes the precision "at least", it must not
not be extended in this case, otherwise it would have to be specified. The National Commission therefore understands that it is more
specifically surname, first name, address, date of birth, medical diagnosis, date of first symptoms
and date of medical diagnosis, date of sample and origin of sample, country where the disease was contracted
and source of infection if known.

It is therefore undeniable that special categories of personal data, known as data
"sensitive", will be processed through this information system. These data, including data concerning the
health, are specifically regulated by Article 9 of the GDPR. As a matter of principle, it is prohibited to process personal data
sensitive, unless one of the ten conditions provided for in paragraph (2) of Article 9 of the GDPR is met. Below

138

52020 ANNUAL REPORT CNPDANNEXES reserves the following comments and in relation to the declaration of January 30, 2020 of the World Organization
of Health (WHO) that the appearance of the SARS-CoV-2 (Covid-19) coronavirus constitutes a “health emergency
world”, as well as to the subsequent declaration of the state of crisis on the basis of article 32 paragraph 4 of the
Luxembourg Constitution by Grand-Ducal regulation of 18 March 202067, the CNPD considers that the exception
provided for in Article 9 paragraph 2) letter i) of the GDPR is applicable in this case. This provision provides more
specifically that the processing of sensitive data can be carried out when it is “necessary for reasons
of public interest in the field of public health, such as protection against cross-border threats
serious harm to health, or for the purpose of ensuring high standards of quality and safety of healthcare
and medicinal products or medical devices, on the basis of Union law or the law of the Member State which
provides for appropriate and specific measures to safeguard the rights and freedoms of the data subject,
including professional secrecy. »

Recital (46) of the GDPR specifies in this context that certain types of processing may be justified
both for important reasons of public interest and for the vital interests of the data subject, for example
when treatment is needed to track outbreaks and their spread.

In addition to Article 9 of the GDPR, the processing of personal data envisaged by the Health Department
must be based on one of the lawfulness criteria provided for in Article 6 of the GDPR. Based on the same considerations, the
CNPD considers that said processing is to be considered as "necessary for the performance of a mission of interest
public or falling within the exercise of official authority vested in the controller” (Article 6
paragraph (1) letter e) of the GDPR).

Recital (54) of the GDPR states in this context that the "processing of special categories of data
of a personal nature may be necessary for reasons of public interest in the areas of public health,
without the consent of the person concerned. »

The legal basis of the public interest on which the processing in question therefore rests68 makes applicable all
the rights provided for by the GDPR for the benefit of the persons concerned, excluding the right to portability. THE
bill n°7606 nevertheless provides in its article 9 paragraph (4) that persons infected or presumed
infected cannot object to the processing of their data in the information system referred to in that article.
By this exclusion of the right of opposition, it appears that the authors of the bill are making use of the option offered
by Article 23 paragraph (1) letter e) of the GDPR to limit the rights of individuals to guarantee, in particular,
important public health goals.

Without prejudice to its remarks under point 2. concerning the data retention period, the CNPD may
a priori understand that this limitation of the right of opposition of infected persons, as well as persons
suspected infected and whose test is positive, is mandatory in order to be able to follow the evolution of this virus

67 This is the Grand-Ducal regulation of 18 March 2020 introducing a series of measures in the context of the fight against Covid-19.
68 By Article 6 paragraph (1) letter e) as well as Article 9 paragraph 2) letter i) of the GDPR.

ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPD still very little known by the scientific world, especially since at this stage it is premature to affirm with certainty

any such protection. Therefore, at present, a positive result of a serological test does not guarantee a
immunity. »69

However, since the CNPD does not have the necessary scientific and epidemiological skills, it is not

is really necessary in the fight against Covid-19.

Furthermore, pursuant to paragraph (2) of Article 23 of the GDPR, each legislative measure which aims to limit the
rights of data subjects must contain a number of specific provisions, including
listed. In order to assess whether the text of bill n°7606 complies with the provisions of the GDPR and responds more
in particular to the requirements of Article 9 paragraph (2) letter i) of the GDPR and of the said Article 23 paragraph (2)
of the GDPR, the CNPD will successively analyze the purposes of the processing and the categories of personal data
personnel (1.), the data retention period (2.), the safeguards intended to prevent misuse or access
or illicit transfer (3.), as well as the right of persons to be informed (4.).

1. Regarding the purposes of the processing and the categories of personal data

Article 9 paragraph (1) of draft law n°7606 lists four different purposes pursued by the implementation of
place of the information system for which the Health Department is to be considered responsible for processing
in accordance with the meaning of Article 4 point 7) of the GDPR. Pursuant to Article 5 paragraph (1) letter b) of the GDPR,
the purposes of data processing must be determined, explicit and legitimate. The CNPD considers that
the purposes, as currently described in article 9 of bill n°7606, may seem quite broad,
what it can a priori understand given that the consequences and the future development of Covid-19 have not
could yet be analyzed in detail by the Health Department. However, given the scale of the processing and the
sensitivity of the data that will be processed there, the National Commission recalls that these purposes must be understood
strictly and that any use of the data that would not be part of them would not respect the principle of
GDPR purpose limitation.

With regard to the categories of personal data, the CNPD wonders about the categories of
data subjects whose data are processed. The Covid-19 test strategy presented by Madame
the Minister of Health on May 22, 202070 includes three different ways in which PCR71 diagnostic tests are
used in Luxembourg: reactively in the presence of symptoms, actively for the benefit of certain
categories of people particularly at risk, as well as preventively by representative samples
(“cluster prevalence studies”) to support deconfinement.

2. Regarding the storage period

Article 5 paragraph (1) letter (e) of the GDPR provides that personal data must be
“kept in a form allowing the identification of the persons concerned for a period not exceeding
not that necessary for the purposes for which they are processed”. It is also apparent from recital
(45) of the GDPR only when the processing is necessary for the performance of a task in the public interest or falling within the scope of
the exercise of official authority vested in the controller, it should be left to Union law
or the right of a Member State to establish, inter alia, the retention period of the data. Moreover, as already
aforementioned, Article 5 paragraph (1) letter (b) of the GDPR provides that personal data must
be “collected for specified, explicit and legitimate purposes, and not be further processed in a
manner incompatible with those purposes.

Thus, the retention period must be determined according to the purpose that led to the collection of the data.
in question. Once this objective has been achieved, this data should be deleted or anonymised (in order in particular to
produce statistics).

74 Last name, first name, address, date of birth, medical diagnosis, date of first symptoms and date of medical diagnosis, date of sample and origin of sample,

country where the disease was contracted and the source of infection if known.

5ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPDANNEXES Article 9 paragraph (5) of bill no. 7606 provides that the personal data of persons
infected or presumed to be infected will be stored in the information system in a form allowing
the identification of persons for “the period necessary to prevent and combat Covid-19 and the data
are anonymized no later than six months after the law ceases to have effect. »

A priori, the draft law under examination will enter into force the day after its publication in the Official Journal of the
Grand Duchy of Luxembourg for a period of one month (article 13 of bill n°7606). The authors of the project
of law explain in the explanatory memorandum that the particularity of the bill is based on its applicability in the
time and that it will produce effects a priori only from June 25, 2020, end of the state of crisis, to July 25, 2020.
 
In the commentary to article 13 of the bill, it is specified that “the health situation in relation to the
The spread of Covid-19 is constantly evolving, which explains the limited duration of application of this
law. The National Commission therefore understands that the state of the health crisis will be reassessed before July 24
2020 and depending on the results, it assumes that the Chamber of Deputies may, if necessary, decide to
extend the applicability of the law in question.

It appears from the above that there is a double data retention period: the first period being that of
the end of applicability of the law (a priori on July 24, 2020 but depending on the circumstances, this period could be
extended as mentioned above) and the second deadline is six months after the end of the first deadline.

The commentary to article 9 of bill no. 7606 specifies in this context that “in view of the purposes of the
information system, the retention period of the personal data contained in the system is limited to
the duration of the management of the pandemic, increased by a period of six months to process any requests for
processing of data from foreign or European health authorities as well as to process any
requests related to scientific research, historical or statistical purposes. »

The National Commission wishes to stress first of all that it does not have the scientific expertise and
necessary epidemiological analysis, in order to assess whether the very retention of the data in the information system
people presumed to be infected, but whose test is negative, is really necessary within the framework of the
fight against Covid-19. In the absence of more precise explanations by the authors of the bill, it cannot
assess whether any arguments from scientific and epidemiological experts justify why
this data should be kept for a certain period of time.

With regard to the GDPR, it is necessary and essential to define a data retention period at the
within the Health Department's information system that is proportionate to the purpose pursued.
Therefore, it is necessary to define objective criteria making it possible to justify a retention period.
adequate.

143

2020 CNPD ANNUAL REPORT At the risk of repeating ourselves, the CNPD is not an expert in health and epidemic management,
it is difficult for her to assess whether it is proportionate, in order to combat the expansion of Covid-19, that the data
personal data of infected and presumed infected persons will be kept in the system
information for a specified number of months. She wonders, however, what are the reasons
sanitary and/or scientific which led the authors of the bill to insert in article 9 paragraph
(5) GDPR a specific retention period of 6 months after future law ceases to produce
its effects.

By way of comparison, French law n°2020-546 of May 11, 2020 extending the state of health emergency and
supplementing its provisions contains a provision a priori similar to the text proposed by the legislator
Luxembourgish. Indeed, article 11 provides “that for the sole purpose of combating the spread of the epidemic of
covid-19 and for the duration strictly necessary for this purpose or, at most, for a period of six months from
the end of the state of health emergency declared by article 4 of emergency law n°2020-290 of March 23, 2020 for
dealing with the covid-19 epidemic, personal data concerning health relating to individuals
affected by this virus and to people who have been in contact with them can be treated and shared, as appropriate
where applicable without the consent of the persons concerned, within the framework of an information system created by decree
in the Council of State and implemented by the Minister for Health. »

However, paragraph 2 of the aforementioned article 11 contains an important clarification, insofar as "the personal data
personnel collected by these information systems for these purposes cannot be retained after a period of
three months after their collection”. Thus, even if the French system in itself will be able to operate until the
later than six months after the end of the state of health emergency, personal data should regularly
be deleted, or even anonymized, three months after they were collected.

In Belgium, Royal Decree No. 25 of May 28, 2020 amending Royal Decree No. 18 of May 4, 2020 establishing
a database from Sciensano as part of the fight against the spread of the coronavirus
COVID-19, entered into force on June 5, 2020. As Royal Decree No. 18 of May 4, 2020 already ceased its effects
on June 4, 2020, it was decided to extend it until June 30, 2020. In the report to the King, the Minister of Foreign Affairs
Belgian Social and Public Health clarified that "the deadline for the erasure of personal data
would be adjusted accordingly (July 5, 2020 instead of June 9, 2020)”, i.e. a retention period for
data five days after the end of validity of the decree in question.

In conclusion, the CNPD can only note that the legislators of the neighboring countries of Luxembourg have opted in
this context for much shorter retention periods. However, as mentioned above, the Commission
does not have the necessary elements and explanations at its disposal to decide on the proportionality
a retention period for the data of infected and presumed infected persons of six months after the
law will cease to have effect.

144

5ANNUALREPORT2020 CNPDANNEXES In order to ensure that the data is not kept longer than necessary, time limits should be
be set either for erasure or for periodic review. Thus, an alternative would be to provide that in
depending on the evolution of Covid-19, the relevance of a storage period a priori shorter than six months,
be regularly evaluated, especially since at present it is not possible to predict how many
times and for what period of time the applicability of the draft law will be extended.

3. As to safeguards intended to prevent misuse or unlawful access or transfer

The National Commission recalls that, whatever the emergency context, sufficient guarantees with regard to
compliance with the fundamental principles of the right to the protection of personal data must be
brought. The supervision of access to health data is essential in this context with regard to the requirements
provided for in Article 9 paragraph 2 letter i) of the GDPR.

Under Article 9 paragraph (3) of the bill under review, “only doctors and healthcare professionals
health, specifically designated and authorized within the framework of this law by the director of health or his
delegated to detect, assess, monitor and combat Covid-19 are authorized to access data relating
to the health of people who are infected or presumed to be infected. Said paragraph goes on to limit access to
data relating to health to the strict extent that it “is necessary for the performance of legal missions or
entrusted to them to prevent and combat Covid-19. »

Given the sensitive nature of health-related data, the National Commission can only approve
that the circle of people who can access health-related data and the context in which they access it
is circumscribed. It follows from Article 9 paragraph (3) of the Bill that all persons whom the Director of
health can authorize access to the information system are subject to the professional secrecy provided for in article 458
of the Penal Code, as furthermore required by the aforementioned Article 9 paragraph 2 letter i) of the GDPR.

Article 9 paragraph (5) of the bill further requires that “data are processed under conditions
guaranteeing its security, confidentiality and integrity. » In view of the nature and volume of the data
processed as well as risks for individuals in the event of a breach of data security, the CNPD considers
It is essential that adequate technical and organizational security measures are put in place in order to
to guarantee a state-of-the-art level of security in the health sector.

In this regard, the CNPD would like to emphasize the importance of the security obligation provided for in Article 5 paragraph (1)
letter f) and Article 32 of the GDPR, requiring that technical and organizational measures guaranteeing a
level of security appropriate to the risk, are put in place. It considers that the implementation of the processing of
personal data contained in the information system must in particular guarantee the use of
strong authentication of people with access and said system should be equipped with tracking (logging)

145

ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPD individual access for a period of five years from the recording of the log, which constitutes a
additional guarantee regarding the protection of personal data. It is also essential
that the data is irreversibly destroyed after the retention period has expired.

4. Regarding the rights of data subjects

Paragraph (4) of Article 9 of the draft law specifies that “the rights of data subjects provided for by the
General Data Protection Regulation (EU) 2016/679 are exercised with the Directorate of Health”.
As regards the limitation of the right of opposition, the CNPD refers to its observations above.

Under Articles 13 and 14 of the GDPR, the controller is obliged to provide data subjects
certain information when personal data is collected directly from them or
indirectly through a third party. Precise and appropriate information must therefore be provided to people
concerned in a particular health context.

Thus, under Article 14 of the GDPR, the Health Directorate is obliged to provide this information to the person
infected, these data coming a priori from a third party (hospital establishments, accommodation structures and
care networks). With regard to personal data relating to alleged persons
infected in the context of “contact tracing”, it is not clear whether this collection is carried out directly by
the Directorate of Health or indirectly (for example via the infected person himself). In both
hypotheses, the right to information of said persons is to be respected by the Health Department.

Finally, the CNPD would like to point out that when a person carries out a test, they should in principle already
be informed that in the event of a positive result, their personal data will be transferred to the
Department of Health and recorded there in their information system.

Thus decided in Esch-sur-Alzette on June 8, 2020.

52020 ANNUAL REPORT CNPDANNEXES Supplementary opinion relating to bill no. 7606 introducing a series of

(Deliberation n°14/2020 of June 16, 2020)

In accordance with Article 57, paragraph 1, letter (c) of Regulation No. 2016/679 of April 27, 2016 relating to
the protection of individuals with regard to the processing of personal data and the freedom
movement of such data, and repealing Directive 95/46/EC (general data protection regulation)
(hereinafter “the GDPR”), to which article 7 of the law of 1 August 2018 on the organization of the Commission refers
national data protection system and the general data protection regime, the Commission
Commission for Data Protection (hereinafter referred to as “the National Commission” or “the CNPD”)
“advises, in accordance with the law of the Member State, the national parliament, the government and other institutions
and organizations regarding legislative and administrative measures relating to the protection of the rights and freedoms of
natural persons with regard to the processing”.

Following the amendments adopted by the Health and Sports Committee on June 11, 2020 concerning
project no. 7606 introducing a series of measures concerning natural persons within the framework of
the fight against the SARS-CoV-2 (COVID-19) virus and amending 1. the amended law of 25 November 1975 concerning
the distribution of medicines to the public; 2. the amended law of 11 April 1983 regulating the placing
on the market and advertising of medicinal products (hereinafter the “draft law”), the CNPD still intends to formulate,
in addition to its initial opinion n°13/2020 of June 8, 2020, the following observations.

By amendment 3 of bill n°7606, the authors have notably inserted a new paragraph 2
in article 5 of the said draft and which concerns the activities of transporting people by collective means in
as part of an organized trip. Thus, in order to follow the evolution of the spread of Covid-19 within the framework
of an organized trip, the operator of a collective means of passenger transport “is required to transmit
as far as possible, on request, to the doctor of the Health Directorate or to the professionals
of health authorized under this law by the director of health the surname, first name, telephone number
phone and email addresses of individuals who have experienced high-risk exposure due to one of the
situations referred to in Article 2, point 4°. » The comment of the amendment adds that the data relating to
to passengers at high risk of being infected and which are sent to the Health Department on request specify
in addition “the means, date and time of transport and, if applicable, the car and the seat occupied by the case
index. »

147

ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPD It follows, as the authors of the amendment specify, that the operator of such a means of
transport already has the identification data of travelers who have used the means of transport
concerned and in which one or more infected persons could be located. However, while the initial purpose
of this collection of personal data of passengers is rather the organization of a trip, the CNPD
understands that the authors of the amendment have another purpose in this obligation to transfer on request
passenger data by the operator of a collective means of passenger transport to the Health Department:
set up a tracing system to identify anyone at risk or at high risk as soon as possible
to be infected in order to implement the necessary precautions (such as quarantine) and to prevent
thus the dissemination of the infection by these people who are in turn contagious.

The CNPD does not call into question the principle that this processing is necessary in order to pursue the aforementioned purpose.
and in order to provide the Health Department with the means to contact passengers as quickly as possible
potentially exposed.

Nevertheless, it wishes to make two remarks in this context:

• The commentary to the article specifies that the terms “operator of a collective means of passenger transport”
concern “land transport (long-distance buses, in particular during the summer holidays, trains
long distance), air and river". However, the CNPD wonders more specifically what is meant by
" organized trip ". Are individual and "unorganized" journeys (such as a simple flight
round trip from Luxembourg airport or a train journey by TGV from Luxembourg-city station)
are not covered by the provision in question and that therefore, even if we are in the presence of an operator of a means
collective transport of persons, this obligation to transmit, on request, passenger data to the
Direction de la santé would not apply in this case?

• As mentioned above, in order to be able to transfer the surname, first name, telephone number and address
electronic mail of people who have suffered high-risk exposure to the Health Directorate, operators
of a collective means of passenger transport must already be in possession of the said data.
The commentary to the amendment in question specifies in this respect that “the passenger affected by this measure must
give prior consent. »

  Nevertheless, the CNPD is of the opinion that it is implicit in the use of the words “is required to transmit”
in the body of the text of article 5 paragraph (2) of bill n°7606 as amended, that it is not a
simple option for operators depending on the passenger's agreement to transmit on request to Management
of health the aforementioned data, but that, on the other hand, they are obliged to carry out the said transfer. Moreover,
it doubts that the consent of the passengers would respect, if necessary, all the conditions provided for by the
GDPR to be lawful, especially with regard to its free nature.

148

52020 ANNUAL REPORT CNPDANNEXES Concerning the retention period of personal data in general, the CNPD refers to
its comments thereon made in its aforementioned opinion of 8 June 2020 on draft law no. 7606. Related
the specific retention period of the personal data of passengers with the operator,
the authors specify in the commentary to the amendment that it “must not be longer than 14 days (duration
maximum incubation period, in the event of contamination during the trip, the contaminated passenger will already be ill
even) ; at the end of this period, they are destroyed. » The CNPD can only support this short deletion period
which appears to be proportionate in relation to the purpose pursued by the processing in question, that is to say, to send the
data relating to passengers at high risk of being infected to the Directorate of Health to ensure that they
self-quarantine in an attempt to break the chain of transmission of the virus. However, for reasons of
clarity and legal certainty, the CNPD suggests inserting this clarification on the data retention period
in the body of the text of article 5 paragraph (2) of the amended bill n°7606.

Thus decided in Esch-sur-Alzette on June 16, 2020.

movement of such data, and repealing Directive 95/46/EC (general data protection regulation)
(hereinafter “the GDPR”), to which article 7 of the law of 1 August 2018 on the organization of the Commission refers
national data protection system and the general data protection regime, the Commission
Commission for Data Protection (hereinafter referred to as “the National Commission” or “the CNPD”)
“advises, in accordance with the law of the Member State, the national parliament, the government and other institutions
and organizations regarding legislative and administrative measures relating to the protection of the rights and freedoms of
natural persons with regard to the processing”.

By letter dated June 11, 2020, the Minister of Communications and Media invited the Commission
to decide on the draft Grand-Ducal regulation establishing the seat of the Commission
National Data Protection Authority (hereinafter the "Draft Grand-Ducal Regulation").

The entry into application of the GDPR was accompanied by an implementing law, the law of August 1, 2018 on
organization of the National Commission for Data Protection and the general data protection regime
data. Article 3 of this law provides that the headquarters of the CNPD is determined by Grand-Ducal regulation. The rule
of 1 August 2018 establishing the seat of the National Commission for Data Protection
confirmed that maintaining the headquarters in Esch-sur-Alzette was the appropriate solution for the situation of the CNPD.

The explanatory memorandum to the draft Grand-Ducal regulation mentions in a relevant manner that with the entry into force
application of the GDPR, the implementing law and the law of 1 August 2018 on data protection
in criminal matters as well as in matters of national security, the human and financial resources of the CNPD
have been substantially increased in order to respond to its new skills and missions, as well as its
new powers. The maximum capacities in terms of office spaces that can be made available by the
Fonds Belval were however affected.

The CNPD has therefore itself looked for premises corresponding to its needs located in the district
du Belval, but now coming under the municipality of Sanem, and no longer that of Esch-sur-Alzette. The project

150

52020 ANNUAL REPORT CNPDANNEXES of the Grand-Ducal regulation under opinion is part of the implementation of article 3 of the law of 1 August 2018 on
organization of the National Commission for Data Protection and the general data protection regime
data in that it is necessary to change the location of the CNPD headquarters from Esch-sur-Alzette to Sanem.

However, as the new premises of the CNPD are located more precisely in the territory of Belvaux, a
of the four localities of the municipality of Sanem, the CNPD proposes to specify in article 1 of the draft regulation
grand-ducal that the seat of the National Commission for Data Protection is located in the “Commune
of Sanem, locality of Belvaux”, instead of simply “Sanem”.

Furthermore, Article 2 of the draft Grand-Ducal regulation provides that the “Grand-Ducal Regulation of 1 August 2018
transferring the headquarters of the National Commission for Data Protection is repealed. “However, the text to
to repeal was entitled "Grand-ducal regulation of 1 August 2018 establishing the seat of the National Commission
for data protection”. Thus, the CNPD recommends replacing the word “transfer” by “fixation”.

Thus decided in Esch-sur-Alzette on June 26, 2020.

to the criminal penalties provided for in Article 458 of the Criminal Code, the CNPD considers that additional access to the
information system appear legitimate.

I. On the bill

1. Preliminary remarks

The National Commission is pleased that Article 58 of the amended law of 18 July 2018 on the Grand Ducal Police,
from the point of view of legal certainty, constitutes the legal basis of the morality investigation carried out by the Police
Luxembourg in the context set out above, in accordance with Article 6 paragraph (3) of the GDPR76.

Indeed, it should be recalled that the processing of personal data collected and processed in the
framework of the performance of a mission of public interest or relating to the exercise of the official authority vested in the
responsible for the processing must have a legal basis in accordance with Article 6 paragraph (3) of the GDPR,
read together with its paragraph (1) letters c) and e)77 which provides that: “The basis of the processing referred to in
paragraph 1, points c) and e), is defined by:

To. Union law; Or
b. the law of the Member State to which the controller is subject.
  The purposes of the processing are defined in this legal basis or, with regard to the processing referred to in
paragraph 1(e) are necessary for the performance of a task carried out in the public interest or relating to the exercise

75 Article 1 of the bill aimed at modifying article 58 of the amended law of 18 July 2018 on the Grand Ducal Police.
76 It should be specified that the Law of 1 August 2018 on the protection of individuals with regard to the processing of personal data in the field of
criminal law as well as in terms of national security is not intended to apply in the present case while the processing implemented by the Grand Ducal Police
are for recruitment purposes.

77 Article 6, paragraph (1), letters c) and e) provides that: “Processing is only lawful if and insofar as at least one of the following conditions is met:
fulfilled: (…) c) the processing is necessary for compliance with a legal obligation to which the controller is subject; (…) e) the processing is necessary
for the performance of a task in the public interest or in the exercise of official authority vested in the controller; (…)”

155

CNPD 2020 ANNUAL REPORT public authority vested in the controller. This legal basis may contain
specific provisions to adapt the application of the rules of this regulation, among others: the conditions
general rules governing the lawfulness of processing by the controller; the types of data that are the subject
treatment; the people concerned ; the entities to which the personal data can
be communicated and the purposes for which they may be communicated; purpose limitation; durations
storage; and processing operations and procedures, including measures to ensure a
lawful and fair processing, such as those provided for in other specific processing situations such as
provides for Chapter IX. »

This article provides for a specific constraint related to the lawfulness of data processing necessary for compliance with a
legal obligation or the performance of a task in the public interest or in the exercise of official authority, of which
is vested with the controller. In these two cases, the basis and purposes of the processing of
data must specifically be defined either by European Union law or by the law of the Member State
to which the controller is subject.

In addition, recital (45) of the GDPR specifies that it should “[…] belong to Union law or to European law.
of a Member State to determine the purpose of the processing. Furthermore, this law could specify the conditions
general rules of this Regulation governing the lawfulness of the processing of personal data, establish the
specifications aimed at determining the controller, the type of personal data making
the purpose of the processing, the persons concerned, the entities to which the personal data can be
be disclosed, purpose limitations, retention periods and other measures to ensure
lawful and fair processing. […]”.

Pursuant to the abovementioned provisions, these legal bases should establish specific provisions aimed at
to determine, among other things, the types of data processed, the persons concerned, the entities to which the
data may be communicated and for what purposes, the data retention periods or even
processing operations and procedures. Recital (41) of the GDPR further states that "Where this
regulation refers to a legal basis or legislative measure, it does not necessarily mean that
the adoption of a legislative act by a parliament is required, without prejudice to the obligations provided for under the order
constitution of the Member State concerned. However, this legal basis or legislative measure should be
clear and precise and its application should be foreseeable for litigants, in accordance with the case law of
the Court of Justice of the European Union and the European Court of Human Rights. ". On a national level
a legal basis can therefore constitute a legislative or regulatory act, which is the case in this case of the project
law and the draft Grand-Ducal regulation under consideration.

Thus, the National Commission must emphasize the fundamental importance of the principle of lawfulness of processing
of personal data which must be read in the light of Article 8 paragraph (2) of the Convention
European Court of Human Rights concerning the right to respect for private life, as well as Article 52

156

5ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPDANNEXES paragraphs (1) and (2) of the Charter of Fundamental Rights of the European Union. In essence, these two
articles, together with the consistent case law of the European Court of Human Rights, hold that a
data processing carried out by a public authority may constitute an interference with the right to respect for
privacy or limit the exercise of the right to data protection. This interference or limitation may be justified
provided that it:

• is provided for by a law accessible to the persons concerned and foreseeable as to its repercussions, i.e.

formulated with sufficient precision;

• is necessary in a democratic society, subject to the principle of proportionality;
• respects the essential content of the right to data protection;
• effectively meets objectives of general interest or the need to protect rights and freedoms

of others.

With regard to the first condition, according to the case law of the European Court of Human Rights,
an interference with the right to respect for private life is not “in accordance with the law”, within the meaning of Article 8 paragraph
(2) of the European Convention on Human Rights78, unless it is based on an article of national law
which has certain characteristics. The expression “prescribed by law” therefore implies, according to case law
of the European Court of Human Rights that domestic legislation must use fairly clear terms to
indicate to all sufficiently in what circumstances and under what conditions it empowers the power
to resort to measures affecting their rights protected by the Convention79. Domestic legislation should be
“accessible to the persons concerned and foreseeable as to its repercussions”80. A rule is foreseeable "if
it is formulated with sufficient precision to allow any person – possibly benefiting from a
appropriate assistance – to adapt one’s behavior”81 as well as “The degree of precision required of the ‘law’ in this
regard will depend on the subject in question. »82.

In order to fulfill these criteria of accessibility and predictability of the "law", on the one hand, and thus limit possible
arbitrary and abusive behavior on the part of public authorities, on the other hand, national law can therefore
provide for and regulate more specifically the processing of personal data carried out by such
authorities, such as the Grand Ducal Police. This legal framework would also guarantee the principle of security
legal aid for the benefit of those concerned, candidates for police careers. Legal certainty is
even a general principle of European Union law, requiring in particular that regulations resulting in
adverse consequences for individuals is clear and precise and its application foreseeable for
litigants. The regulations must allow interested parties to know the exact extent of the obligations
that it imposes on them, must enable them to know without ambiguity their rights and their obligations as well as their
allow them to make their arrangements accordingly.

78 Article 8 paragraph (2) of the European Convention on Human Rights provides that: “There shall be no interference by a public authority with the exercise
of this right only insofar as such interference is prescribed by law and constitutes a measure which, in a democratic society, is necessary for the security
national security, public safety, the economic well-being of the country, the maintenance of order and the prevention of criminal offences, the protection of health or
morality, or the protection of the rights and freedoms of others”

79 CouEDH, Fernández Martínez c. Spain [GC], n°56030/07, para. 117
80 CoEDH, Amann c. Switzerland [GC], n°27798/95, February 16, 2000, para. 50; see also CouEDH, Kopp c. Switzerland, n°23224/94, March 25, 1998, para. 55 and

CouEDH, Iordachi and others v. Moldova, n°25198/02, February 10, 2009, para. 50.

81 CoEDH, Amann c. Switzerland [GC], n°27798/95, February 16, 2000, para. 56; see also CouEDH, Malone v. United Kingdom, n°8691/79, 26 April 1985, para.

66; CouEDH, Silver and others v. 5947/72, 6205/73, 7052/75, 7061/75, 7107/75, 7113/75, 25 March 1983, para. 88.

82 ECtHR, The Sunday Times v. United Kingdom, n°6538/74, 26 April 1979, para. 49; see also CouEHD, Silver and others c. United Kingdom, n°5947/72, 6205/73,

7052/75, 7061/75, 7107/75, 7113/75, March 25, 1983, para. 88.

83 See e.g. Court of Human Rights, Aurubis Balgaria of 31 March 2011, C-546/09, points 42-43; Judgment, Alfamicro v. Commission of 14 November 2017, T-831/14, points

155-157.

157

ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPD This is why the European Court of Human Rights in its case law affirms that
“domestic law must offer some protection against arbitrary attacks by public power on
rights guaranteed by Article 8 paragraph 1”84. Therefore, domestic legislation “should define the scope and
procedures for exercising power with sufficient clarity – taking into account the legitimate aim pursued – to provide
the individual adequate protection against arbitrariness. The Court of Justice of the European Union considers that in the event
limitation of the protection of personal data or the right to respect for private life a legal text
“must provide clear and precise rules governing the scope and application of the measure in question and imposing
a minimum of requirements so that the persons whose data has been retained have guarantees
sufficient to effectively protect their personal data against the risk of misuse as well as
only against any unlawful access and use of such data.

Therefore, insofar as the data processing implemented by the Grand-Ducal Police within the framework
of the bill and the draft Grand-Ducal regulation constitute an interference with the right to respect for the
privacy of candidates for police careers, the bill and the draft Grand-Ducal regulation should
provide a more specific framework for such processing in accordance with the case law of the European Court of
human rights. This legal framework would also guarantee the principle of legal certainty for the benefit
of the persons concerned, candidates for police careers. Legal certainty is even a principle
general law of the European Union, requiring in particular that a regulation entailing consequences
adverse to individuals is clear and precise and its application predictable for litigants. There
regulation must allow interested parties to know exactly the extent of the obligations it imposes on them.
imposes, must allow them to know without ambiguity their rights and obligations as well as allow them to
make their arrangements accordingly.

In addition, the protection of personal data constitutes at the national level a matter reserved for the
law insofar as it relates to the protection of the privacy of citizens (Article 11 paragraph (3) of the Constitution). In
under article 32, paragraph (3), of the Constitution, in the said matters reserved to the law by the Constitution,
“The Grand Duke can only issue regulations and decrees by virtue of a specific legal provision which sets,
in addition to the objectives, the principles and essential points of the implementing measures. »88.

The essential elements89, the objectives and the principles90 must therefore be included in the law in the strict sense of the term.

Thus, and although the investigation of morality has a legal basis, as provided for by article 58 of the amended law
of 18 July 2018 on the Grand Ducal Police, the National Commission notes that the terms and conditions
of the processing implemented during the morality investigation are not specified in the aforementioned article 58, such as

5ANNUAL REPORT2020 CNPDANNEXES amended by article 1 of the bill, while this investigation constitutes an interference in the right to respect for

2. On the investigation of morality

The National Commission welcomes that the morality investigation is provided for by a legal basis, as provided for in
Article 58 of the amended law of 18 July 2018 on the Grand Ducal Police. Just as she welcomes the fact that the article
58 cited above, as amended by article 1 of the bill, now specifies the consequences of failure to
moral qualities necessary for the performance of one of the functions of the police officer. However, and as explained below
before, some clarifications should be made.

To. On the lack of definition of the moral qualities required by candidates for one of the functions of the

police officer in law

Indeed, in the absence of definition of the moral qualities required by the candidate for one of the functions of the executive
policeman, it is difficult to grasp the contours and the scope of the investigation of morality.

In addition, the lack of definition of the moral qualities required by candidates for one of the executive functions
police officer, which is nevertheless one of the essential conditions for their admission to the internship, does not respect the requirements
precision and predictability that a legal text must meet. A law must be sufficiently clear and precise
in order to allow the persons concerned to know the extent of the limitations, as well as the consequences
possible for them.

i. Principles identified by administrative case law

If article 58 of the amended law of 18 July 2018 on the Grand-Ducal Police, as amended by the draft
law, remains silent as to the definition of the notions of moral qualities, it should be noted that case law
administration provides some answers as to what such notions cover.

It follows in particular from the principles identified by administrative case law that the morality of a candidate
to one of the functions of the police officer is assessed on the basis of broad criteria which are not based solely
on the assessment of the candidate's criminal record (for example the candidate's state of mind, his sense of
responsibilities)92. This is also corroborated by the parliamentary work relating to Bill No. 7045
on the reform of the Grand Ducal Police which specify in parliamentary document 7045/0993 that: "the
police officers are responsible for ensuring internal security and are therefore equipped with the means of constraint, which justifies
that their moral conditions are not assessed on the basis of the criminal record alone”.

91 See among others CourEDH, Zakharov e. Russia [GCL n°47413/06, § 228-229, December 4, 2015.
92 Indeed, administrative case law specifies that the notion of morality implies an overall assessment “of the moral qualities of a candidate for the examination and
in particular their state of mind, their sense of responsibility, their social abilities, their attitude towards respecting the standards of the country, given that the
members of the Grand Ducal Police have the mission of ensuring internal security by ensuring the maintenance of public order and the respect and execution
laws and regulations. ". See in this sense judgment of April 2, 2019 of the Administrative Tribunal of the Grand Duchy of Luxembourg, 4th chamber, number 39804 of the roll,
and judgment of January 18, 2019 of the Administrative Tribunal of the Grand Duchy of Luxembourg, 4th chamber, roll number 41619.

93 Pages 29 and 30.

159

ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPD It also emerges from administrative case law in this area that the assessment of a candidate's morality
must be done in concreto94 and falls within the discretionary power of the Grand Ducal Police95.

Consequently, insofar as it is not the law that defines the elements on which the investigation of morality or
what are the criteria or the degree of seriousness of the antecedents taken into account but the administrative jurisprudence and
this while the investigation of morality constitutes an undeniable interference in the right to privacy and protection
candidate data, the National Commission considers it necessary that Article 58, as referred to in the draft
law, reflects the principles established by administrative case law regarding the notion of moral quality.

Indeed, the current text lacks transparency and does not allow the candidate to know what the criteria or
what degree of seriousness of his background, if any, are taken into account by the Grand-Ducal Police, in order to
to assess whether he has the necessary moral qualities. During this investigation, is the Grand-Ducal Police
have to consult state files, internal files of the Grand Ducal Police or other files?
An enumeration of the files consulted, rather than the current formula used96, would allow the candidate to have a
better understanding of the conditions he must meet in order to access one of the functions of the police officer.

Furthermore, by way of example, it is necessary to draw the attention of the authors of the bill to the fact that the law of June 15
Exhibit Classification and Security Clearances 2004, as amended, provides for the terms
the security investigation to which State officials and State employees assigned to the Security Service are subject
information, in order to check in particular whether they have the guarantees of morality. Although this law does not define
not the notion of "guarantee of morality", it is however more precise with regard to access to databases.
data consulted as part of the security investigation and the processing of data collected during such
investigation (see Articles 21 to 23 of the aforementioned law).

ii. The national legal framework compared to that in Belgium and France

In the context of the recruitment of police officers, it is interesting to note that both Belgian and French law provide
a condition similar to that required by article 58 of the amended law of July 18, 2018 on the Grand-Police
ducale namely that the candidate for one of the functions of the police officer must have the "moral qualities
necessary for the performance of one of the functions of the police officer”.

Indeed, in Belgium, a candidate for one of the posts of the operational framework within the police must in particular "be
irreproachable conduct and not present risk factors that constitute an obstacle to the commitment to the
font”97. The text of Belgian law further specifies that the aforementioned conditions result from: “a) a certified copy
complete criminal record dating from less than three months on the date of submission of the application;
b) an investigation of the environment and background, including in particular an interview with the candidate at the residence
and at the place of possible residence of the latter, carried out by the local police force; c) all the information

94 Case law states that: “the power to appoint is not required to take into consideration only criminally qualified facts directly related to the
function to be exercised, just as he is not bound by the assessment of certain facts made by correctional judges, in that the objective of his intervention is different
that of the latter who have to sanction reprehensible behavior within the meaning of the law, whereas the minister must ensure, through an assessment in concreto,
compliance with the conditions set by law for police candidates, particularly from the point of view of their morality. ". See in this sense judgment of April 2
2019 of the Administrative Tribunal of the Grand Duchy of Luxembourg, 4th chamber, number 39804 of the roll, and judgment of January 18, 2019 of the Administrative Tribunal of
Grand Duchy of Luxembourg, 4th chamber, roll number 41619.

95 Judgment of 2 April 2019 of the Administrative Tribunal of the Grand Duchy of Luxembourg.
96 Article 58 of the amended law of 18 July 2018 provides that the Grand-Ducal Police “may consult the files which are legally accessible to it and provided that

this consultation is relevant for the purpose sought”.

97 Article 12, 3° of the Law of 26 April 2002 relating to the essential elements of the status of members of the staff of the police services and laying down various other

policing provisions.

5ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPDANNEXES transmitted by the intelligence and security services and by the Coordination Body for
threat analysis; d) all available information relating to administrative sanctions
municipal penalties imposed for a mixed offence; e) judicial data, communicated by the services
police, with the authorization of the competent judicial authorities; f) other data and information
validated available to the police. »98.

Ministerial decree of 28 December 2001 implementing certain provisions of the royal decree of 30 March
2001 on the legal position of police service personnel (hereinafter the "Royal Decree of 30 March 2001")
clarifies the contours and scope of the environmental and background investigation. Section 3 entitled “The investigation of
environment and background” details the people who carry out the survey and specifies the list of databases
consulted.

In France, in the context of recruitment within the police, one of the conditions required by the candidate is
that the latter must not have "entries in bulletin no. 2 of his criminal record that are incompatible with
performance of duties”99. In addition, an administrative inquiry is also carried out, as provided for in
Article L.114-1 of the Internal Security Code which essentially provides that “Administrative decisions
recruitment, (…) concerning either public employment involved in the exercise of sovereignty missions
of the State, either public or private jobs in the field of security or defense (…) may
be preceded by administrative inquiries intended to verify that the behavior of natural persons or
morals concerned is not incompatible with the exercise of the functions or missions envisaged” and that
“These surveys may give rise to the consultation of automated processing of personal data.
personnel covered by article 31 of law n°78-17 of January 6, 1978 relating to data processing, files and
freedoms, with the exception of identification files. The conditions under which interested persons are
informed of this consultation are specified by decree. ".

According to article 31 of law n°78-17 of January 6, 1978 relating to data processing, files and freedoms,
the processing of personal data implemented on behalf of the State and which is of interest to the
State security, defense or public security, or have as their object the prevention, research, observation
or the prosecution of criminal offenses or the execution of criminal convictions or security measures,
thus including the processing carried out by the national police and gendarmerie services, must be authorized
by order of the competent minister(s), issued after publication of a reasoned opinion by the National Commission
IT and Liberties (the French counterpart of the CNPD). The said treatments are thus all framed
legally, such as the criminal history file100, the serial analysis file101 or even
the file of wanted persons102.

Consequently and contrary to the current national legal framework, respectively to the draft law and draft regulation
grand-ducal under examination, it should be noted that both Belgian law and French law provide a framework

In order to meet the requirements of precision and predictability that a legal text must meet, the CNPD
recommends that the authors of the bill and the draft Grand-Ducal regulation draw inspiration from it.

b. On access to files which are legally accessible to the Grand-Ducal Police during the investigation of

morality

The National Commission welcomes the fact that Article 58 of the amended law of July 18, 2018 provides that the Police
Grand-Ducale “may consult the files which are legally accessible to it and provided that this consultation
is relevant to the purpose sought”.

The CNPD understands that the Grand Ducal Police therefore has access to all the files referred to in
Article 43 of the amended law of 18 July 2018 on the Grand Ducal Police. In the absence of details about the
categories of data contained in such files and which would actually be consulted within the framework of
morality investigation, the National Commission cannot however assess whether such access is justified and
proportionate to the purpose sought, namely the recruitment of a candidate for one of the functions of the
police officer. This is all the more so since the access provided for to the files listed in article 43 of the amended law
of 18 July 2018 on the Grand Ducal Police are for different purposes, namely in the exercise of
judicial and administrative police missions of members of the Police having the status of judicial officer or
administrative police officer.

In addition, the CNPD considers that the bill should specify the possible access of the Grand Ducal Police to its
own files, following the example of Belgian and French laws. This is all the more so in view of the recent concerns of citizens
regarding respect for public freedoms and the protection of their personal data in the police and
judiciary, it is therefore important that clarifications on access to the aforementioned files and systems be
found at the level of the law in the formal sense.

vs. On the criteria to be taken into account when assessing the moral qualities of the candidate

The National Commission welcomes the fact that the authors of the bill have specified in Article 1 of the bill which
modifies article 58 of the amended law of 18 July on the Grand Ducal Police what would be the consequences of
the candidate's lack of moral qualities. Indeed, when such qualities are lacking, the candidate is not
admitted to the course.

However, no details as to the criteria taken into account by the Grand Ducal Police when assessing
of the moral qualities of the candidate does not appear in the said article or in the comments of the authors of the project
of law. However, it is necessary that the criteria taken into account or the degree of seriousness of the history taken into

162

5ANNUAL REPORT2020 CNPDANNEXES account by the Grand Ducal Police are specified beforehand in order to draw the consequences, namely the refusal
the admission of the candidate to the internship.
 
The National Commission also refers to its developments under point I. 2. A. of this opinion.

d. On the retention period of personal data

It should be recalled that pursuant to Article 5 paragraph (1) letter e) of the GDPR, personal data
must not be kept longer than necessary for the achievement of the purposes for which they
are collected and processed.

The National Commission regrets that the draft law does not indicate the retention period for the data collected
relating to a candidate and therefore wonders what criteria would be used to determine such a duration.
Clarifications on this subject deserve to be provided by the authors of the bill.

Therefore, the CNPD is not in a position to assess whether the principle of limited data retention period is
respected.

3. Final remarks

Finally, it should be noted that on the basis of the current wording of article 58 of the amended law of 18 July
2018, the National Commission does not have all the information it needs in order to
enable a full assessment of whether the processing implemented as part of the morality investigation is
GDPR compliant.

Moreover, in the absence of details, certain questions remain unanswered: what are the categories of
data collected as part of these morality surveys? The right to information (articles 13 and 14 of the GDPR)
of the persons concerned is it respected during the implementation of the processing generated by the investigation of
morality?

In addition, the National Commission notes that in order to access certain professions,
background checks or investigations of character or good repute or even administrative investigations.
Such investigations or checks are carried out in particular to:

• persons subject to the background check procedure provided for in Chapter 3 of the draft
of the Grand-Ducal regulation relating to civil aviation security and the conditions of access to the airport of
Luxemburg;

163

CNPD 2020 ANNUAL REPORT • State officials and State employees assigned to the State Intelligence Service. A survey of

security is taken against them in order to verify in particular whether they have the guarantees of morality103;

• the candidate for a state employee position who must offer the “required guarantees of morality”104;
• candidates for the competitive examination for legal attachés. Candidates to be admitted to the examination-competition

must “provide the required guarantees of good repute”105;

• the agent who wishes to carry out the activity of guarding and surveillance. This must fulfill the conditions

of good repute106;

• persons requesting the granting of an authorization, approval or license in the field of weapons107.

Although this list is not exhaustive, it already makes it possible to realize the disparities that exist
under Luxembourg law with regard to investigations or background checks that would be carried out by
public administrations or authorities for access to certain professions.

For the sake of consistency, the National Commission wonders whether it would not be appropriate to harmonize
all of these procedures, for example using similar terminologies. Indeed, each of the laws
special use their own terminology, while certain notions seem to overlap. Such is the
case for example with the notions of honourability and morality. The use of a common terminology would allow
thus to ensure consistency in national law between these various disparate special laws which pursue
yet a similar purpose.

II. On the draft Grand-Ducal regulation

The purpose of the draft Grand-Ducal regulation is to specify the special stages organized by the
Grand Ducal Police must be passed for candidates wishing to access police careers in order to hold
account of the changes in terms of recruitment within the Police following the alignment of the procedures for
the examination-competition for access to police careers in the general framework of the Public Service.

In order to have a better reading of the regulatory text relating to recruitment in police careers, it is
It has been decided not to modify the Grand-Ducal regulation of 17 August 2018 on: 1° setting the conditions
recruitment of police personnel; 2° repeal of the Grand-Ducal regulation of 31 October 2001
determining the national services and international organizations in which police personnel can be
employed by order of the existing Government (hereinafter the “Grand-Ducal regulation of 17 August 2018”), but of
repeal it and replace it with the draft Grand-Ducal regulation under consideration.

The National Commission intends to limit its observations to the questions raised by the provisions of the draft
Grand-Ducal regulation under examination which deals with aspects related to respect for private life and the protection of
personal data.

52020 CNPD ANNUAL REPORTANNEXES 1. On the morality investigation

Due to the repeal of the Grand-Ducal Regulation of 17 August 2018, it should be noted that this
draft Grand-Ducal regulation does not provide as a condition of eligibility for the special test
for admission to the internship that the candidate offers the "guarantees of morality required in view of the investigation of
morality ordered by the Minister", contrary to what is specified in the Grand-Ducal regulation of 17 August
2018108.

However, the CNPD notes that this condition is intended to be maintained, as is apparent from Article 1 of the
bill under consideration. Since this condition is included in the draft law, it is therefore not necessary for it to be
also appears in the draft Grand-Ducal regulation.

2. On the medical examination

It follows from article 23 of the draft Grand-Ducal regulation under opinion that the candidate before each admission to the
internship is subject to an in-depth medical examination, as detailed in article 24 of the said draft.

It should be recalled that the personal data collected as part of these examinations are to be
qualify as sensitive data within the meaning of Article 9 of the GDPR, because they relate to the health of the candidates.
The processing of such data therefore requires specific protection109 and is subject to specific requirements.
more stringent.

The National Commission welcomes the fact that the authors of the draft Grand-Ducal regulation have specified in detail the
medical examinations to which candidates are subjected. Just as it is pleased that the criteria for incapacity to
such examinations are specified in Appendix A of the draft Grand-Ducal regulation.

In addition, the CNPD understands that the doctor from the Occupational Health Division of the public sector, the Administration
medical services of the public sector, in charge of such medical examinations, will transmit only to the service
competent authority of the Grand Ducal Police the information that the candidate is fit or unfit in accordance with
Article 3 of the Grand-Ducal Regulation of 5 March 2004 concerning health and safety at work and the control
service in the public service, as amended.

3. On access to the national register of natural persons

Paragraph (3) of Article 19 of the draft Grand-Ducal regulation lists the documents that must be produced
by the candidate when applying for the special stage.

108 See articles 2 point 3°, 5 point 4° and 15 point 5° of the Grand-Ducal regulation of 17 August 2018 with regard to the conditions of eligibility for the examination-competition.
109 See the cases rendered by the CJEU of 8 April 1992, C-62-90, point 23 and of 5 October 1994, C-404/92, point 17.

ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPD The last paragraph of the paragraph of the aforementioned article, however, provides that the latter “does not need to provide
a copy of his identity card or an extract of the birth certificate when the data concerning his name and
first name(s), date of birth and nationality are qualified as correct in the national register of persons
physical and if he has his habitual residence in the Grand Duchy of Luxembourg”.

The National Commission understands that the Grand Ducal Police is therefore verifying the information
aforementioned of the candidate in the national register of natural persons.

However, if article 43, 1° of the amended law of 18 July 2018 on the Grand-Ducal Police provides for access to this register to
members of the Grand Ducal Police with the status of judicial police officer or administrative police officer
within the framework of the exercise of their missions of judicial police and administrative police, it is necessary to note
that access to such data in the present case does not take place within the same framework but takes place within the
recruitment (see our developments in point I. 2. c. of this notice).

Access to this file for the purpose of recruiting candidates for police careers is therefore not provided for in Article
43 of the amended law of 18 July 2018 on the Grand Ducal Police. This purpose should therefore be
provided for in the aforementioned article.
