52020 ANNUAL REPORT CNPDANNEXES Opinion relating to bill no. 7634 amending the law of July 17, 2020 introducing
of a series of measures to combat the Covid-19 pandemic and amending: 1° the law
amended of November 25, 1975 concerning the delivery of medicines to the public;
2° the amended law of 11 April 1983 regulating the placing on the market and
drug advertising.

(Deliberation n° 18/2020 of July 21, 2020)

In accordance with Article 57, paragraph 1, letter (c) of Regulation No. 2016/679 of April 27, 2016 relating to
the protection of individuals with regard to the processing of personal data and the freedom
movement of such data, and repealing Directive 95/46/EC (general data protection regulation)
(hereinafter “the GDPR”), to which article 7 of the law of 1 August 2018 on the organization of the Commission refers
national data protection system and the general data protection regime, the Commission
Commission for Data Protection (hereinafter referred to as “the National Commission” or “the CNPD”)
“advises, in accordance with the law of the Member State, the national parliament, the government and other institutions
and organizations regarding legislative and administrative measures relating to the protection of the rights and freedoms of
natural persons with regard to the processing”.

On July 20, 2020, the Minister of Health seized the National Commission to rule on the
bill no. 7634 amending the law of 17 July 2020 introducing a series of measures to combat
the Covid-19 pandemic and amending: 1° the amended law of 25 November 1975 concerning the delivery to the public
medication ; 2° the amended law of 11 April 1983 regulating the placing on the market and the
drug advertising (hereinafter the "Bill").

After analyzing the bill, the CNPD finds that the provisions concerning the protection of the rights and
freedoms of natural persons with regard to the processing of personal data, and more specifically
articles 5 and 10 of the said bill, have not changed with respect to the corresponding provisions of the law
currently in force from 17 July 2020 introducing a series of measures to combat the pandemic
Covid-19.

Nevertheless, the CNPD wishes to make a remark concerning the starting point of the retention period of the
personal data contained in the information system set up by the Director of Health in order to
to follow the evolution of the spread of the SARS-CoV-2 virus.

Initially, bill n°7606 became the law of June 24, 2020 introducing a series of measures
concerning natural persons in the context of the fight against the Covid-19 pandemic provided in its article

167

2020 CNPD ANNUAL REPORT 9 paragraph (5) that the aforementioned data “shall be anonymised no later than six months after the law ceases to apply.
produce its effects. »
 
In its opinion n°13/2020 of June 8, 2020 relating to said bill n°7606, the CNPD wondered what
were the health and/or scientific reasons which led the authors of the bill to insert a duration of
specific conservation of 6 months after the future law will cease to produce its effects and it had noted that
the legislators of the countries neighboring Luxembourg had opted in this context for retention periods
much shorter.

Following the amendments adopted by the Health and Sports Committee on June 11, 2020, it was held
account of the opinion of the CNPD and the retention period has been reduced from 6 to 3 months. Thus, Article 9 paragraph
(5) of Bill No. 7606 read as follows: "Without prejudice to the provisions of paragraph 6 of this
article, their conservation in a form allowing the identification of the persons is limited to the duration necessary
to prevent and combat Covid-19 and the data is anonymized no later than three months after the law
ceases to produce its effects. »

Following the additional opinion of the CNPD of June 16, 2020,110 additional amendments had been
adopted by the Health and Sports Commission.

On the proposal of the opinion of the Council of State of June 16, 2020, the provision in question has been modified in this sense
that the new article 8 (old article 9) paragraph (5) of bill no. 7606 read as follows “the
personal data processed are anonymized after a period of three months from the date of
end of the state of crisis as declared by the amended Grand-Ducal regulation of 18 March 2020 introducing
of a series of measures in the context of the fight against Covid-19 and extended by the law of March 24, 2020 on
extension of the state of crisis declared by the Grand-Ducal regulation of 18 March 2020 introducing a
series of measures in the fight against Covid-19. »

Like the repealed law of 24 June 2020 introducing a series of measures concerning
natural persons in the context of the fight against the Covid-19 pandemic and the law currently in force
of 17 July 2020 introducing a series of measures to combat the Covid-19 pandemic, the project
of law under opinion provides in its article 10 paragraph (5) that "the personal data processed are
anonymized at the end of a period of three months from the end of the state of crisis as declared by the
amended Grand-Ducal regulation of 18 March 2020 introducing a series of measures within the framework of the
fight against Covid-19 and extended by the law of March 24, 2020 extending the state of crisis declared by
the Grand-Ducal regulation of 18 March 2020 introducing a series of measures as part of the fight against
against Covid-19. »
 

110 Deliberation n°14/2020 of June 16, 2020.

168

52020 CNPD ANNUAL REPORTANNEXES The CNPD must admit that in its opinion n°16/2020 of July 8, 2020 concerning bill n°7622 which became
the aforementioned law of July 17, 2020 introducing a series of measures to combat the Covid-19 pandemic,
he escaped raising this point. However, as the law of March 24, 2020 extended the state of crisis declared by
the Grand-Ducal regulation of 18 March 2020 introducing a series of measures within the framework of the
fight against Covid-19 until June 24, 2020, omitting to modify this point would have the consequence that the
the aforementioned personal data must be anonymized by September 24, 2020, i.e. three
months from the end of the state of crisis. This would mean that as Article 18 of the bill under consideration
provides that the future law remains applicable until September 30, 2020 inclusive, the personal data
should be anonymized before the end of the applicability of the law. Furthermore, no more personal data
staff could not be processed by the health department from September 24, 2020.

The CNPD assumes that this situation was not desired by the authors of the bill. Thus, she suggests
foresee as a starting point, in terms of the duration after which the data will have to be anonymized,
the date of data collection or at least the day on which the future law will cease to have effect.
 
Thus decided in Esch-sur-Alzette on July 21, 2020.

2020 ANNUAL REPORT CNPD Opinion on bill no. 7524 on the quality of services for people
elderly and amending: 1° the amended law of 16 May 1975 on the status of
co-ownership of built buildings; 2° the amended law of 8 September 1998 regulating
relations between the State and organizations working in the social and family fields
and therapy as well as the draft Grand-Ducal regulation on the quality of
services for the elderly.

(Deliberation n°19/2020 of 07/22/2020)

In accordance with Article 57, paragraph 1, letter (c) of Regulation No. 2016/679 of April 27, 2016 relating to
the protection of individuals with regard to the processing of personal data and the freedom
movement of such data, and repealing Directive 95/46/EC (general data protection regulation)
(hereinafter “the GDPR”), to which article 7 of the law of 1 August 2018 on the organization of the Commission refers
national data protection system and the general data protection regime, the Commission
Commission for Data Protection (hereinafter referred to as “the National Commission” or “the CNPD”)
“advises, in accordance with the law of the Member State, the national parliament, the government and other institutions
and organizations regarding legislative and administrative measures relating to the protection of the rights and freedoms of
natural persons with regard to the processing”.

By letter dated February 6, 2020, the Minister for Family and Integration invited the Commission
to rule on draft law no. 7524 amending: 1° the amended law of 16 May 1975
on the status of the co-ownership of buildings constructed; 2° the amended law of 8 September 1998 regulating relations
between the State and organizations working in the social, family and therapeutic fields (hereinafter the “draft
law”) as well as on the draft Grand-Ducal regulation on the quality of services for the elderly
(“draft Grand-Ducal regulation”).

According to the explanatory memorandum, the purpose of this bill is to overhaul the section concerning the
the elderly of the law of September 8, 1998 regulating the relations between the State and the organizations working in the
social, family and therapeutic areas (hereinafter the “ASFT law”). The authors of the bill further specify
that the purpose of the bill is to “create a new legal framework intended for bodies managing services
and structures for the elderly which will supplement the provisions relating to the ASFT law”. The text under
avis thus organizes the action of the management bodies intervening in the fields of active ageing,
home care and long-stay accommodation for the elderly. The objective pursued by
the bill is to clarify the terminology and complete the concepts used in relation to the regulations
current.

170

5ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPDANNEXES The National Commission intends to limit its observations to the questions raised by the provisions of the draft
law under examination which deal with aspects related to respect for private life and the protection of personal data
staff. This opinion will not deal with the draft Grand-Ducal regulation because it does not raise any
observation from a personal data protection and privacy perspective.

I. On the processing of personal data carried out by the Minister

1. On the creation of a new public register

According to the explanatory memorandum, the bill provides for the creation of a “register of structures and services for people
who will make public all information deemed relevant to the attention of the general public, in particular the
project of establishment and the standard contract concluded with the users”.

This new public register is created under the authority of the Minister having in his competences the law under opinion (here-
after the “minister”) and contains 7 headings entitled: “accommodation structures for the elderly”111,
“home help and care services”112, “day centers for the elderly”113, “Aktiv Plus clubs”114,
“meals on wheels services”115, “senior activity services”116 and “remote alarm services”117. The bill lists
for each of the aforementioned headings, all the information contained therein.

It should be noted that the said register will make public information which does not essentially constitute
personal data. Only information relating to the name of the manager and his
delegates, if any, constitute personal data.

The publication of such personal data does not raise any difficulty from the point of view of the application.
of the GDPR while this publication would be made under a legal obligation, which constitutes Articles 8,
22, 36, 49, 58, 68 and 77 of the bill.

2. On the processing of personal data carried out by the Minister in the context of requests

of approval addressed to him

The National Commission understands from reading the bill that the Minister is required to collect and process
personal data in the context of requests for approval addressed to it.

Indeed, the bill specifies that the application for approval, necessary for the exercise of each of the services
covered by the bill, is sent by the managing bodies to the Minister, and is accompanied by a
accreditation file which includes a number of documents and information118. These documents and
information contains personal data.

111 Article 8 of the bill.
112 Article 22 of the bill
113 Article 36 of the bill.
114 Article 49 of the bill.
115 Article 58 of the bill.
116 Article 68 of the bill.
117 Article 77 of the bill.
118 See Article 15 for “Services and accommodation structures for the elderly” (chapter 1); Article 29 for “Home help and care services”
(chapter 2) ; Article 43 for “Day centers for the elderly” (Chapter 3); Article 53 for “Aktiv Plus Clubs” (Chapter 4); section 62 for
“Meal Services on Wheels” (Chapter 5); Article 72 for “Services for senior citizens” (chapter 6); and article 84 for “Remote alarm services” (chapter 7).

171

ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPD The National Commission therefore wonders whether the Minister is not required to keep a file centralizing all
data collected and processed as part of the authorization requests sent to it. If this should be the
case, it should be remembered that keeping a file of personal data collected and processed by a
administrative authority must have a legal basis in accordance with Article 6, paragraph (3) of the GDPR119.

This article provides for a specific constraint related to the lawfulness of data processing necessary for compliance with a
legal obligation or the performance of a task in the public interest or in the exercise of official authority, of which
is vested with the controller. In these two cases, the basis and purposes of the processing of
data must specifically be defined either by European Union law or by the law of the Member State
to which the controller is subject.

In addition, recital (45) of the GDPR specifies that it should “[…] belong to Union law or to European law.
of a Member State to determine the purpose of the processing. Furthermore, this law could specify the conditions
general rules of this Regulation governing the lawfulness of the processing of personal data, establish the
specifications aimed at determining the controller, the type of personal data making
the purpose of the processing, the persons concerned, the entities to which the personal data can be
be disclosed, purpose limitations, retention periods and other measures to ensure
lawful and fair processing. […]”.

Pursuant to the abovementioned provisions, these legal bases should establish specific provisions aimed at
to determine, among other things, the types of data processed, the persons concerned, the entities to which the
data may be communicated and for what purposes, the data retention periods or even
processing operations and procedures.

The National Commission therefore considers it essential, in the event that the Minister keeps a file, that it
be provided for in this bill. The legal provisions establishing such a register must contain the
items mentioned in the paragraph above.

In addition, the CNPD already intends to make observations in the developments below as to the
processing of personal data carried out by the Minister in the context of applications for approval which
are addressed to him.

To. On the purposes of the processing

In accordance with the principle of purpose limitation, personal data must be collected for purposes
specified, explicit and legitimate purposes, and not to be further processed in a manner incompatible with
these purposes.

119 Article 6 paragraph (3), read together with its paragraph (1) letters c) and e), provides that: “The basis for the processing referred to in paragraph 1, points c) and e),

is defined by:
To. Union law; Or
b. the law of the Member State to which the controller is subject.
 

The purposes of the processing are defined in this legal basis or, with regard to the processing referred to in paragraph 1, point e), are necessary to
the performance of a task in the public interest or in the exercise of official authority vested in the controller. This legal basis can
contain specific provisions to adapt the application of the rules of this Regulation, among others: the general conditions governing the lawfulness of the
processing by the controller; the types of data that are subject to processing; the people concerned; the entities to which the data to
personal character can be communicated and the purposes for which they can be communicated; purpose limitation; retention periods; and the
processing operations and procedures, including measures to ensure lawful and fair processing, such as those provided for in other situations
special treatment as provided for in Chapter IX. ".

172

5ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPDANNEXES Although the draft law and the commentary to the articles do not expressly specify the purposes pursued
by the Minister, the CNPD understands that the personal data collected and processed by the Minister on
are for licensing and management purposes. In accordance with the principle of purpose limitation, such
data could not be used by the Minister for purposes other than those mentioned above.

b. On the categories of personal data and data subjects

The managing bodies, as defined in Chapters 1 to 7 of this bill, must attach to their
requests approval of the documents and information listed by the bill120.

The CNPD welcomes such a list which specifies, for each document or information covered by the project
of law, the persons concerned as well as the categories of data relating thereto.

Nevertheless, it appears from the provisions of the bill that the Minister "may request any other document
or information essential to the establishment of the file of the application for approval”121. Authors
of the bill specify on this subject in the comments of the articles that "The Minister reserves the right
to request any other document to verify the proper functioning and the non-endangerment of the
residents”122 without however specifying what types of documents it could be and the persons concerned
by these additional measures. If this "essential document or information" contains data to be
personal nature then the CNPD considers it necessary that details on this subject be provided in the draft
of law.

Furthermore, if the Minister were in this case to indirectly collect data relating to the bodies
managers, executives or supervisory staff from other state files, then such
communication of data between ministries or administrations should be specified in the text of the draft law.

vs. On access to data

In accordance with Article 5 paragraph (1), letter f) of the GDPR, personal data must be
“processed in a way that ensures appropriate security of personal data, including the protection
against unauthorized or unlawful processing and against accidental loss, destruction or damage,
using appropriate technical or organizational measures (integrity and confidentiality)”.

In addition, Article 32 of the GDPR provides that “the controller and the processor shall implement the
appropriate technical and organizational measures to guarantee a level of security appropriate to the risk”.
Such measures must be implemented in order to avoid in particular unauthorized access to data or
data leaks.

(chapter 2) ; Article 43 for “Day centers for the elderly” (Chapter 3); Article 53 for “Aktiv Plus Clubs” (Chapter 4); section 62 for
“Meal Services on Wheels” (Chapter 5); Article 72 for “Services for senior citizens” (chapter 6); and article 84 for “Remote alarm services” (chapter 7).

CNPD 2020 ANNUAL REPORT Among these security measures, the National Commission considers it important that only people who have them
necessary in the exercise of their functions and professional tasks are entitled to have access to the
necessary data. The CNPD therefore recommends that provision be made within the ministry having the bill under
notice in its competence that access to such data is limited to only agents with a need to know.
as part of their function. It would also be appropriate to provide for the modalities of this access and to put in place
a procedure including appropriate safeguards aimed at excluding any use going beyond the purposes
for which these data are initially processed and in particular, provide for a logging system (i.e.
i.e. a recording in “log files” or “logs”) of user activities, anomalies and
security-related events.

d. On the data retention period

According to Article 5 paragraph (1), letter e) of the GDPR, personal data must not be stored
longer than necessary to achieve the purposes for which they are collected and processed.

The National Commission regrets that the authors of the bill did not indicate the retention periods
data processed for the purposes of granting and managing approvals, so that it is not able
to assess whether, in this case, the principle of limited data retention period is respected with regard to the
collection of this data.

II. On the processing of personal data carried out by the managing bodies

1. On the processing of personal data relating to the condition of professional good repute

managers and supervisory staff

It should be noted that the managing bodies responsible for the management and operation of the
structures or activities, as targeted by the bill, cannot employ for the positions of charge of
management and supervisory staff only persons who meet the conditions set by the bill for
occupying such positions.

One of the conditions that must be met by the manager and the supervisory staff is in particular
the condition of “professional integrity” which “is assessed on the basis of his background insofar as they
relate to facts dating back no more than ten years”123 124.

The bill further specifies with regard to this condition that: "constitutes a breach depriving the
responsible for managing professional integrity, any behavior or action that so seriously affects his
professional integrity that can no longer be tolerated, in the interest of the residents concerned, that he exercises or continues to

123 The same wording is repeated identically by articles 4, 18, 33, 47, 57, 66 and 75 of the bill with regard to executive officers.
124 The same wording is repeated identically by articles 5, 19, 34, 48, and 67 of the bill with regard to supervisory staff.

174

5ANNUALREPORT2020 CNPDANNEXES perform the function authorized or to be authorized”125 and that: “constitutes a breach depriving the agent of good repute
professional any behavior or action that affects his professional integrity so seriously that one does not
can no longer tolerate, in the interest of the residents [or users] concerned, that he exercises or continues to exercise the function
with which he is responsible.

In the absence of details in the bill and in the commentary to the articles, the CNPD wonders what
are the criteria for assessing such professional integrity.

If the condition of professional integrity is assessed on the basis of a criminal record, the CNPD includes
that this will be done in accordance with the legal provisions of article 8-5 of the law of July 23, 2016 on
amendment 1) of the law of 29 March 2013 on the organization of criminal records, 2) of the Code of instruction
criminal, 3) of the Penal Code. If this is the case, she suggests specifying in the text of the bill for more
clarity the term "legal" just after the term "antecedents". Furthermore, it would be important to specify which
degree of seriousness of the criminal record would be taken into account by the managing bodies in order to assess
the condition of professional integrity of the manager and the supervisory staff. The CNPD questions
in particular if any entry in the criminal record automatically leads to a negative assessment in terms of
of professional integrity or if, on the other hand, the registrations must have reached a certain level of seriousness.

If this condition of professional integrity is not limited to criminal records alone, the Commission
Nationale recommends specifying in the bill the elements to be taken into account to assess
professional integrity.

For example, the CNPD refers the authors of the bill to Chapter 3 entitled "Professional integrity".
of the amended law of September 2, 2011 regulating access to the professions of craftsman, merchant, industrialist
as well as certain liberal professions and the Grand-Ducal regulation of 1 December 2011 determining the
terms of the administrative instruction provided for in article 28 of the amended law of 2 September 2011. These
legal provisions specify the elements on which professional integrity is assessed.

Finally, the CNPD understands that it is up to the managing bodies to assess the condition of good repute
professionalism of the person in charge of management and the supervising personnel, because the latter must produce, during the
application for approval, a certificate attesting that the manager and the supervisory staff fulfill
this condition. However, she wonders if the Minister is not equally likely to appreciate such a
reputation on the basis of the provisions set out in paragraphs (3) of Articles 15, 29, 43, 53, 62, 72 and 84 of the
bill128. If this were the case, it should be provided for in the text of the bill.

Furthermore, the National Commission wonders about the continuity of the provisions of Article 8 of the
amended Grand-Ducal regulation of 8 December 1999 concerning the authorization to be granted to managers of

2. On the creation of an individual file

To. About the controller

The National Commission regrets that the draft law does not specify which entity will be responsible
of the processing for the processing of personal data carried out within the framework of the establishment of the
aforementioned individual file.

Nevertheless, the draft law provides for an obligation for the managing bodies in charge of the operation
accommodation services and facilities for the elderly, home help and care services, health centres,
day care for the elderly, and remote alarm services, to establish an individual file for each resident or user
such services129. Indeed, it appears from the commentary on the articles of the authors of the bill130 that
manager must establish an individual file for each resident or user.

The controller within the meaning of the GDPR therefore seems to be the managing body. The CNPD suggests,
therefore, that it be mentioned in all the articles relating to the individual file that the managing body
will be the data controller.

b. On the legal basis on which the processing is based

It should be recalled that any processing of personal data is only lawful if at least one of the
conditions referred to in Article 6, paragraph (1), letters a) to f) are fulfilled.

The processing of personal data carried out by the managing bodies, within the framework of the
creation of an individual file, is based on a legal obligation, introduced by the bill in articles 12, 26,
40 and 81, and raises no particular observation.

Furthermore, it should be noted that the data collected under Articles 12, paragraph (2),
point 7°, 26, paragraph (2), point 6°, 40, paragraph (2), point 8° and 81, paragraph (2), point 7° are at

129 See article 12 for accommodation services and structures for the elderly, article 26 for home help and care services, article 40 for

day centers for the elderly, article 81 for remote alarm services.

130 See comments on articles 12, 26, 40 and 81.

176

5ANNUAL REPORT2020 CNPDANNEXES qualify as sensitive data within the meaning of Article 9 of the GDPR, these being relating to the health of residents and
users.

Such processing requires specific protection131 and is subject to stricter requirements. The treatment
of sensitive data is, in fact, prohibited unless one of the conditions referred to in paragraph (2) of Article 9 of the
GDPR is fulfilled.

With regard to the processing of sensitive data referred to in the aforementioned articles of the bill, the CNPD considers
that the condition referred to in Article 9, paragraph (2), letter h) of the GDPR is fulfilled in the present case in the
to the extent that the individual file is put in place in order to ensure, in particular, the medical follow-up and the continuity of
care of the resident or user.

vs. On the purposes of the processing of personal data

It should be noted that the articles of the bill creating an individual file do not mention the
purposes of the processing of personal data.

However, it appears from the commentary to Article 12 that the purpose of the personal file is to facilitate “the creation and
monitoring of the resident's care plan as well as access to the resident's data and thus makes it possible to find,
at any time, all the historical elements concerning his career and his activities. The single individual file
ensures continuity of care by offering a common file accessible by the various stakeholders during the
care of the resident and allows the traceability of each action on his file with regard to the aspects
forensic. ". Therefore, with regard to Article 12, the CNPD understands that the purposes of the processing are
essentially the follow-up of the care of the resident.

In the absence of comments from the authors of the bill on this subject for articles 26, 40 and 81, the
CNPD wonders whether the same purposes are applicable for the individual files referred to in Articles
aforementioned.

The National Commission recommends that the purposes of the processing be indicated in Articles 12, 26, 40
and 81 taking into account each time the specificity of the services offered.

d. On the categories of personal data

The National Commission welcomes that the categories of personal data collected by the
managing bodies are precisely listed in the articles of the bill relating to the file
individual.

ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPD However, the National Commission assumes that the national identification number (matricule) of residents

e. On access to data

The National Commission welcomes that the draft law lists the persons and entities having access to
personal data contained in the personal file133. Just as it is pleased that the project
of law provides that the individual file is accessible to the persons and entities referred to in paragraphs (1) of the
articles 12, 26, 40 and 81 for the information referred to in paragraphs (2) of the said articles which concern them
in the exercise of their mission. It also appears from the bill that the Minister will not have access to such information.
folders.

With regard to access to the Long-Term Care Insurance Assessment and Control Administration, the CNPD
understands that the latter has access to the individual file within the framework of its control missions as detailed
in paragraph (1) of article 384bis.

Finally, with regard to access to the individual file for residents or users and, where applicable, their
legal representative with regard to their data, the National Commission wonders whether such access is
not similar to that provided for in Article 15 of the GDPR, which grants the data subject the right to access the
personal data that has been collected about him in order to become aware of the processing and to
verify legality.

If this is the case, it is suggested that the legal provisions providing that the resident or user, where applicable,
his legal representative, can access his personal file or only the data concerning him for the
legal representative under the conditions and in accordance with Article 15 of the GDPR.

f. On the shelf life

It should be recalled that pursuant to Article 5 paragraph (1) letter e) of the GDPR, personal data
must not be kept longer than necessary for the achievement of the purposes for which they
are collected and processed.

5ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPDANNEXES The bill provides for each individual file a retention period of 10 years from the date of
end of the hosting contract134, the care contract135 or the service contract136.

The CNPD assumes that for setting the retention period of 10 years, the authors of the draft
law wanted to align with the retention period for patient medical records provided for in Article 15 (4)
of the law of July 24, 2014 on the rights and obligations of the patient. However, in the absence of explanations from the authors
of the draft law in the commentary to the aforementioned articles as to the need to keep the data for
a period of 10 years, the CNPD is unable to assess whether this period is adequate and proportionate to the
purposes pursued.

g. On the security of the processing

It should be recalled that under Article 32 of the GDPR, the controller must implement the
appropriate measures to ensure a level of security appropriate to the risk.

It is particularly appropriate to draw the attention of the authors of the draft law to the fact that insofar as
sensitive data (health data) are to be processed, the managing bodies must put in place
puts in place protective measures to ensure the confidentiality and security of such data, including
a logging system (i.e. recording in “log files” or “logs”) of activities
users, anomalies and security-related events. Indeed, the disclosure of sensitive data
could cause serious harm to users or residents.

2020 ANNUAL REPORT CNPD Opinion on bill no. 7257 amending the amended law of 21
September 2006 on residential leases and amending certain provisions of the
Civil Code.

(Deliberation n° 20/2020 of 07/28/2020)

In accordance with Article 57, paragraph 1, letter (c) of Regulation No. 2016/679 of April 27, 2016 relating to
the protection of individuals with regard to the processing of personal data and the freedom
movement of such data, and repealing Directive 95/46/EC (general data protection regulation)
(hereinafter “the GDPR”), to which article 7 of the law of 1 August 2018 on the organization of the Commission refers
national data protection system and the general data protection regime, the Commission
Commission for Data Protection (hereinafter referred to as “the National Commission” or “the CNPD”)
“advises, in accordance with the law of the Member State, the national parliament, the government and other institutions
and organizations regarding legislative and administrative measures relating to the protection of the rights and freedoms of
natural persons with regard to the processing”.

By letter dated May 7, 2020, the Minister of Housing invited the National Commission
to decide on bill no. 7257 amending the amended law of September 21, 2006
on the lease for residential use and amending certain provisions of the Civil Code (hereinafter the “proposal
of law ").

The purpose of this bill is to amend the law of 21 September 2006 on leases for residential use
and amending certain provisions of the Civil Code (hereinafter the “Law of September 21, 2006”). Planned measures
by the bill are intended to deal with the housing crisis currently facing the Grand Duchy
of Luxemburg.

The main points of the bill are as follows:

• the introduction of a weighting coefficient (“rental reference value”) intended to limit the impact of

purchase price of rental properties on the legally possible rents;

• the obligation for lessors to include in any new lease contract the capital invested as well as the value of

rent reference;

• the redefinition of the concept of “luxury accommodation” appearing in the aforementioned law of September 21, 2006; And
• the creation of the national rent commission with national powers, in particular for the
collection of data relating to residential leases and for the subsequent setting of the “reference value
rent”.

180

5ANNUAL REPORT2020 CNPDANNEXES This opinion will limit its observations to Article 1 point 5° of the bill insofar as it creates a bank of
data which will be kept by the National Rent Commission, the only article relating to data protection
of a personal nature.

1. Preliminary remarks

Article 1 point 5° of the bill introduces a new paragraph (6) to article 3 of the law of 21
September 2006 which provides for the obligation for each lessor to include in any new lease contract the capital
invested as well as the rent reference value. The lessor or his representative must also send a
copy of the lease contract to the national rent commission which will record in a database
“subject to the provisions of the law of 2 August 2002 relating to the protection of individuals with regard to the processing
personal data”137 “the location of the accommodation, the type of accommodation, the surname, first name and
address of the owner, surname and first name of the tenant, the capital invested, that adjusted to the reference year 1995,
the revalued and discounted amount, the reference value of the rent and the amount of the rent”. Surname, first name and address
of the owner, and the surname and first name of the tenant constitute personal data within the meaning of Article
4, paragraph (1) GDPR.

The National Commission is pleased that the bill provides for the principle of the creation of such a bank
of data in accordance with Article 6, paragraph (3) of the GDPR138. This article provides, in fact, a constraint
particular related to the lawfulness of data processing necessary for compliance with a legal obligation or for the execution
a mission of public interest or relating to the exercise of public authority vested in the person responsible
of the treatment. In these two scenarios, the basis and purposes of the data processing must
specifically be provided for either by European Union law or by the law of the Member State to which the
controller is submitted.

However, and although the principle of creating such a database is provided for in the proposal for
law, the National Commission notes that certain elements relating to the processing of data are not (or not
sufficiently) specified in the bill.

Indeed, recital (45) of the GDPR specifies that it should “[…] belong to Union law or to European law.
of a Member State to determine the purpose of the processing. Furthermore, this law could specify the conditions
general rules of this Regulation governing the lawfulness of the processing of personal data, establish the
specifications aimed at determining the controller, the type of personal data making
the purpose of the processing, the persons concerned, the entities to which the personal data can be
be disclosed, purpose limitations, retention periods and other measures to ensure
lawful and fair processing. […]”. Pursuant to the aforementioned provisions, these legal bases should contain
specific provisions concerning, inter alia, the types of data processed, the persons concerned, the

137 The National Commission would like to point out that the law of 2 August 2002 on the protection of individuals with regard to the processing of personal data
was repealed by Article 72 of the Law of 1 August 2018 on the organization of the National Commission for Data Protection and the general regime on
data protection. It is therefore appropriate to refer to the legislation currently in force.

138 Article 6, paragraph (3) provides that the “legal basis may contain specific provisions to adapt the application of the rules of the Regulation, inter alia:
the general conditions governing the lawfulness of the processing by the controller; the people concerned ; the entities to which the personal data
may be disclosed and the purposes for which they may be disclosed; purpose limitation; retention periods; and operations
and processing procedures, including measures to ensure lawful and fair processing, such as those provided for in other specific situations of
treatment as provided for in Chapter IX”.

181

ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPD entities to which the data may be communicated and for what purposes, the retention periods of the
data or processing operations and procedures.

2. On the determination of the purposes of the processing

It should be noted that in accordance with Article 5 paragraph (1), letter b) of the GDPR, personal data
personal data must be “collected for specified, explicit and legitimate purposes, and not be processed
subsequently in a manner incompatible with those purposes ...”.

As set out above, Article 6 paragraph (3) of the GDPR, read together with its paragraph (1) letter c) and (e),
provides for a specific constraint related to the lawfulness of data processing necessary for compliance with an obligation
law or for the performance of a task in the public interest or in the exercise of official authority vested in
the controller. In these two cases, the basis and purposes of the data processing
must specifically be provided for either by European Union law or by the law of the Member State to which
the controller is subject.

Recital (41) of the GDPR further specifies that “this legal basis or legislative measure should be clear
and precise and its application should be foreseeable for the litigants, in accordance with the case-law of the Court
Justice of the European Union and the European Court of Human Rights. ".

However, it should be noted that the current wording of the bill does not comply with the aforementioned provisions
whereas the purposes of the processing referred to in the new article 3 paragraph (6) of the law of 21 September 2006
do not clearly result from the bill.

Details as to the purposes that would be pursued are also not provided by the authors of the
Law proposition. Indeed, the latter limit themselves to setting out, in their commentary on Article 1 point 5° of the
bill, the process by which the data will be collected by the National Rent Commission.

Thus, it is specified that: "a copy of each lease contract concluded or modified after the entry into force of
this law, is transmitted within one month following the conclusion by the lessor or his representative - by
example a real estate agent — at the national rent commission”, then “the national commission registers
the location of the accommodation, the type of accommodation, the last name, first name and address of the owner, the last name and first name
of the tenant, the capital invested, that adjusted to the reference year 1995, the revalued and discounted amount, the value
rental reference and the agreed rental amount in a database”. It is further specified that
“(…) the national commission verifies the copies of the lease contracts sent to them under these
provisions. If the National Commission finds that a contract does not meet the requirements of the
this law, it shall take the initiative to have the contractual terms in question rectified”.

182

52020 CNPD ANNUAL REPORTANNEXES The CNPD therefore understands that the personal data collected by the National Commission
rents via the copy of the lease contract would be for two distinct purposes, one would be to meet
to its mission of control and the second would be so that these data appear in the database
aforementioned.

It should also be noted that, according to the explanatory memorandum, the aforementioned database makes it possible to put "the
statistical data, anonymized, available to the Observatoire de l’Habitat, STATEC and other research centers
research and statistics, in order to improve the monitoring of the rental market”139.

Nevertheless, as noted by the Council of State in its opinion of April 28, 2020 relating to this proposal
of law: “The communication of personal data could still be understood, to a certain extent,
with the view that the National Rent Commission would be in charge of setting the weighting coefficients.
However, according to the authors of the bill, this fixing will belong to the National Institute of Statistics and Statistics.
economic studies. »140.

Therefore, like the Council of State, the CNPD considers it necessary that the purposes of the processing carried out
by the National Rent Commission are clearly specified in the text of the bill and considers
therefore essential that the authors of the bill indicate precisely which categories of data
are processed for what purposes.

This problem arises with all the more importance since, as the Council of State points out, the
bill “in addition provides for substantial criminal penalties in the event of a breach of the obligation
Communication ".

In the absence of clarification of the purposes pursued, the National Commission wonders how the
national rent commission in the context of the implementation of the processing of personal data
staff covered by this bill is able to determine whether it complies with the principles
data minimization and limitation of data retention while in accordance with the
paragraph (2) of Article 5 of the GDPR the controller “shall be responsible for compliance with the
paragraph (1)141 and is able to demonstrate that it is complied with" (see sections 3 and 5 of this
notice).

The CNPD can only approve the remarks of the Council of State in that it “cannot accept that, failing
clear determination of a specific purpose of the processing, the data can be stored and used by
the National Rent Commission beyond the immediate examination which it may carry out under Article 5
of the bill under review. »142.

140 Parliamentary document n°7257/05, page 4.
141 Article 5 paragraph (1) of the GDPR provides that: “(1) Personal data must be: (…)

c) adequate, relevant and limited to what is necessary in relation to the purposes for which they are processed (data minimization); (…)

183

2020 CNPD ANNUAL REPORT 3. On the principle of minimizing personal data

Article 5 paragraph (1), letter c) of the GDPR provides that personal data must be
“adequate, relevant and limited to what is necessary in relation to the purposes for which they are processed”.

It follows from this principle that only the data necessary for the fulfillment of the purpose should be processed.
of the treatment. In other words, it is a question of not giving access to more data than those necessary for the
national rent commission as part of the processing implemented.

However, in the absence of precision as to the purposes that would be pursued by the National Commission for Rents
in the context of the implementation of the processing of personal data referred to in Article 1 point
5° of the bill, the CNPD is unable to assess whether the categories of data listed in
the aforementioned article are adequate, relevant and limited to what is necessary with regard to these purposes. By
Consequently, the CNPD does not know whether the principle of data minimization would be respected in this case.

The National Commission would also like to emphasize the importance of this principle, since it is up to the legislator
to implement and concretely apply the principle of data minimization, otherwise the law will not
would not meet the requirement of precision and foreseeability that a legal text must meet without case law
of the European Court of Human Rights.

Although it is not possible for it to assess whether the principle of minimization would be respected, the CNPD is pleased that
point 5° of article 1 of the bill lists the personal data that will be included in the
the database144. However, it notes that it is not clear from the bill whether the copies
rental contract - which will undeniably contain other personal data than those listed
in Article 1 point 5° - are also required to appear in the database while lessors or
their representative are required to provide such a copy to the National Rent Commission, or if such
copies will appear in a separate database while the explanatory memorandum specifies that the commission
national “will centralize the lease contracts”.

Clarifications on this subject deserve to be provided by the authors of the bill.

4. Consultation of data contained in the database held by the National Commission

rents

The bill provides that "The database of data recorded by the National Commission for Rents
may be consulted for statistical and research purposes. The terms of consultation are set by
Grand-Ducal Regulation”.

143 See in particular ECHR, Libert c. France, 22 February 2018, paragraph 43.
144 According to article 1 point 5° of the bill, the national rent commission would collect “the location of the accommodation, the type of accommodation,
the surname, first name and address of the owner, the surname and first name of the tenant, the capital invested, that adjusted to the reference year 1995, the revalued amount and
discounted, the reference value of the rent and the amount of the rent” as well as the “(…) copy of each lease contract concluded or modified after the entry into force of
this law (…)”.

184

5ANNUAL REPORT2020 CNPDANNEXES The authors of the bill specify in the commentary to the articles that "The database
recorded by the National Rent Commission can be consulted for statistical and information purposes.
research, such as STATEC, the Housing Observatory, research centres, for example. To this end the
data is obviously made anonymous. The terms of consultation are set by general regulation.
ducal”.

In the absence of a draft Grand-Ducal regulation setting the procedures for consulting the bank of
data, the National Commission is not in a position to fully assess whether consultation of the data
contained in the “data bank registered by the National Rent Commission” complies with the
GDPR.

However, it would already like to point out that it appears from the comments of the authors of the bill
as to the provisions set out above, that only data made anonymous would be communicated to the
STATEC and the Observatoire de l’Habitat and “research centres”. The CNPD therefore understands that STATEC,
the Housing Observatory and the “research centres” (whose identity should be specified where necessary)
would not, in fact, have access to said database but only access to anonymized data
by the national rental commission.

To the extent that the comments of the authors of the bill differ from the legal provisions of
Article 1 point 5° of the bill, it is necessary to clearly mention in the bill whether it
this is direct access to personal data in the database or if it is
a communication of anonymized data, obtained on the basis of data appearing in the database of
data.

In addition, article 1 point 5° of the bill aimed at consulting data “for the purposes
statistics and research", it should be recalled that under the purpose limitation principle (Article 5
of the GDPR), personal data must be collected for specific, explicit and legitimate purposes,
and not be further processed in a manner incompatible with those purposes. Thus, the data should not
not be processed for purposes "incompatible" with the original purposes. Nevertheless, recital 50 of the
GDPR specifies that “Further processing for archival purposes in the public interest, for research purposes
scientific or historical or statistical purposes should be considered a lawful processing operation
compatible ".

Articles 6 paragraph (4) and 89 of the GDPR as well as article 65 of the law of 1 August 2018 on the organization
the National Data Protection Commission and the general data protection regime,
specify the appropriate measures that must be implemented by the controller during such
further data processing.

185

ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPD The National Rent Commission will therefore have to fulfill all the conditions provided for by the provisions
legal provisions set out above in order to allow the communication of the personal data contained
in the said database at STATEC, at the Observatoire de l’Habitat and at “research centres”.

As mentioned above, the actual text of the bill does not specify that the data must be
anonymized for "statistical and research purposes", but only the commentary of the articles refers to it.
That said, the CNPD notes that such a measure does indeed constitute one of the appropriate measures referred to in Article 65.
of the aforementioned law of August 1, 2018. This article indeed states that: “anonymisation, pseudonymisation at the
meaning of Article 4(5) of Regulation (EU) 2016/679 or other functional separation measures
ensuring that data collected for scientific or historical research purposes, or for statistical purposes,
cannot be used to take decisions or actions with regard to the persons concerned”.

However, the National Commission intends to draw the attention of the authors of the bill to the fact that
anonymization is a treatment that consists of using a set of techniques in such a way as to make it impossible,
in practice, any identification of the person by any means whatsoever and this in an irreversible manner. At
contrary, pseudonymisation, as defined by Article 4 paragraph (5) of the GDPR, is a processing of
personal data carried out in such a way that they can no longer be attributed to a data subject
accurate without recourse to additional information, provided that such additional information
are stored separately and subject to technical and organizational measures to ensure that the
personal data are not attributed to an identified or identifiable natural person”.

Therefore, it is important that the authors of the bill analyze whether the national commission for
rents will communicate pseudonymized data within the meaning of the GDPR, or truly anonymized data
or “rendered anonymous”, in which case the GDPR would not apply from the moment such
data no longer allow any identification of the person by any means whatsoever and this in a way
irreversible.

Finally, the National Commission also suggests that the authors of the bill adopt the terminology
of the GDPR and the law of 1 August 2018 on the organization of the National Commission for the Protection
data and the general data protection regime, with regard to the processing of data to
personal character "for statistical purposes" or for "scientific or historical research" purposes and not
as mentioned in this bill "for statistical and research purposes".

5. On the retention period of personal data

According to Article 5 paragraph (1), letter e) of the GDPR, personal data must not be
kept longer than necessary for the achievement of the purposes for which they are collected and processed.

186

52020 CNPD ANNUAL REPORTANNEXES In the absence of a retention period specified in the bill or in the comments on the
articles, the CNPD is unable to assess whether, in this case, the principle of limited retention
of data is respected concerning the collection of personal data recorded in the database of
data covered by this bill as well as concerning copies of the lease contract.

The CNPD therefore considers it necessary for the authors of the bill to specify in the text of Article 1
point 5° what is the retention period or at least the criteria for determining what is the
proportionate storage for the categories of data set out above.

Thus decided in Belvaux on July 28, 2020.

that the authors of the said bill had followed his argument146 with regard to the starting point of the
period after which the personal data appearing in the information system set up by the
director of health in order to follow the evolution of the spread of the SARS-CoV-2 virus should be anonymized.
Indeed, Article 10 paragraph (5) of the initial version of the bill provided that personal data
personnel processed are anonymized at the latest at the end of a period of three months after the law has ceased
to produce its effects. For this reason, the CNPD did not consider it necessary to notify the project at that time.
of law under object.

5ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPDANNEXES Through government amendment no. 6, the Minister of Health proposes to amend the wording of the new
article 6 (former article 2) of bill n°7645 in the sense that article 10 paragraph (5) of the amended law
of 17 July 2020 introducing a series of measures to combat the Covid-19 pandemic provides
now that the personal data in question are no longer anonymized, but pseudonymized, and
this within three months after their collection.

As regards first of all the fixing of the starting point of the duration after which the personal data
personnel must be pseudonymised, the CNPD can only, for the purposes of proportionality and necessity,
support the choice to take the date of data collection and no longer the day when the future law will stop producing
its effects, especially if we take into account the government's desire to extend the applicability of the aforementioned law
from July 17, 2020 to December 31, 2020 inclusive147. However, the National Commission cannot approve the
decision to insert in the bill under consideration only an obligation for the health directorate of
pseudonymize and not to anonymize or even delete the data collected after a certain period of time. THE
commentary to amendment no. 6 does not specify why the term "anonymized" has been replaced
by the term "pseudonymised".

The National Commission would like to point out in this context that it welcomes the establishment of a procedure for
pseudonymization of the data processed by the health department, such a procedure being to be considered as
one of the technical and organizational measures that the controller may be required to implement
place, in order to mitigate the risks for the data subjects.148 However, it is essential to know that the data
of a personal nature that have been pseudonymised can by definition always be attributed to
a natural person determined by the use of additional information.149 Thus, the CNPD cannot
disagree with the assertion of the authors of the amendments in the commentary to amendment n°6 of the
bill that “it is still appropriate to retain the process of pseudonymization which prevents the data to be
personal character are linked to the original identity of a natural person”.

Therefore, as pseudonymized data is always to be considered as information
concerning an indirectly identifiable natural person, the GDPR applies in full to their processing.
Only if the personal data is rendered completely anonymous in such a way that the
data subject is not or no longer identifiable, by any means, than the GDPR
no longer applies.150 As this is not the case in the current text of the bill, there is a certain
number of questions relating to the processing of pseudonymised data, such as: who manages the
pseudonymization? Do we use a subcontractor? How is pseudonymization carried out from a
technical point of view? Who can make a re-identification and who controls, if necessary, the legitimacy of these re-identifications?
IDs? If the data is pseudonymised for long-term tracking, what are the purposes
continued? Is the data pseudonymised for scientific or statistical research purposes
or does the pseudonymization of the data constitute a technical and organizational security measure or

147 As provided by amendment n°7 of the bill.
148 As provided for by Article 32 paragraph (1) letter a) of the GDPR, see also Article 25 (1) of the GDPR which provides that: “[…] the controller shall
implement, both at the time of determining the means of processing and at the time of the processing itself, technical and organizational measures
appropriate, such as pseudonymisation, which are intended to implement the principles relating to data protection […]. »

149 Article 4 point (5) of the GDPR defines pseudonymisation as “the processing of personal data in such a way that they can no longer be
attributed to a specific data subject without resorting to additional information, provided that this additional information is
kept separately and subject to technical and organizational measures to ensure that personal data is not attributed
to an identified or identifiable natural person. »

150 See recital (26) GDPR.

189

ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPD are we pursuing these two goals? Are pseudonymised data made available to third parties for the purposes
scientific research?

Furthermore, as the GDPR fully applies to pseudonymised data, the authors of the bill
No. 7645 must imperatively take into account Article 5 paragraph (1) letter (e) of the GDPR providing that the
personal data may only be “kept in a form which allows the identification of
data subjects for a period not exceeding that necessary with regard to the purposes for which
they are processed”. It also follows from recital (45) of the GDPR that where the processing is necessary
for the performance of a task in the public interest or in the exercise of official authority vested in the
controller, it should be for Union law or the law of a Member State to establish, between
others, the data retention period. Furthermore, Article 5 paragraph (1) letter (b) of the GDPR provides that
personal data must be “collected for specified, explicit and legitimate purposes, and
not to be further processed in a manner incompatible with those purposes”.

Thus, as already mentioned in its opinion 13/2020 of June 8, 2020 relating to bill n°7606, the CNPD
recalls that “the retention period must be determined according to the objective that led to the collection
data in question. Once this objective has been achieved, this data should be deleted or anonymised (in order to
in particular to produce statistics). »

Furthermore, as mentioned above and taking into account that the legislation regulating the measures of
fight against the Covid-19 pandemic will, if necessary, be extended as long as the legislator deems it necessary,
the National Commission now favors as a starting point the duration after which the data to be
personal character must be pseudonymized the date of collection of the data and not the day when the future
law will cease to have effect. In this context, it aligns itself with the opinion of the Consultative Commission on Rights
Human Rights of August 28, 2020 concerning the bill under consideration which also considers that “with the aim of
proportionality and necessity, such a stricter approach would be preferred. »

To conclude, with regard to the GDPR, it is therefore necessary and essential to define in the body of the text of the draft
law a retention period for personal data stored in pseudonymized form within
of the information system of the health department which is proportionate with regard to the purpose pursued, i.e.
that is to say that the bill should specify a period after which data, whether pseudonymised or not, should
be permanently deleted or completely anonymized within the meaning of the GDPR. In the absence of such a provision,
it must be concluded that the pseudonymised data can be kept without limitation in time, this
which would be incompatible with the rules of the GDPR.

Thus decided in Belvaux in plenary session and unanimously adopted remotely by the four Commissioners on
September 10, 2020.

For the National Data Protection Commission

52020 ANNUAL REPORT CNPDANNEXES Opinion on government amendments to bill no. 7683 amending 1) the
amended law of 17 July 2020 introducing a series of measures to combat
the Covid-19 pandemic and amending: 1° the amended law of 25 November 1975 concerning
the distribution of medicines to the public; 2° the amended law of 11 April 1983 on
regulation of the marketing and advertising of medicinal products; 2) the law
of September 23, 2020 on measures concerning the holding of meetings in the
companies and other legal persons.

(Deliberation n°23/2020 of October 27, 2020)

In accordance with Article 57, paragraph 1, letter (c) of Regulation No. 2016/679 of April 27, 2016 relating to
the protection of individuals with regard to the processing of personal data and the freedom
movement of such data, and repealing Directive 95/46/EC (general data protection regulation)
(hereinafter “the GDPR”), to which article 7 of the law of 1 August 2018 on the organization of the Commission refers
national data protection system and the general data protection regime, the Commission
Commission for Data Protection (hereinafter referred to as “the National Commission” or “the CNPD”)
“advises, in accordance with the law of the Member State, the national parliament, the government and other institutions
and organizations regarding legislative and administrative measures relating to the protection of the rights and freedoms of
natural persons with regard to the processing”.
 
On October 26, 2020, the Minister of Health seized the National Commission to decide
on government amendments to bill no. 7683 amending 1) the amended law of July 17, 2020
introducing a series of measures to combat the Covid-19 pandemic and amending: 1° the amended law
of November 25, 1975 concerning the delivery to the public of medicinal products; 2° the amended law of 11 April 1983
regulating the marketing and advertising of medicinal products; 2) the law of September 23
2020 on measures concerning the holding of meetings in companies and other legal persons
(hereafter: “Bill n°7683”). Said government amendments to bill n°7683 were
approved by the Government Council at its meeting of October 26, 2020.
 
Amendment 5 of bill n°7683 aims to modify the new article 6 (former article 5) in the sense that the
infected persons must provide information on their state of health and on the identity of persons with
which they have had contacts likely to generate a high risk of infection, to the director of health or
his delegate, to officials and employees designated for this purpose by the Director of Health, and henceforth also
employees made available to the Ministry of Health pursuant to Article L. 132-1 of the Labor Code relating to
temporary loan of labour.

191

CNPD 2020 ANNUAL REPORT It appears from the commentary to the aforementioned amendment 5 of bill no. 7683 that its purpose is “to be able to use
in addition to civil servants and employees, to employees made available to the Ministry of Health within the framework
a loan of labor in application of the provisions of the Labor Code relating thereto, in order to collect
information on the state of health of infected persons and on the identity of the persons with whom they have
been in contact. »

The CNPD fully understands that due to the vigorous increase in new infections these
days and correlatively of the workload of the contact tracing team of the health directorate, it
is necessary to increase in parallel the number of collaborators of the said team. Nevertheless, she finds
that article 10 paragraph (3) of bill n°7683, providing who is authorized in the context of the tracing of
contacts to access the data of infected persons or persons at high risk of being infected contained in the
information system set up for this purpose, has not been modified to add the aforementioned employees.
 
Thus, the CNPD recommends to the authors of bill n°7683 to insert in article 10 paragraph (3) of the said
project after "Only doctors and health professionals as well as civil servants and employees"
words “and employees made available to the Ministry of Health pursuant to Article L. 132-1 of the Code of
work ". Article 10 paragraph (3) of bill no. 7683 would then read as follows:
 
“Only doctors and health professionals as well as civil servants and employees and employees placed on
disposal of the Ministry of Health pursuant to Article L. 132-1 of the Labor Code, specifically designated
by the Director of Health, are authorized to access data relating to the health of infected persons or
at high risk of being infected. They access health-related data strictly to the extent that access is
necessary for the performance of the legal or conventional missions entrusted to them to prevent and combat
the Covid-19 pandemic and are bound by professional secrecy under the conditions and under the penalties provided for
in article 458 of the Penal Code. »
 
This addition would also have the consequence that the said employees would also be subject in this context to the
criminal sanctions provided for in article 458 of the criminal code in the event of breach of professional secrecy. Under these
restrictive conditions, the CNPD considers that additional access to the information system appears
legitimate.
 
Thus decided in Esch-sur-Alzette on October 27, 2020.

52020 ANNUAL REPORT CNPDANNEXES Opinion on bill no. 7635 introducing a series of temporary measures
in occupational safety and health in the context of the fight against COVID-19.

(Deliberation n°24/2020 of October 30, 2020)

In accordance with Article 57 paragraph (1) letter (c) of European Regulation 2016/679 of 27 April 2016 relating
the protection of individuals with regard to the processing of personal data and the freedom
movement of this data, and repealing Directive 95/46/EC (hereinafter referred to as "the GDPR"), each authority
has the task of advising “in accordance with the law of the Member State, the national parliament, the
government and other institutions and bodies regarding legislative and administrative measures relating to
the protection of the rights and freedoms of natural persons with regard to the processing”. Article 7 of the law of the 1st
August 2018 on the organization of the National Commission for Data Protection and the general system
on data protection specifically provides that the National Commission for Data Protection
(hereinafter referred to as “the National Commission” or “the CNPD”) carries out the missions entrusted to it by virtue of
of Article 57 of the GDPR.

In this respect, the National Commission takes the matter itself to advise on draft law no. 7635 introducing a series
temporary occupational safety and health measures in the context of the fight against COVID-19
(hereinafter the "draft law") and more specifically its article 2 paragraph (2).

The CNPD's own referral comes in the context of numerous requests for information and complaints
introduced to it concerning on the one hand the data, in particular health, that the employees would be authorized
to communicate to the employer in the event of suspicion or contamination with the SARS-CoV-2 virus, and on the other hand the
obligations of the employer with regard to the processing of this data.

Article 2 paragraph (2) of the bill under opinion, provides that “employees must report immediately, to
employer or designated employees and safety and health representatives, any work situation which they
have reasonable cause to believe that it presents a serious and immediate danger to safety and health in
the context of the COVID-19 epidemic”.

However, the National Commission wonders what is covered by the “work situation[s] of which [employees] have
reasonable cause to believe that they present a serious and immediate danger to safety and health
in the context of the COVID-19 epidemic", and wonders about the health data that could be transmitted
on these reports. Should an employee communicate to his employer the symptoms
that he presents, the fact that he has recently been in contact with an infected person, or the positive result of a
diagnostic test for SARS-CoV-2 virus infection? Could the employer require the communication of such

193

ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPD information or the production of the result of a positive or negative test carried out by an employee? Or is the
transmission by the employee of a notice of fitness or inability to return to work issued by a professional
health is considered sufficient, like other sick leave, in accordance with legal obligations
work ?

In the event that this provision should be interpreted as meaning that health data should be
transmitted by employees to employers (result of a diagnostic test, report of symptoms, and/or
or reporting of contacts with an infected person), the National Commission still wonders what
processing employers could then implement regarding these special categories of data
of a personal nature within the meaning of Article 9 of the GDPR, known as “sensitive data”, and under what conditions?

The CNPD would like to point out that under Article 9 of the GDPR, it is in principle prohibited to process so-called data.
“sensitive”, unless one of the exceptions provided for in paragraph (2) of this article is met. One of these
exceptions can be invoked by health professionals and covers the situation in which "the treatment
is necessary for the purposes of preventive medicine or occupational medicine, the assessment of the capacity
of the worker's work, of medical diagnoses, of health or social care, or of the management
healthcare or social protection systems and services on the basis of Union law, national law
of a Member State or under a contract concluded with a healthcare professional and subject to the conditions and
guarantees referred to in paragraph 3” (article 9 paragraph (2) letter (h)), while the exception provided for in article
9 paragraph (2) letter (b) can be mobilized by employers in the event that "the processing is necessary for the
purposes of the performance of the obligations and the exercise of the rights specific to the controller or to the person
concerned in terms of labor law, social security and social protection, insofar as this
processing is authorized by Union law, by the law of a Member State or by a collective agreement concluded
under the law of a Member State which provides appropriate safeguards for the fundamental rights and interests
of the person concerned”.

In addition, the CNPD includes, following the adoption of the law of July 17, 2020 introducing a series of
measures to combat the Covid-19 pandemic, whether it arises from the missions of the director of health or his
delegated to process the health data of people who are infected or at high risk of being infected in order to follow up
the evolution of the spread of the SARS-CoV-2 virus.

On the contrary, it would not be up to an employer to replace the health directorate by conducting its
own investigation concerning the health status or contacts of its employees, or by establishing its own
“contact tracing” register, with regard to the principle of data minimization, set out in Article 5 paragraph
(1) letter e) GDPR. However, this would not prevent an employer from being called upon to collaborate and
to transmit any relevant information on a case-by-case basis with the health inspection division of the management of
Health, as part of a contact tracing procedure implemented by the latter.

194

52020 ANNUAL REPORT CNPDANNEXES The National Commission concludes that, in the absence of additional details in the text of the bill
under notice as to the health data that should be transmitted, and as to the processing of these reports
by the employers, the latter can then only process the opinions of aptitude or inaptitude to resume the
work issued by a health professional, without further details relating to the state of health of the employee, the
the same way they deal with other sick leaves. Collaboration between employers and the division
of the health inspection of the health department could also be considered for the communication
information it deems relevant.

However, there is currently some uncertainty as to this interpretation of the provisions currently
applicable, not allowing employers to precisely determine the data that should be processed in
the framework of taking occupational health and safety measures to combat the spread of the epidemic
of COVID-19, nor for employees to know what data they are required to communicate to their employer. There
CNPD therefore considers that the draft law under opinion does not meet the requirements of clarity, precision and
foreseeability to which a legal text must respond, in accordance with the case law of the Court of Justice of
the European Union and the European Court of Human Rights. In order to deal with this legal uncertainty
regarding this interpretation and to ensure compliance of the Luxembourg legal framework with the GDPR and case law
European Union, the CNPD suggests that the authors of the bill clarify the type of personal data that
employees should communicate and the obligations of employers with regard to the processing of this data.

Thus decided in Esch-sur-Alzette on October 30, 2020.

(1) Any person acting under the authority of the controller who has access to data may not
process them only on instructions from the controller, except under legal obligations.
 
(2) The controller must implement all appropriate technical and organizational measures
to ensure the protection of the data it processes against accidental or unlawful destruction, accidental loss,
unauthorized alteration, dissemination or access, in particular when the processing involves transmissions of
data in a network, as well as against any other form of unlawful processing.

(a) prevent any unauthorized person from gaining access to the facilities used for data processing
(control at the entrance to the facilities);

authorized using data transmission facilities (usage control);
