2020 ANNUAL REPORT CNPD Opinion on the draft Grand-Ducal regulation setting the terms and conditions for the registration of
establishments of food business operators.

(Deliberation n°27/2020 of December 2, 2020)

In accordance with article 57, paragraph 1, letter c) of regulation n°2016/679 of April 27, 2016 relating to the
protection of individuals with regard to the processing of personal data and freedom
movement of such data, and repealing Directive 95/46/EC (general data protection regulation)
(hereinafter referred to as the “GDPR”), to which article 7 of the law of 1 August 2018 on the organization of
the National Commission for Data Protection and the general data protection regime,
the National Commission for Data Protection (hereinafter referred to as the “National Commission”)
“advises, in accordance with the law of the Member State, the national parliament, the government and other institutions
and organizations regarding legislative and administrative measures relating to the protection of the rights and freedoms of
natural persons with regard to the processing”.
 
By letter dated August 12, 2020, the Minister of Consumer Protection invited the
National Commission to decide on the draft Grand-Ducal regulation setting the registration procedures
establishments of food business operators (hereinafter referred to as the “draft Grand-Ducal regulation”).
 
It appears from the explanatory memorandum that according to European regulations, any food business operator
must register with the competent authorities each of the establishments for which it is responsible and which
intervenes in the food chain.
 
At the national level, article 6 of the law of July 28, 2018 establishing a system of control and sanction
relating to foodstuffs relates to this registration by providing that “(…) any operator of the sector
food notifies the [government commissioner for quality, fraud and food safety], for the purposes
of registration, each of the establishments for which it is responsible and which implement one of the steps
production, processing and distribution of foodstuffs. To this end, the commissioner's office is
authorized to use a file, and the data entered therein will be transmitted to the authorities responsible for the control
foodstuffs. »
 
Paragraph 2 of the said article continues that “[a] Grand-Ducal regulation specifies the procedures as well as the
procedures for registering the establishments referred to in paragraph 1 of this article. »

The draft Grand-Ducal regulation is part of the implementation of the aforementioned article 6, even if, in
facts, a computerized notification system has been operational since 2016.

200

5ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPDANNEXES The National Commission will limit its observations to the provisions of the draft Grand-Ducal regulation without
examine in more detail the European and national legislation on the basis of which the draft regulation
ducal was taken.
 
Article 3 of the draft Grand-Ducal regulation lists the information that food business operators
must notify the Government Commissioner for Food Quality, Fraud and Safety, namely (i)
the commercial sign of the establishment, (ii) the physical address where the activities take place, (iii) the contact details of
contact and address of the operator, (iv) activities related to the stages of production, processing and
the distribution of foodstuffs and (v) when the operator is a legal person, the natural person
designated as recipient of official control reports and as contact person in the event of incidents related to
foodstuffs on behalf of this legal entity.
 
Based on the information available to it, the National Commission considers that the categories of data
to be notified are adequate, relevant and limited to what is necessary with regard to the purposes for
which they are processed, namely the control of food business operators156, so that the principle
minimization of data set out in Article 5, paragraph 1, letter c) of the GDPR is complied with.
 
Article 5 of the draft Grand-Ducal regulation provides that the government commissioner for quality, fraud
and Food Safety shall keep a register of the establishments containing the data notified in accordance with
article 3 and that the data, regularly updated, are kept for a maximum period of 5
years after the end of activities related to the stages of production, processing and distribution of
foodstuffs.
 
The National Commission considers that, given the fact that the law of 28 July 2018 establishing a system of control
and sanctions relating to foodstuffs provides for correctional penalties prescribed by 5 years,
limiting the retention of data to a maximum of 5 years is in accordance with the principle of
limitation of retention of personal data provided for in Article 5, paragraph 1, letter e) of the GDPR.
 
 
With regard to the term "register" used in Article 5 of the draft Grand-Ducal regulation, the Commission
national considers that it would be preferable to use the same term as that already used in article 6 of the law
cited above of July 28, 2018, namely “file”. Not only would this way of proceeding highlight the fact
that it is indeed the same file, but this term also has the advantage of being defined by the GDPR157.
 
Finally, the National Commission wonders whether there might not be a possible inconsistency between the law of
28 July 2018 establishing a control and sanction system relating to foodstuffs and the draft
grand-ducal regulations. Indeed, article 6 of the aforementioned law states that the data entered in the file will be

156 Even if the purpose of the processing emerges from reading the various normative texts, it could be useful to recall it in Article 1 of the draft regulation

grand-ducal.

157 Article 4(6) GDPR.

201

ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPD transmitted to the administrations responsible for food control while the draft regulation
Grand Ducal, while remaining silent on this subject in the body of the text, indicates in his explanatory memorandum that "in order
in particular to avoid duplication and to enhance the effectiveness of official controls, it is essential
to have a centralized register of establishments in the food chain”.
 
According to the understanding of the CNPD, the aforementioned law of July 28, 2018 does not allow the administrations responsible for
control of foodstuffs to directly access said file insofar as the data entered therein
sent to them by the government commission for quality, fraud and food safety. In
evoking a "centralized register", the authors of the draft Grand-Ducal regulation nevertheless seem to want to create
a file directly accessible to the authorities responsible for controlling foodstuffs.
 
For all intents and purposes, the National Commission takes the liberty of pointing out that access to external files containing
personal data and the transmission of such data to third parties constitutes interference with
privacy and therefore, by virtue of Article 11, paragraph 3, of the Constitution, a matter reserved for the law
formal. In this case, most of the normative framework must appear in the law158. In case of direct access, the law should
in particular list the administrations that can access the file in question, as well as the purposes of this
access.
 
 Thus decided in Belvaux on December 2, 2020.

202

52020 ANNUAL REPORT CNPDANNEXES Opinion relating to bill no. 7639 amending the law of 23 December 2016 concerning the
collection, entry and control of aid files relating to housing and the development project
grand-ducal regulation repealing the grand-ducal regulation of 23 December 2016 setting
the measures for implementing the law of 23 December 2016 concerning the collection, seizure
and control of housing assistance files.

(Deliberation n°28/2020 of December 2, 2020)

In accordance with article 57, paragraph (1), letter (c) of regulation n°2016/679 of April 27, 2016 relating to
the protection of individuals with regard to the processing of personal data and the freedom
movement of such data, and repealing Directive 95/46/EC (general data protection regulation)
(hereinafter the “GDPR”), to which article 7 of the law of 1 August 2018 on the organization of the Commission refers
national data protection system and the general data protection regime, the Commission
Commission for Data Protection (hereinafter referred to as the “National Commission” or the “CNPD”)
“advises, in accordance with the law of the Member State, the national parliament, the government and other institutions
and organizations regarding legislative and administrative measures relating to the protection of the rights and freedoms of
natural persons with regard to the processing”.

By letter dated July 21, 2020, the Minister of Housing invited the National Commission to
rule on the draft law amending the law of 23 December 2016 concerning the collection, seizure and control
housing aid files (hereinafter the “draft law”) and the draft Grand-Ducal regulation
repealing the Grand-Ducal regulation of 23 December 2016 setting the implementing measures for the law of 23 December
2016 concerning the collection, entry and control of housing aid files (hereinafter the “project
Grand-Ducal regulation”).

As a preliminary remark, it should be noted that Article 1, last paragraph, of the law of 23 December 2016
concerning the collection, entry and control of housing aid files (hereinafter the “Law of 23
December 2016”) continues to refer to the law of 2 August 2002 on the protection of persons with regard to
of the processing of personal data. However, this law was repealed by the law of 1 August 2018 on
organization of the National Commission for Data Protection and the general data protection regime
data. This reference should therefore be deleted and henceforth reference should be made to the legislation currently
in force.

I. Repeal of the Grand-Ducal Regulation

The authors of the bill propose to repeal the Grand-Ducal regulation of 23 December 2016 setting the
implementing measures for the law of 23 December 2016 and inserting these provisions directly into the law.

203

ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPD Thus, the provisions of the aforementioned Grand-Ducal regulation would now be found in Articles 4, paragraph (5),
and 5, paragraph (1), paragraph 2, of the law of December 23, 2016, as amended by the draft law.

The CNPD welcomes such a modification as it had suggested in its opinion of 25 November 2016 relating to the
bill n°7054.

However, given the other amendments that the authors of the bill intend to make to the law of 23 December
2016, the National Commission is wondering whether it is still appropriate to include in the law the list of
personal data that may be exchanged between state authorities. The CNPD will come back to this below at the point
III. of this notice.

II. The change in terminology

The authors of the bill indicate in the explanatory memorandum that they want to take the opportunity "to clarify the text
in some places, which now provides for more appropriate terminology". Explanations in this regard
however, are not provided in the explanatory memorandum or in the commentary to the articles.
 
However, the National Commission wonders whether the terms "personal data" and "files",
would not be more appropriate than the terms "information" or "information", used henceforth
throughout the bill, for reasons of consistency with the GDPR which defines these concepts in its
Article 4, paragraphs (1) and (6). The use of notions defined by the GDPR would also allow a better
understanding of the device under review.

Indeed, by way of example, the initial version of paragraph (3) of Article 4, which used the term
"file", allowed a better understanding of the device, while the new terminology used,
namely "access to the information files of the Minister having the Environment in his attributions" and "access
to the information files of the Minister having Housing in his attributions", is likely to bring to
confusion.

Furthermore, the authors of the bill specify in Article 4, paragraph (4), as amended by the bill
law, that applicants and recipients of housing assistance must give their “explicit” consent.
The National Commission wonders what the authors mean when they wish to specify the nature
“explicit” consent.

In any case, it should be remembered that in terms of data protection, consent is defined
by Article 4, paragraph (11), of the GDPR (which implies that it must be “free, specific, informed and unambiguous”)
and that its collection must be carried out in accordance with the legal provisions of Article 7 of the GDPR. Therefore, the

204

52020 ANNUAL REPORT CNPDANNEXES consent of applicants for and beneficiaries of housing aid must be given in accordance with the
legal provisions of the GDPR, so that the explicit term can be considered superfluous.

III. Creating new files

According to the text currently in force, the Minister having Housing in his attributions and the Minister having
the Environment in its attributions have access to external files for the purposes detailed in the text
cited above, while the bill under opinion provides in Article 4, paragraphs (1) and (2), that the ministers are
communicate by the state authorities, listed in the aforementioned paragraphs, certain data of a
personnel, "necessary information" or "relevant information".

Are we to understand that, unlike the current system providing for consultation of external files by the
Minister having Housing in his attributions and by the Minister having the Environment in his attributions, these
latter are now recipients of data from such files? Are the ministers therefore led to
maintain files containing such data?

If this were to be the case, the CNPD would like to reiterate its observations made in its opinion of 25 November 2016 relating to
to bill no. 7054159 and recalls that keeping a file of personal data collected and processed
by a state authority must have a legal basis in accordance with Article 6(3) GDPR.

This article provides for a specific constraint related to the lawfulness of data processing necessary for compliance with a
legal obligation or the performance of a task in the public interest or in the exercise of official authority, of which
is vested with the controller. In these two cases, the basis and purposes of the processing of
data must specifically be defined either by European Union law or by the law of the Member State
to which the controller is subject.

In addition, recital (45) of the GDPR specifies that it should "[...] belong to Union law or to the law
of a Member State to determine the purpose of the processing. Furthermore, this law could specify the conditions
general rules of this Regulation governing the lawfulness of the processing of personal data, establish the
specifications aimed at determining the controller, the type of personal data making
the purpose of the processing, the persons concerned, the entities to which the personal data can be
be disclosed, purpose limitations, retention periods and other measures to ensure
lawful and fair processing […]”.

Recital 41 of the GDPR further specifies that this legislative measure should be clear and precise and its
application should be foreseeable for litigants, in accordance with the case law of the Court of Justice of
the European Union and the European Court of Human Rights.

159 The CNPD noted in particular in its opinion of 25 November 2016 relating to bill no. 7054 that “[e]n the absence of the consent of the person
concerned that the competent ministers check directly in the files held by other administrations the information necessary for the investigation
applications for housing aid, the persons concerned have in principle an alternative consisting in providing supporting documents themselves
containing information from said files and documenting their administrative situation. This results in a need to frame beyond the hypothesis of a
prior consent of the persons concerned, the cases where the ministers concerned would be made recipients of data from databases
administrative managed by other administrations. The CNPD considers it essential that the normative framework on this point be included in the law”

160 In this sense, see M. Besch, “Personal data processing in the public sector”, Norms and legistics in Luxembourg public law, Luxembourg,

Promoculture Larcier, 2019, p.470, n°619.

205

ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPD Pursuant to the aforementioned provisions, these legal bases should establish specific provisions aimed at
to determine, among other things, the types of data processed, the persons concerned, the entities to which the
data may be communicated and for what purposes, the data retention periods or even
processing operations and procedures.

Thus, for the lawfulness of processing in the public sector to be ensured, it is necessary to have a normative text.
national or supranational which may lead an administration or a service to have to process data for
fulfill its missions. While a text should not specifically prescribe data processing, “the
purpose of the processing must however be precise, insofar as the text leading the administration to deal with
data must allow citizens to deduce the nature of the data and the purposes for which they are
used”162. The National Commission therefore considers it essential, in the event that the above-mentioned ministers,
keep one or more files, as controllers, that this bill provides for the
legal provisions creating such files. These must contain the elements mentioned above.

Insofar as the provisions of article 3 paragraph 2 of the law of December 23, 2016 concerning the collection,
the entry and control of aid files relating to housing163 already seem to provide for the keeping of a
file by the Minister having Housing in his attributions, these should be completed while they are
are worded too vaguely. The said provisions should in particular specify that the aforementioned minister
will have the quality of data controller and that the file will contain, in addition to the data currently covered by
Article 3 of the aforementioned law, the data received by the said Minister by the administrations referred to in Article 4, such as
modified by the bill.

Furthermore, similar provisions concerning the keeping of a file by the Minister responsible for the Environment in
its attributions should also be inserted in the aforementioned article 3 whereas this is not currently specified
by the text under notice.

In any case, if the will of the authors of the bill is to provide for the communication of data to
aforementioned Ministers by the administrations referred to in paragraphs (1) and (2) of the aforementioned Article 4, then this should
clearly be reflected throughout the bill.

Thus, the terminology used in Article 4, paragraph (4), and in Article
5, subsection (1), as amended by the bill, while these sections notably continue to make
respectively reference to “access to information in the national register and the general register” and to
“access by ministers to information from the files listed in article 4 (…)”.

IV. The exchange of data between the Minister having Housing in his attributions and the Minister having

the Environment in its attributions

161 M. Besch, “Personal data processing in the public sector”, Norms and legislation in Luxembourg public law, Luxembourg, Promoculture

Larcier, 2019, p.470, n°619.

162 M. Besch, “Personal data processing in the public sector”, Norms and legislation in Luxembourg public law, Luxembourg, Promoculture

Larcier, 2019, p.470, n°619.

163 Article 3 paragraph 2 of the law of 23 December 2016 concerning the collection, entry and control of housing aid files provides that: “After the
collection and input of housing aid applications and related documents, personal data is transferred to storage media.
secure data to which the agent of the Minister having Housing in his attributions having carried out the collection and seizure does not have access”.

206

52020 CNPD ANNUAL REPORTANNEXES Article 4, paragraph (3), as amended by the bill, maintains an exchange of data between the Minister
having Housing in his attributions and the Minister having the Environment in his attributions through a
respective access to their files.

However, the categories of data to which this exchange relates are not specified so that the
National Commission is not in a position to comment on the necessary and proportionate nature of the exchange
of data as referred to in paragraph (3) above.

It would therefore be appropriate for the bill to indicate at least the categories of data that would be exchanged
between the said ministers.

In addition, insofar as the draft law provides for specific communication of data from files
administrative duties for each of the aforementioned ministers, the attention of the authors of the bill should be drawn to the
fact that this exchange of data should not allow ministers to indirectly obtain data from
personal character from the files of state authorities to which they do not have access under the draft
law under notice.

V. The Communication of “Information” by Other Authorities

According to the explanatory memorandum, the bill under opinion intends to provide in the law of December 23, 2016 "in what
extent and under what conditions the Ministry of Housing may obtain the communication of information
from the Administration of direct contributions respectively from the Fund for the future of children,
which are not yet among the authorities listed in article 4 of the said law of 2016 (…)”.

It should be noted that Article 4, paragraph (1), point 1°, as amended by the draft law, lists the
personal data that may be transmitted by the Administration of direct contributions to the minister having the
Housing in its attributions.

However, such details are not provided for the data that would be transmitted to the minister having the
Housing in his attributions and to the Minister having the Environment in his attributions under paragraphs
(1), point 2° to 4° and (2) of Article 4, as amended by the bill.

However, article 4, paragraph (5), as resulting from the draft law, which is taken from article 1 of the regulation
of 23 December 2016 setting the implementing measures for the law of 23 December 2016, lists the
categories of personal data that can be communicated “from the files listed in
paragraphs 1 and 2” to the aforementioned ministers, without specifying which authorities transmit which data to which
minister.

207

ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPD The National Commission therefore wonders how the specific provisions of Article 4 are structured,
paragraph (1), point 1° with the general ones of article 4, paragraph (5).

Thus, for the sake of consistency of the internal structure of Article 4, as amended by the bill, it would be
judicious that the authors of the bill specify for each communication of data referred to in paragraphs
(1) and (2) of the aforementioned article which are the categories which could be transmitted to the ministers and to delete
consequently paragraph (5) of Article 4, the provisions of which would become superfluous.

Finally, would it not be more relevant to provide that the identification data of the persons concerned who
would be transmitted to the ministers so that they can verify its authenticity and accuracy would only be communicated
by the administration that maintains the national register within the meaning of the amended law of 19 June 2013 relating to the identification
natural persons, rather than providing for this data to be communicated by the Administration of
direct contributions?

Thus decided in Belvaux on December 2, 2020.

5ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPDANNEXES Opinion on the draft law on the recognition of qualifications
professionals in the field of inland navigation and amending the
amended law of 28 July 1973 creating a navigation service.

(Deliberation n°29/2020 of December 17, 2020)

In accordance with article 57, paragraph 1, letter c) of regulation n°2016/679 of April 27, 2016 relating to the
protection of individuals with regard to the processing of personal data and freedom
movement of such data, and repealing Directive 95/46/EC (general data protection regulation)
(hereinafter the “GDPR”), to which article 7 of the law of 1 August 2018 on the organization of the Commission refers
national data protection system and the general data protection regime, the Commission
Commission for Data Protection (hereinafter referred to as the “National Commission” or the “CNPD”)
“advises, in accordance with the law of the Member State, the national parliament, the government and other institutions
and organizations regarding legislative and administrative measures relating to the protection of the rights and freedoms of
natural persons with regard to the processing”.

By letter dated August 18, 2020, the Minister of Mobility and Public Works invited the
National Commission to decide on the draft law on the recognition of qualifications
professionals in the field of inland navigation and amending the amended law of 28 July
1973 establishing a navigation service (hereinafter the "draft law").

The purpose of the draft law is in particular to transpose into national law Directive (EU) 2017/2397 of the European Parliament
European Parliament and of the Council of 12 December 2017 on the recognition of professional qualifications in
the field of inland navigation and repealing Council Directives 91/672/EEC and 96/50/EC (hereinafter the
“directive”). The directive aims to establish a common framework for the recognition of qualifications
minimum professional requirements in the field of inland navigation.

The Directive is supplemented by Commission Delegated Directive (EU) 2020/12 of 2 August 2019 as regards
concerns the standards relating to skills and the corresponding knowledge and skills, to the tests
practices, approval of simulators and medical fitness, by Implementing Regulation (EU) 2020/182 of the
Commission of 14 January 2020 on models relating to professional qualifications in the field of
inland navigation and by Commission Delegated Regulation (EU) 2020/473 of 20 January 2020 regarding
concerning the standards applicable to databases relating to Union qualification certificates,
service books and logbooks.

209

ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPD Insofar as the draft bill under opinion transposes the directive into national law, the National Commission
will limit its comments to the legal provisions concerning the concrete implementation of this directive in
Luxemburg.

This notice will deal with issues relating to aspects of the protection of personal data
raised by the articles of Chapters 3 and 4 of the draft law.

I. On the data processing carried out by the Minister

It emerges from the reading of the draft law that the minister having transport in his attributions (hereafter
the “Minister”) will collect and process data (i) upon issuance, renewal and suspension,
respectively the withdrawal of Union qualification certificates and specific authorisations164, (ii) when
access to state files165 and (iii) when keeping registers as referred to in Article 20 of the preliminary draft
of law.

Before examining in more detail the processing carried out on these occasions (points B to D), the National Commission
makes some general observations (point A below).

A. General Considerations

First of all, it should be noted that article 19 entitled “Protection of personal data” indicates
in particular that the GDPR applies to the processing of personal data provided for by law166, specifies
the purposes for which the data will be processed167, refers to the rights of data subjects168 and
specifies that the Minister has the status of data controller169.

1. On the reference to the GDPR (Article 19 paragraphs (1) and (3) of the draft law)

Although paragraph (1) of Article 19 is a literal repetition of paragraph (1) of Article 24 of the Directive,
the CNPD wonders whether it is necessary, or even appropriate, to provide that the GDPR applies to the processing of
personal data provided for by the draft law while the GDPR is, as a regulation
European, compulsory in all its elements and directly applicable in Luxembourg.

In addition, paragraph (3) of Article 19 of the draft law reproduces the wording of Article 24 paragraph (4)
of the directive by stipulating that the persons whose data are processed are informed in advance of this
processing and that they have the right to access their personal data and to have a copy of this data,
on request, at any time.

164 Articles 8 et seq.
165 Article 19 paragraph (5).
166 Section 19 paragraph (1).
167 Article 19 paragraph (2).
168 Section 19 paragraph (3).
169 Section 19 paragraph (4).

210

52020 CNPD ANNUAL REPORTANNEXES The CNPD wonders whether this provision would not be superfluous compared to the provisions that are already
provided for by the GDPR with regard to the right to information of data subjects170 as well as the right
access by data subjects171.

For all practical purposes, the National Commission takes the liberty of drawing the attention of the authors of the preliminary draft law to
the problem related to the fact of partially or fully reproducing the text of a European regulation in
the internal legal order. Indeed, the Council of State regularly recalls in its opinions the case law of the Court
of Justice of the European Union according to which Member States must not impede the direct applicability of
regulations nor conceal their European nature172.

2. On the purposes of the processing (Article 19 paragraph (2) of the draft law)

The authors of the draft law are to be congratulated on the fact that they have specified the purposes of the processing of
personal data.

However, and although these provisions are a repetition of the wording of Article 24 paragraph (3) of the Directive,
the National Commission regrets that these are not more detailed, in particular according to the different
processing carried out by the Minister. This particularly applies to access by the Minister to data from
files held by other state administrations.

The CNPD will return to this point later.

3. On the designation of the controller

Finally, with regard to article 19 paragraph (4), which is an addition to the directive, the CNPD
asks for the rationale for this provision when it is already apparent from Articles 8, 9, 10, 11, 18, 19,
20, 21 and 23 of the draft law that the Minister has the quality of data controller for the processing
of personal data arising from said articles.

In addition, the amended law of 2 August 2002 on the protection of individuals with regard to the processing of
personal data to which reference is made has been repealed by the law of 1 August 2018 on
organization of the National Commission for Data Protection and the general data protection regime
data. Reference should therefore be made to the legislation currently in force, namely Article 4(7)
of the GDPR.

B. On the processing implemented in the context of the issue, renewal, suspension

and the withdrawal of Union qualification certificates and specific authorizations

ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPD Pursuant to articles 8 and following of the draft law, the CNPD understands that the Minister collects data from

1. On the condition of good repute (Article 8 paragraph (1) letter d) of the draft law)

Even if article 8 of the draft law constitutes a quasi-faithful reproduction of article 11 of the directive, there are
instead of noting that an additional condition has been provided for in paragraph (1) letter d). In fact, the draft
of law indicates that applicants must provide the Minister with “supporting documents” establishing in a manner
satisfactory that they meet the criteria of good repute.

Thus, the applicant, in order to establish his good repute, must provide a “recent extract from the criminal record or a
equivalent document from the place of habitual residence that does not indicate a criminal conviction in force
of res judicata pronouncing a prison sentence of at least six months for theft, fraud, abuse of
confidence, willful attack on the integrity of the person, sexual assault or violation of the legislation on
narcotics or driving while intoxicated or under the influence of alcohol”. However, this condition is not a condition
required by the directive.

The authors of the draft bill specify that the provision under notice was added “because of the approach
equivalent in the Rhine diet and in order to maintain a high level of quality". However, the Rhine diet
applicable to navigation personnel on the Rhine, to which the authors of the draft law refer173, is
separate regime from the Directive and is not governed by it. Therefore, the CNPD wonders whether it is relevant to
provide for this additional condition provided by an international organization. By adding such a condition,
wouldn't the draft law under opinion result in an incorrect transposition of the directive?

Furthermore, the National Commission is not in a position to assess, based on the comments of the authors of
the aforementioned draft law, if the collection of such data complies with the principle of data minimization provided for
in Article 5 paragraph (1) letter c) of the GDPR. The authors of the draft bill should, indeed, explain the
reasons why they believe that the collection of such data is adequate, relevant and limited to this
which is necessary in relation to the purposes for which they are processed.

Furthermore, the authors of the draft bill should be made aware of the fact that in the legal order
Luxembourg, the communication of information relating to court decisions is done by issuing
extracts from criminal records in accordance with the amended law of 29 March 2013 on the organization of criminal records
(hereinafter the "Law of March 29, 2013"). This law creates several bulletins with a breakdown
entries on the respective bulletins. The National Commission therefore recommends specifying
in the draft bill which bulletin the applicants will have to provide to the Minister174. Furthermore, it would be useful to

173 Regulations relating to navigation personnel on the Rhine adopted by the Central Commission for the Navigation of the Rhine.
174 In view of the convictions listed in Article 8 paragraph (1) letter d) of the draft law, the CNPD considers that only bulletins Nos 2 and 3 are likely to

to be targeted by the authors of the draft law.

52020 ANNUAL REPORT CNPDANNEXES specify what is meant by a “recent” extract from the criminal record, by providing for example that the extract
must be less than 1 month old.

In the event that bulletin No. 2 is covered by Article 8 paragraph (1) letter d) of the draft law, it should be noted
that this bulletin is not issued to the person concerned but, under certain conditions, to the authorities
listed in Article 8 of the Law of March 29, 2013, respectively in Article 1 of the Grand-
of 23 July 2016 fixing the list of administrations and legal persons governed by public law that may
request a bulletin N°2 or N°3 of the criminal record with the written or electronic agreement of the person concerned
(hereinafter the “Grand-ducal regulation of 23 July 2016”).

Thus, it follows from Article 1, point 1) of the Grand-Ducal Regulation of 23 July 2016 that the "Minister
having Transport in its attributions" can obtain a copy of a bulletin N°2 "for the instruction of any
request for approval, license or permit addressed to a service of its competence”. The Commission
national authority wonders whether the issue of Union qualification certificates could be covered by this
arrangement.

With regard to bulletin N°3, this can be delivered to the data subject himself. Article 8-1 of the
law of March 29, 2013 also provides for the possibility of delivering this bulletin to third parties, including in particular
the administrations and legal persons governed by public law listed by the Grand-Ducal regulation of 23 July
2016. It should be noted that the minister in charge of transport is not among the authorities
public listed therein.

With regard to the wording of Article 8 paragraph (1) letter d) of the draft law, the National Commission
understands that it is the applicant's responsibility to provide the Minister with an extract from the criminal record and that the draft
of law does not provide for the possibility for the Minister to be issued directly, under the conditions provided for by
the law of 29 March 2013 and the Grand-Ducal regulation of 23 July 2016, the bulletin in question.

In view of the foregoing, it would be appropriate for clarifications as to the articulation of the provisions of the law of
March 29, 2013 and the Grand-Ducal Regulation of July 23, 2016, on the one hand, with Article 8 paragraph (1) letter d)
of the draft bill, on the other hand, be made by the authors of the draft bill.

For all intents and purposes, it should be noted that bill no. 7691, tabled on November 2, 2020, aims
to specify the various “good repute” procedures currently provided for in several texts of
law and which come under the jurisdiction of the Minister of Justice175. The provisions of the draft law relating to
to good repute would therefore not be subject to the aforementioned draft. However and if such
should not be the case, shouldn’t Bill 7691 be adapted in order to incorporate the provisions relating to
good repute that appear in the draft bill under opinion?

213

2020 CNPD ANNUAL REPORT 2. On medical fitness (article 8 paragraph (1) letter c))

It should be recalled that the personal data which would be collected by the Minister in order to certify that a
candidate has the required medical skills are to be qualified as “sensitive” data within the meaning of
GDPR Article 9. These are indeed related to the health of the candidates. The processing of such data
therefore require specific protection and are subject to stricter requirements.

In order to establish that applicants meet the proficiency standards, they must issue a certificate
on the basis of the procedure referred to in Article 18 of the draft law.

Article 18 provides in particular that applicants “demonstrate their medical fitness by presenting to the Minister
a valid medical certificate issued by a doctor recognized by the Minister, on the basis of an examination confirming
medical fitness”. The National Commission therefore understands that the Minister will only be sent a
certificate confirming or not the medical fitness of the applicant.

In addition, the medical fitness standards specifying the requirements relating to medical fitness, in particular
with regard to the tests that doctors must carry out are detailed in Annex III of the directive as well as
than in Annex IV of Commission Delegated Directive (EU) 2020/12 of 2 August 2019 supplementing the Directive.

This article is a faithful transposition of article 23 of the directive and does not call for comments from
the CNPD.

3. On the verification of the authenticity and validity of documents

It appears from Articles 8 and 9 of the draft law that the Minister “verifies the authenticity and validity of the documents
provided by applicants. Article 23 paragraph (1) of the draft law further provides that “[t]he Minister
takes appropriate measures to prevent fraud and other illegal practices regarding certificates of
the Union, the service books, the logbooks, the medical certificates and the registers provided for by this law”.

The National Commission understands that such measures aim to prevent fraud and other illegal practices.
Nevertheless, in the absence of precision in the commentary of the articles, she wonders by what means the
Minister intends to verify the authenticity and validity of the documents. Verification of authenticity and validity
records is done through access to state files, such as those listed in subsection (5)
of article 19 of the draft law? If so, this should be clearly stated in the draft law.

If access to other state files than those listed in the aforementioned article should be provided, then this should be
clearly determined and framed by the draft law.

214

52020 CNPD ANNUAL REPORTANNEXES 4. On Article 21 entitled “Monitoring”

Article 21 of the draft law provides for the creation of a monitoring system, particularly with regard to the
"issue and update of Union qualification certificates, service records and logbooks".
This article is a quasi-literal reworking of Article 27 of the Directive.

The National Commission understands that this monitoring system is referred to in Article 11 paragraph (1) of the
draft law which provides that “[w]here there are elements to suggest that the requirements relating to the certificates
qualifications or specific authorizations are no longer satisfied, the Minister when he issued the certificate or
the specific authorization carries out all the necessary assessments and, if necessary, withdraws these certificates or this
specific authorization.

Subsection (2) of the aforementioned article further provides that “[t]he Minister may temporarily suspend a certificate
qualification of the Union, when he considers that this suspension is necessary for safety reasons
or public order".

Thus, if it emerges from the provisions mentioned above that the Minister seems to operate a follow-up of the certificates of
qualification or specific authorisations, the terms of such monitoring are however not specified in the preliminary
law Project.

However, in such a context, the CNPD wonders what information would be transmitted if
appropriate to the Minister. In addition, is the Minister directly provided with data of a personal nature
personnel by the Grand-Ducal Police or the Public Ministry when a suspension of a Union certificate “is
necessary for reasons of security or public order”?

Therefore, it would be appropriate to specify the methods of such monitoring, and more particularly which categories of
personal data would be communicated, if necessary, to the Minister and by which administrations.

C. On access to the files referred to in Article 19 paragraph (5) of the draft law

Article 19 paragraph (5) of the draft law provides that the Minister may access:

• national register of legal persons created by the law of March 30, 1979 organizing digital identification

natural and legal persons (hereinafter the “national register of legal persons”);

• national register of natural persons created by the law of 19 June 2013 relating to the identification of persons

physical (hereinafter the “RNPP”);

• file operated by the minister responsible for registration and domains; And

215

ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPD • register of companies carrying out an activity referred to in the amended law of 2 September 2011 regulating
access to the professions of craftsman, trader, industrialist as well as certain liberal professions (hereinafter
the “register of companies”).

It should be noted that the provisions of this paragraph do not transpose an article of the directive and have been
added by the authors of the draft law in order “to make the provisions of the law operational”. There
CNPD welcomes when the latter specify for each of the aforementioned files what are the categories
of data that may be transmitted to the Minister and for what purposes. However, as explained below,
some clarifications should be made.

In addition, it should be recalled that access to said files should not allow the Minister to obtain in any way
indirect personal data from other files of state authorities for which he has not
access under draft legislation under notice. If so, such communication of data between
ministries or administrations should be specified in the draft bill under opinion.

1. On the purposes

As a preliminary remark, the CNPD assumes that a clerical error has crept in at the beginning of Article 19
paragraph (5) of the draft bill in that it refers to paragraph (1) and not to paragraph (2) of
section 19.

The authors of the draft bill specify that the files can be consulted for “the implementation, the
control of the application and evaluation of this law” and the “exchange of information between the authorities having
access to the database referred to in Article 20 and the European Commission”. However, the purposes are not
written with sufficient precision so that the CNPD is unable to assess whether the data
transmitted are, in accordance with the principle of minimization of data enshrined in Article 5 paragraph (1)
letter c) of the GDPR, which are actually necessary for the fulfillment of said purposes.

The National Commission wonders whether this access would not instead allow the Minister to verify the authenticity
supporting documents provided on the basis of articles 8 and following of the draft law176. Access to these files
is it, moreover, necessary in order to prevent fraud or other illegal practices as referred to in Article 23
of the draft law?

Clarifications on this subject should be provided.

2. On the categories of personal data

176 See the developments above in point I. A. 3. of this opinion.

216

52020 ANNUAL REPORT CNPDANNEXES First of all, it should be noted that the Minister is provided with personal data as well as
as data relating to legal persons. In this regard, it should be noted that recital 14 of the
GDPR states that the GDPR “(…) does not cover the processing of personal data relating to
legal persons, and in particular companies with legal personality, including the name,
legal form and contact details of the legal person”.However, information relating to persons
legal entities may, where appropriate, relate to natural persons (for example: if they include the name of the
manager of the company) and must therefore be considered as personal data.

Thus, the GDPR will only apply to personal data that would be communicated to the Minister.

a) On access to the national register of legal persons:

Article 19, paragraph (5) letter a) provides that the Minister may access data from the national register
legal persons created by the amended law of March 30, 1979 organizing the digital identification of
natural and legal persons in order to obtain the identification information of river transport companies,
including in particular the “national identification number” and the “address”.

According to the understanding of the CNPD, the national register of legal persons does not in principle contain
personal data so that the GDPR does not apply. However, in order to avoid any
confusion, it would be desirable for the authors of the bill to use the terms as they are used by law
cited above of March 30, 1979. If the National Commission is not mistaken, the correct terminology is “number
identity” and “head office”.

b) On access to the RNPP:

Pursuant to Article 19 paragraph (5) letter b), the Minister has access to the national register of natural persons
“in order to obtain the identifying information of the deck crew member (…)” such as names and
first name, national identification number, nationality and country of residence.

It should be recalled that access to the RNPP by the Minister should also be carried out in accordance with the procedure
provided for by article 10 of the amended law of 19 June 2013 relating to the identification of natural persons as well as
Articles 5 to 7 of the Grand-Ducal Regulation of 28 November 2013 setting the terms of application of the aforementioned law.

c) On access to the file providing information on boats registered in Luxembourg

The draft bill provides that the minister can access data from the file used by the minister having
registration and domains in its attributions providing information on boats registered in Luxembourg.

217

ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPD More specifically, the Minister can access the "data necessary to identify the buildings, owner,
holder, address, deadlines”.

The National Commission considers that the draft law should refer to the law establishing the file in
question. In the absence of this indication, it is not able to decide on access to said file.

d) On access to the business register

The CNPD understands that this is the register referred to in Article 32 paragraph (1) of the amended law of 2 September
2011 regulating access to the professions of craftsman, merchant, industrialist as well as certain professions
Liberals and that the minister will only be granted limited access to the registry. The National Commission
therefore welcomes the fact that the authors of the draft law only provide for limited access to this register.

However, with regard to information relating to the financial capacity of the transport company
fluvial, which categories of personal data would, if necessary, be transmitted to the Minister?
Clarifications on this subject deserve to be provided by the authors of the draft law. Moreover, and in order
To avoid confusion, it should also be clarified what is covered by the term “addresses”. Is it the
registered office of the river transport company or the address of its legal representatives?

D. On the registers kept by the Minister

Article 20 of the draft law transposes Article 25 of the directive and aims in particular to provide for the
creation of registers to be kept by the Minister. Indeed, paragraph (1) of the said article provides that the Minister
keep records “for Union qualification certificates, service records and logbooks issued under
its authority (…) and, where applicable, for documents recognized under Article 7, paragraph 2, which have been
issued, renewed, suspended or withdrawn, which have been declared lost, stolen or destroyed, or which have expired (…)”.

The CNPD understands from reading the aforementioned paragraph that the Minister keeps a register for certificates of
Union qualification, another register for service records and a third register for logbooks.
The paragraph further lists the categories of data that would be collected by the Minister for each of the
registers.

However, the National Commission wonders whether these registers will not constitute one and the same processing
of data relating to this register, while the authors of the draft law mention in their comments
“the keeping of the national register” and refer in the body of the text on several occasions to the “register referred to in Article
20, paragraph 1”177. Thus, it is recommended to clarify in the draft law whether it is a single register
or, on the other hand, of several separate data processing operations or registers kept by the Minister.

177 See in particular Articles 19, paragraph (6) and 20, paragraph (3) of the draft law.

218

52020 CNPD ANNUAL REPORTANNEXES 1. Categories of personal data

Article 20 paragraph (1) of the draft law lists the personal data that the registers
held by the Minister contain. From this point of view, this article, which is a faithful transposition of article 25
paragraph (1) of the directive, does not raise any comments from the CNPD.

It should however be noted that in addition to the data listed in paragraph (1) of the aforementioned Article 20, the CNPD
understands that data from the files listed in paragraph (5) of Article 19 will be kept in
these registers. Indeed, article 19 paragraph (6) provides that “the consultation and reception of the data of these
databases can be done automatically in the register referred to in Article 20, paragraph 1”.

It should also be noted that the Minister will also be required to collect and process data of a
personnel under the procedure for issuing Union qualification certificates and authorizations
covered by Articles 8 and 9. The CNPD understands that only the data appearing on the certificates
qualification of the Union will be contained in the registers and not the supporting documents provided by the
applicants for certificates in support of their application.

In any case, if the registers kept by the Minister were to contain other data than those listed
in Article 20 paragraph (1), then this should be clearly reflected in the draft law.

2. On the security measures governing access to the registers

In accordance with Article 5 paragraph (1) letter f) of the GDPR, personal data must be
“processed in a way that ensures appropriate security of personal data, including the protection
against unauthorized or unlawful processing and against accidental loss, destruction or damage,
using appropriate technical or organizational measures (integrity and confidentiality)”.

In addition, Article 32 of the GDPR provides that “the controller and the processor shall implement the
appropriate technical and organizational measures to guarantee a level of security appropriate to the risk”.
Such measures must be implemented in order to avoid in particular unauthorized access to data or
data leaks.

Thus, the National Commission is pleased that the authors of the preliminary draft law have specified the procedures for access
to the registers kept by the Minister, even though they have provided for traceability of access to the said registers.

Furthermore, the last sentence of Article 19 paragraph (4) of the draft law provides that “[o]nly are
authorized to have access to the data the persons who need it in the exercise of their function and their

219

ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPD professional tasks”. According to the understanding of the CNPD, this provision applies to all
processing provided for by the draft law, therefore also access to the registers referred to in Article 20 paragraph
(1).

3. On the retention period of personal data

According to Article 5 paragraph (1) letter e) of the GDPR, personal data must not be stored
longer than necessary to achieve the purposes for which they are collected and processed.

Paragraph 1 of paragraph (3) of Article 20 of the draft law faithfully transposes Article 25
paragraph (3) of the directive. Paragraph 2 of paragraph (3) of Article 20 of the draft law is an addition by
relation to the directive. This paragraph gives details as to the date of elimination of certain categories of
data.

Thus, it is provided that "the data relating to a boat are deleted from the register referred to in paragraph 1 when
this building is dismantled" and that "the data relating to a service book or a certificate of qualification are
deleted from the register referred to in paragraph 1 when the data subject is deceased”.

The CNPD wonders whether such a retention period for personal data, in particular until
death of the data subject, is proportionate and necessary. Indeed, is it necessary to keep until
death of a person the data concerning him and appearing on the qualification certificate if he does not have
no longer have a qualification certificate or no longer meets the conditions required by the draft law?

In the absence of details from the authors of the draft bill as to the criteria that would justify such a duration,
the National Commission is not in a position to assess whether, in this case, the principle of retention period
data limitation is observed regarding the collection of this data.

In addition, it should be clarified that a retention period should also be determined for each
category of personal data that would be collected by the Minister within the framework of the preliminary draft
law under notice. For example, the data collected during the procedure for issuing certificates of
qualification of the Union will have to be deleted or anonymized as soon as their conservation is no longer necessary
for the achievement of the purposes for which they are collected and processed.

Therefore, even if the retention period does not necessarily have to be defined in the draft law, it does
it should at least specify the criteria that would be taken into account in order to determine the duration of
proportionate retention for each category of personal data that would be collected by the
minister.

220

5ANNUAL REPORT2020 CNPDANNEXES II. On the exchange of personal data

Article 23 paragraph (2) provides in particular that “[t]he Minister exchanges relevant information with
the competent authorities of the other Member States concerning the certification of persons involved in
operation of a vessel, including information relating to the suspension and withdrawal of certificates”.

It should be recalled that personal data can, in principle, circulate freely from the
Grand Duchy of Luxembourg within the European Economic Area, as long as the general principles of the GDPR
are respected. In particular, care must be taken to respect the principle of purpose limitation, under which
data must not be processed for purposes "incompatible" with the original purposes.

In addition and insofar as the terms of these data exchanges are provided for by the delegated regulation
(EU) 2020/473 of the Commission of 20 January 2020 with regard to the standards applicable to databases
data relating to Union qualification certificates, service records and logbooks, this article
does not raise any observations from the CNPD.

Thus decided in Belvaux on December 17, 2020.

2020 ANNUAL REPORT CNPD Opinion relating to bill no. 7738 amending: 1° the amended law of 17 July 2020 on
introduction of a series of measures to combat the Covid-19 pandemic; 2° the law of 19
December 2020, the purpose of which is to set up a temporary contribution from the State
the uncovered costs of certain companies; 3° the amended law of 4 July 2008 on
the youth.

(Deliberation n°30/2020 of December 22, 2020)

In accordance with Article 57, paragraph 1, letter (c) of Regulation No. 2016/679 of April 27, 2016 relating to
the protection of individuals with regard to the processing of personal data and the freedom
movement of such data, and repealing Directive 95/46/EC (general data protection regulation)
(hereinafter “the GDPR”), to which article 7 of the law of 1 August 2018 on the organization of the Commission refers
national data protection system and the general data protection regime, the Commission
Commission for Data Protection (hereinafter referred to as “the National Commission” or “the CNPD”)
“advises, in accordance with the law of the Member State, the national parliament, the government and other institutions
and organizations regarding legislative and administrative measures relating to the protection of the rights and freedoms of
natural persons with regard to the processing”.
 
On December 21, 2020, the Minister of Health seized the National Commission of a request
of opinion on draft law no. 7738 amending: 1° the amended law of 17 July 2020 introducing a series
measures to combat the Covid-19 pandemic; 2° the law of 19 December 2020 having as its object the implementation
place of a temporary State contribution to the uncovered costs of certain companies; 3° the amended law of
July 4, 2008 on youth (hereinafter “Bill No. 7738”).
 
The purpose of this bill is to ensure that the national health system can continue to function
adequately in the interest of all patients and that the number of SARS-CoV-2 virus infections
can decrease significantly. It is therefore proposed to maintain certain measures to combat
Covid-19 pandemic already in place, to strengthen others and to adopt new ones that will apply during
the period from December 26, 2020 to January 10, 2021.
 
The CNPD would like to point out that given the urgency of the bill under opinion, it is not possible for it to analyze
in depth the proposed amendments and that its opinion was drafted and adopted solely on the basis of the
information available to it to date. The opinion is given subject to possible future considerations.
 
1. Ad article 9 of bill n°7738

52020 ANNUAL REPORT CNPDANNEXES Article 9 of bill no. 7738 aims to modify article 5 paragraph (3) of the amended law of July 17, 2020
introducing a series of measures to combat the Covid-19 pandemic, specifying that the transmission

whose result of a diagnostic test for infection with the SARS-CoV-2 virus was negative is intended not only

are obliged to transmit to the director of health the surname, first names, sex, identification number or date of

than the result of this test. These data are anonymized by the director of health or his delegate after a

time, this immune signature of infection is only of value for a limited time. For this reason, the
the most appropriate shelf life appears to be two years. »
 
In this regard, the National Commission refers to point 2.3 of this opinion, subject to considerations

2. Ad article 10 of bill n°7738: the collection of personal data within the framework of the

vaccination program

 

2.1. Preliminary remarks

 
With regard to the legal basis on which the processing carried out by the director of health with a view to
to monitor the evolution of the spread of the SARS-CoV-2 virus, more specifically the public interest under Article
6 paragraph (1) letter e) as well as Article 9 paragraph 2) letter i) of the GDPR, the CNPD would like to refer to its
related comments in its initial opinion on draft law no. 7606 introducing a series of
measures concerning natural persons in the context of the fight against the SARS-CoV-2 virus (COVID-19).178
 
Nevertheless, it would like to point out that Article 6 paragraph (3) of the GDPR provides for a specific constraint related to
the lawfulness of data processing necessary for compliance with a legal obligation or for the performance of a mission
in the public interest or in the exercise of official authority vested in the controller. In
these two scenarios, the basis and purposes of the data processing must be specifically defined
either by the law of the European Union, or by the law of the Member State to which the controller is
submitted.

178 Deliberation n°13/2020 of June 8, 2020.

223

ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPD In addition, recital (45) of the GDPR specifies that it should "[...] belong to Union law or the law of a State
member to determine the purpose of the processing. [...]". Recital 41 of the GDPR further specifies that this measure
legislation should be clear and precise and its application should be predictable for litigants, in accordance
the case law of the Court of Justice of the European Union and the European Court of Human Rights.
 
Thus, while a national or supranational normative text should not specifically prescribe the treatment of
data, “the purpose of the processing must however be precise, insofar as the text leading the administration
to process data must allow citizens to deduce the nature of the data and the purposes for which
these are used”179.
 

2.2. As for the purpose pursued by the collection of data within the framework of the vaccination program

  
The new Article 10 paragraph (1) of the amended law of July 17, 2020 introducing a series of
measures to combat the Covid-19 pandemic provides that the information system set up by the director
of health with a view to monitoring the evolution of the spread of the SARS-CoV-2 virus now pursues a purpose
additional: “continuously monitor and evaluate the efficacy and safety of vaccines against Covid-19. »
 
Pursuant to article 10 point 2° of bill n°7738, the new point 3 of article 10 paragraph (2) of the
amended law of July 17, 2020 introducing a series of measures to combat the Covid-19 pandemic
thus provides that the information system put in place by the Director of Health covers, in addition to the data
initially contained therein,180 on a whole series of personal data collected within the framework of the
vaccination program concerning the vaccinator, on the one hand, that is to say the doctor in charge of
or confirm the indication for vaccination and prescribe the vaccine in order to set up this system of
surveillance,181 as well as the person to be vaccinated, on the other hand.
 
Nevertheless, on the basis of the information currently available to it, the National Commission has difficulty
to enter the precise purpose of the collection and recording in the information system of all these data at
personal character concerning the vaccinator and the person to be vaccinated. Article 5 paragraph (1) letter (b) of the
GDPR indeed requires that personal data must be “collected for specified purposes,
explicit and legitimate, and not further processed in a manner incompatible with those purposes”.
 
The explanatory memorandum is quite vague in this respect, stating only that it is necessary "to ensure a follow-up
of the quality and effects of the various vaccines, in particular in order to strengthen the confidence of the population
towards vaccination" and that this "monitoring also includes the usual activities of measuring vaccination coverage,
measuring the effectiveness of vaccines and pharmacovigilance. […] Indeed, it is necessary to verify the usefulness
of vaccination in order to identify its benefits. Finally, this monitoring will make it possible to adjust the vaccination strategy
and the deployment plan. »
 

179 M. Besch, “Personal data processing in the public sector”, Norms and legislation in Luxembourg public law, Luxembourg, Promoculture

Larcier, 2019, p.470, n°619

180 These are data collected under article 5 of the amended law of 17 July 2020 introducing a series of measures to combat the pandemic
Covid-19 and data collected pursuant to articles 3 to 5 of the law of 1 August 2018 on the compulsory declaration of certain diseases within the framework of the
protection of public health.

181 As specified in the commentary to article 10 of the bill under opinion.

5ANNUAL REPORT2020 CNPDANNEXES Considering the declarations of the government that vaccination against the coronavirus will not be compulsory,
the CNPD therefore wonders what exactly consists of the purpose pursued by the processing of data to
personal character of the vaccinator and of the person to be vaccinated. Does the purpose as indicated fit or is it
limited in a context of pharmacovigilance182 or requirements of the law of July 4, 2000 relating to liability
of the State in terms of vaccinations establishing the responsibility of the State in certain cases of sequelae due to
a legal, regulatory or state-recommended vaccination?
 
Given the urgency and lack of precision in the commentary on the articles, the CNPD was not able to
research and analyze the legal texts on the subject. It therefore also wonders whether similar data
are collected and recorded in state files as part of vaccinations against other diseases
or pathologies, in particular those listed in the amended Grand-Ducal regulation of 18 October 2001 determining
the list of recommended vaccinations.
 
Finally, the National Commission wonders in this context whether the authors of the bill are not,
furthermore, implicit reference to further processing of data collected for research purposes
scientist ?
 
In order to meet the forecasting and predictability requirements that a legal text must meet, by reference
European case law, and for the sake of transparency and legal certainty, the CNPD recommends
the authors of the bill to specify in the body of the text in more detail what are the purposes
explicit and determined actually pursued by the collection of this large amount of personal data
concerning the vaccinator and the person to be vaccinated.
 

2.3. Regarding the retention period of data collected as part of the vaccination program

 
Article 5 paragraph (1) letter (e) of the GDPR provides that personal data must be “kept
in a form allowing the identification of the persons concerned for a period not exceeding that
necessary in relation to the purposes for which they are processed”. It is also clear from recital (45) of the
GDPR only when the processing is necessary for the performance of a task in the public interest or relating to the exercise
of the official authority vested in the controller, it should belong to Union law or to the
right of a Member State to establish, inter alia, the retention period of the data.
 
Thus, the retention period must be determined according to the purpose that led to the collection of the data.
in question. Once this objective has been achieved, this data should be deleted or anonymised (in order in particular to
produce statistics).
 
Point 4° of article 10 of bill no. 7738 provides in this context that personal data
vaccinators are anonymized no later than two years after their collection, while

182 As provided for by article 4 paragraph (5) of the amended law of 21 November 1980 on the organization of the Department of Health and the amended law of 16 August

1968 creating a center for speech therapy and audiometric and speech therapy services.

225

ANNUAL REPORT 2020 CNPD the personal data of persons to be vaccinated are anonymized at the latest after a period
twenty years after their collection.
 
The explanatory memorandum specifies in this context that "the data of vaccinated persons will be kept
for twenty years from the date of collection, a minimum period to ensure optimal monitoring of the
vaccination, with a view to protecting vaccinated persons over the long term. In fact, one should not
forget that we are in the presence of a new vaccine which is deployed and whose effects will manifest themselves
in real time. It is therefore in the very interest of the vaccinated person that the data concerning them are
kept for as long as possible in order to check whether an effect appearing after several years can, the
if necessary, be linked to the vaccine or not. On the other hand, with regard to data from vaccinators,
these will be kept for two years. This is explained by their necessity in order to ensure the follow-up of
adverse effects in the short and medium term only. This collection having another objective, the principle of
proportionality requires shorter retention periods. »
 
With regard to the GDPR, it is necessary and essential to define a data retention period within the
health department information system that is proportionate to the purpose pursued. Therefore,
it is necessary to define objective criteria making it possible to justify an adequate retention period.
 
However, the National Commission does not have the necessary scientific and epidemiological expertise,
in order to assess whether the retention of data of vaccinated persons for twenty years from the date of
collection is really necessary and proportionate, in view of the vague and imprecise purpose as indicated in
the text of the bill.
 
The CNPD takes this opportunity to underline the fundamental importance of people's right to information
concerned. Pursuant to Articles 13 and 14 of the GDPR, the controller is indeed obliged to provide
to the persons concerned certain information when personal data is collected
directly from them or indirectly through a third party. Precise and appropriate information must therefore be
provided to the persons concerned in a particular health context, both to the vaccinators and especially to the
persons to be vaccinated, in particular of any subsequent use of their data, bearing in mind that the deadline for
Planned data retention is twenty years.
 
Finally, in the absence of precision in the text of bill n°7738, the CNPD wonders what is the origin of the
personal data of vaccinators and vaccinated persons, i.e. who is obliged to collect,
as well as to transmit and record the data in question in the information system of the management of
health. Are they the same vaccinators who are in charge of the vaccination program in the centers?
vaccinations?183 Or is it also planned in the short and medium term that referring doctors can
directly vaccinate their patients and in this case, they would be obliged to transmit the data in question to

183 See in this context the vaccination strategy against COVID-19 in Luxembourg:
 

https://sante.public.lu/fr/actualites/2020/12/communique-strategie-vaccination/index.html.

226

52020 ANNUAL REPORT CNPDANNEXES the health department? In addition, there is reason to wonder what happens to the data thus collected
by the vaccinators and then forwarded to the health department. Does the data collected by the
vaccinators are they immediately destroyed upon transmission or do they remain recorded in files
vaccinators for a certain period of time?
 
These points deserve to be clarified and specified in the text of the bill.
